Anion-exchange membrane water electrolyzers and fuel cells†

Yaxiong Yang‡ a, Peng Li‡ *bg, Xiaobo Zheng c, Wenping Sun de, Shi Xue Dou fg, Tianyi Ma *b and Hongge Pan *ad
aInstitute of Science and Technology for New Energy, Xi’an Technological University, Xi’an, 710021, P. R. China
bSchool of Science, RMIT University, Melbourne, VIC, 3000, Australia. E-mail: peng.li@rmit.edu.au; tianyi.ma@rmit.edu.au
cDepartment of Chemistry, Tsinghua University, Beijing 100084, China
dSchool of Materials Science and Engineering, Zhejiang University, Hangzhou 310058, P. R. China. E-mail: honggepan@zju.edu.cn
eState Key Laboratory of Clean Energy Utilization, Zhejiang University, Hangzhou 310027, P. R. China
fInstitute of Energy Material Science, University of Shanghai for Science and Technology, Shanghai 200093, China
gInstitute for Superconducting & Electronic Materials, Australian Institute for Innovative Materials, University of Wollongong, Wollongong, NSW 2522, Australia

Received 5th July 2022

First published on 8th November 2022

Abstract

Anion-exchange membrane (AEM) water electrolyzers (AEMWEs) and fuel cells (AEMFCs) are technologies that, respectively, achieve transformation and utilization of renewable resources in the form of green hydrogen (H2) energy. The significantly reduced cost of their key components (membranes, electrocatalysts, bipolar plates, etc.), quick reaction kinetics, and fewer corrosion problems endow AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells with overwhelming superiority over their conventional counterparts (e.g., proton-exchange membrane water electrolyzer/fuel cells and alkaline water electrolyzer/fuel cells). Limitations in our fundamental understanding of AEM devices, however, specifically in key components, working management, and operation monitoring, restrict the improvement of cell performance, and they further impede the deployment of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Therefore, a panoramic view to outline the fundamentals, technological progress, and future perspectives on AEMWEs and AEMFCs is presented. The objective of this review is to (1) present a timely overview of the market development status of green hydrogen technology that is closely associated with AEMWEs (hydrogen production) and AEMFCs (hydrogen utilization); (2) provide an in-depth and comprehensive analysis of AEMWEs and AEMFCs from the viewpoint of all key components (e.g., membranes, ionomers, catalysts, gas diffusion layers, bipolar plates, and membrane electrode assembly (MEA)); (3) summarize the state-of-the-art technologies for working management of AEMWEs and AEMFCs, including electrolyte engineering (electrolyte selection and feeding), water management, gas and heat management, etc.; (4) outline the advances in monitoring the operations of AEMWEs and AEMFCs, which include microscopic and spectroscopic techniques and beyond; and (5) present key aspects that need to be further studied from the perspective of science and engineering to accelerate the deployment of AEMWEs and AEMFCs.

Peng Li

Peng Li received his PhD degree under the supervision of Distinguished Prof. Shi Xue Dou from the Institute for Superconducting & Electronic Materials at the University of Wollongong in 2021. Currently, he is a postdoctoral researcher for a joint project “Low-cost electrolysis of seawater and CO2 for co-production of renewable fuel and hypochlorite” between RMIT and CSIRO Energy under the mentorship of ARC Future Fellow Prof. Tianyi Ma. His current research focuses on the rational development of efficient materials, and the fundamental understanding of the electrochemical processes for electrocatalysis and fuel cells.

Wenping Sun

Wenping Sun is a professor at School of Materials Science and Engineering, Zhejiang University. He received his B.S. degree in 2008 and PhD degree in 2013 in Materials Science from the University of Science and Technology of China (USTC). He is a recipient of the ARC DECRA (2016) and CAS Outstanding Doctoral Dissertation Award (2015). His research expertise includes electrocatalysis, fuel cells, and batteries, especially the design of novel materials and structures, and fundamental understanding of the related electrochemical processes. He has authored/co-authored over 130 peer-reviewed papers, which have attracted a total citation of >12000 with an h-index of 59 (Google Scholar). He serves as an Associate Editor for Frontiers in Chemistry and Materials for Renewable and Sustainable Energy.

He received his PhD at Dalhousie University, Canada in 1984, DSc at the University of New South Wales in 1998 and was elected as a Fellow of the Australian Academy of Technological Science and Engineering in 1994. He has supervised and co-supervised over 100 PhD students, and more than 60 postdoctoral and visiting fellows. He is a program leader for Auto CRC 2020 on electrification program and the on-going ARENA 2016–2021 on smart sodium storage system program.

Tianyi Ma is a Professor and the Leader of Carbon Neutrality Group at RMIT University. He received his PhD in physical chemistry in 2013 from Nankai University, China. He was awarded an ARC DECRA in 2015. He has published more than 230 papers which attracted more than 22000 citations with an h-index of 69. He is a world-leading scientist in renewable energy field, with pioneering work done in the areas of functional photocatalytic, electrocatalytic, thermocatalytic, and piezocatalytic materials for renewable solar, mechanical, and thermal energy harvesting storage and utilization, carbon capture, utilization and storage (CCUS), as well as next-generation high-performance battery and supercapacitor energy storage devices. He is an ARC Future Fellow, Fellow of Royal Society of Chemistry, and Clarivate's Global Highly Cited Researcher.

1. Introduction

1.1. Global carbon neutrality and H2 energy

Worldwide industrial activities have created diverse products and services for humans but have also generated a huge amount of CO2 emissions due to the consumption of fossil fuels.1 Intensive CO2 emission sources are everywhere, ranging from agriculture and animal husbandry to transportation and various industrial sectors (e.g., refineries, the petrochemical industry, the iron and steel industry, cement and fertilizer production, etc.), causing rapid growth of our atmospheric CO2 concentration year by year.2,3 It has been reported that the CO2 level in the atmosphere is approaching 420 ppm, which is nearly 50% higher than the level before the Industrial Revolution.4 What is worse, global warming, floods, drought, and other extreme weather problems induced by greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions are huge threats to all humans. Towards this end, stopping CO2 emissions and circumventing climate change are essential ways to build a sustainable society in pursuit of harmony between humanity and nature. In 2016, 196 parties negotiated the Paris Agreement to limit global warming to well below 2 °C by 2050 via undertaking specific emission targets.5 After that, announcements of CO2 emission reduction have been pledged by many countries (e.g., Australia, Germany, Canada, China, the United States, Japan, etc.). Australia has committed itself to a strong and responsible target to reduce its GHG emissions to 26–28% below the 2005 level by 2030 and deliver net zero emissions by 2050.6 Germany's newly amended national climate law proposed a 65% emission reduction by 2030, 85–90% by 2040, and net zero emissions by 2045.7 China has promised to peak its CO2 emissions before 2030 and hit carbon neutrality by 2060.8 President Biden announced that for the United States a 50–52% of GHG emissions based on 2005 levels will be cut by 2030.9 The need to eliminate CO2 emissions to achieve net zero carbon emissions has increasingly been admitted by many countries, which is also a great necessity for attaining the goals of the Paris Agreement as well as sustainable development. It has to be noted that global CO2 emissions associated with energy combustion and industrial processes rebounded in 2021 to reach their highest ever annual level of 36.3 gigatons (Gts) according to the International Energy Agency (IEA).10,11 This implies that it is imperative to find new energy sources that are clean and abundant to satisfy our human demands and attain global carbon neutrality, which is challenging but promising.

Solar energy, wind energy, hydro power, etc. are excellent renewable resources that are adequate to meet our increasing energy demands.12–14 Considering the fluctuating and intermittent nature of these renewable sources of energy, one efficient approach is to store the energy or convert it into chemical fuels.14–16 Hydrogen (H2) is an ideal chemical for such storage with an ultrahigh energy density (120–140 MJ kg−1), the most abundant element resources, the greatest sustainability, and zero emissions.17–19 Furthermore, the use of hydrogen could reduce our dependence on fossil fuels and help the decarbonization of various industries and energy sectors, both of which are imperative pathways to attain global carbon neutrality.18,20 As reported by the International Energy Agency (IEA), hydrogen energy has long been considered an ideal energy alternative to fossil fuels and will experience explosive development in transportation, heavy industry, power generation, etc. To be specific, the huge amount of carbon emission removal (∼1.9 Gts) by 2050 will primarily rely on the initiation of hydrogen energy and the deployment of the carbon capture, utilization, and storage (CCUS) technology.3,21 Currently, hydrogen is mainly produced by the steam reforming reaction, coal gasification, water electrolysis, etc.22 The former two methods unavoidably generate great amounts of GHG emissions, while water electrolysis via renewable resources has almost zero emissions, which is considered green hydrogen technology.23–26 The higher water splitting energy efficiency and the increasing deployment of renewables (e.g., solar photovoltaic and wind energy) in recent years are further driving the implementation of water electrolyzers with MW-scale capacity. By virtue of green H2 production technology, hydrogen would substitute for the current fossil fuels in industry, transportation, the electricity sector, etc. Moreover, H2 fuel cells could be widely mounted in light/medium/heavy-duty vehicles and power any portable devices that use batteries. A green hydrogen cycle associated with H2 production and H2 utilization is expected to be established, as shown in Fig. 1. Water electrolyzers (WEs) and H2–O2 fuel cells (FCs) are the most critical devices to achieve H2 production from renewables and H2 utilization in the form of electricity, respectively. Therefore, developing low-cost, high-efficiency, and durable WEs and FCs is of paramount importance in the pursuit of green hydrogen technology as well as attaining carbon neutrality, which is also challenging but promising.


Fig. 1 A schematic illustration of the green hydrogen (H2) cycle as a clean energy solution mediated by anion-exchange water electrolyzer and fuel cell technologies. GDL gas diffusion layer; CT: computed tomography.

1.2. AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

Water electrolysis can be dated back to the 18th century when the electrolysis phenomenon was discovered and water electrolysis experiments were conducted.27 After the development of electrode catalysts and the search for a suitable electrolyte, alkaline water electrolyzers (AWEs) have become a mature technology since 1920.28 AWEs convert water into hydrogen and oxygen with the input of electricity while alkaline fuel cells (AFCs) utilize the chemical energy of hydrogen and oxygen and generate water and electrical energy.29 Both AWEs and the AFCs feature mature stack components, low capital cost, and decent durability, which have enabled them to be major water electrolysis and fuel cell devices for a long period.26,30,31 So far, a large amount of H2 produced by electrolysis relies on traditional alkaline water electrolyzers, owing to their strong cost competitiveness. Meanwhile, their pollution-free nature and fast reaction kinetics have made alkaline fuel cells increasingly important to build a more sustainable society. The corrosive nature of the KOH electrolyte (20–30% KOH solution), however, and high sensitivity to CO2 usually cause severe carbonate deposition and reduce the overall cell performance.32,33 Furthermore, their relatively low current density, limited operating pressure, and inconvenient device are restricting their fast and large-scale deployment. Based on this, researchers later proposed polymer electrolyte membrane and solid oxide water electrolysis/fuel cell technology – an attempt to achieve breakthroughs in the performance of cells in such aspects as portability, efficiency, current density, etc. Solid oxide water electrolyzers (SOWEs) and fuel cells (SOFCs) are alternative technologies that operate at high temperatures (700–1000 °C) and feature many fascinating advantages such as highly accelerated reaction kinetics and almost 100% device efficiency.34,35 Their high-temperature working environment requires a significant amount of heating energy to be supplied, which greatly limits the practical conditions of SOWEs and SOFCs (coupled with a thermal source). On the other hand, the high temperature also intensifies cell component degradation and usually leads to unsatisfactory durability.36,37 The search for superior ceramic membranes with high ionic conductivity, to some degree, remains unresolved. The above-mentioned problems still keep the SOWEs and SOFCs in the investigation stage and make the capital cost of SOWEs very much more expensive than that of AWEs and polymer electrolyte membrane water electrolysis.33,38 Polymer electrolyte membranes can be classified into proton exchange membranes (PEMs) and anion exchange membranes (AEMs) according to the chemical structure that is fabricated to shuttle cations or anions. For instance, the use of PEM creates strongly acidic conditions inside the cells, while alkaline conditions are formed when using AEMs. Compared with the alkaline water electrolyzer/fuel cell, proton exchange membrane (PEM) technologies feature a higher H2 output rate, extended power density, and improved cell efficiency, so they have achieved scaled applications.14 It has to be noted that the acidic working conditions of PEM cells reduce the choice of catalysts to only the platinum group metals (PGMs), and many other components (membranes and bipolar plates) possess relatively high production costs which make PEM technology quite costly. Furthermore, the strongly acidic conditions also significantly reduce the lifespan of cell devices. In sharp contrast, the highly favorable oxygen reaction/evolution kinetics and improved power density in the alkaline system make the use of AEMs more desirable.39–41 At the same time, the electrode catalysts are no longer dependent on PGMs, thus significantly reducing the overall production cost compared with PEM technology.42,43 Moreover, the corrosion problem in alkaline media is significantly mitigated, which increases the catalyst durability as well as prolongs the lifespan of cell devices. Apart from the aforementioned aspects, a variety of fuels can be used in AEMFCs owing to their higher electro-oxidation kinetics.41 Objectively, these technologies (AWE/AFC, PEMWE/PEMFC, SOWE/SOFC, and AEMWE/AEMFC) have respective advantages/disadvantages owing to their component compositions (e.g., membranes and catalysts) and the membrane electrode assembly (MEA) fabrication approaches involved in them. Meanwhile, the progress of these technologies is also affected by upstream renewable electricity and downstream H2 transportation/utilization, where many factors, such as raw materials, the H2 output rate, delivery pressure, scalability, etc., will exert significant influences on their further development and deployment. It is foreseen that these technologies will play commanding roles in the field of clean hydrogen production and utilization but in different markets, and what we should do is to sweep away the fundamental and technological barriers to ensure that each of them could leverage their strength.

2. H2 for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

2.1. H2 production involving AEM water electrolyzers

The current technologies employed to produce H2 normally focus on several approaches, such as thermochemical, electrochemical, biochemical, photochemical, etc.45–47 The thermochemical avenue is based on converting fossil fuels (e.g., natural gas and coal) into hydrogen, including steam reforming, methane pyrolysis, and coal gasification.46 Biochemical or photochemical approaches are promising methods for transforming biomass waste or solar energy into hydrogen, but their very low efficiency and unaffordable process are still keeping them in the early investigation stage.48,49 The electrochemical method (known as water electrolysis or water-splitting) is a relatively mature technology and has been considered an ideal solution to build a more sustainable society.15,36 This is because water electrolysis is easily integrated with intermittent electricity from renewable resources. Furthermore, the electrochemical technology has nearly zero emissions in H2 production, and the only emissions in the whole process are in power generating facilities upstream. Therefore, H2 produced via water electrolysis powered by renewable electricity is considered green hydrogen (H2 made by the biochemical or photochemical methods is also regarded as green H2).50,51 Hydrogen production via the thermochemical pathway causes millions of tons of emitted CO2 due to the use of fossil fuels. It is estimated that by producing 1 ton of H2, the process will generate almost 10, 12, or 19 tons of CO2 by using natural gas, oil, or coal, respectively.52 Steam methane reforming (SMR) is an efficient and low-cost way to produce H2, but it also generates carbon emissions. Consequently, H2 produced by the combustion of fossil fuels, generating CO2 emissions, is called grey hydrogen (or brown/black hydrogen). At the same time, huge CO2 emissions are stimulating the deployment of carbon capture, utilization and storage (CCUS) technology, which aims to reduce up to 90% of carbon emissions by implementing additional systems used for CO2 recovery, transportation, and storage/utilization.53,54 H2 made from fossil fuels but coupled with the CCUS technology is considered blue hydrogen.50Fig. 2a gives us an overview of globally produced H2 (grey, blue, and green) from different sources and their respective features. According to the International Energy Agency (IEA), global H2 production reached ∼90 million tons (Mt) by 2020, where approximately 72 Mt of H2 were produced from dedicated plants while the remainder (18 Mt) was generated as a by-product.44 In the dedicated H2 amount (72 Mt), the primary source used to make hydrogen is natural gas (∼73%); coal comes with the second biggest feedstock (∼24%), and oil takes the least share (∼0.75%). Collectively, nearly 98% of globally dedicated H2 (∼70.5 Mt) was produced as grey hydrogen, and a significant consequence is that the CO2 emissions have reached over 830 Mt.52 Meanwhile, the blue hydrogen amount merely attained 0.7 Mt and accounted for around 0.9% of global production. In sharp contrast, H2 produced via water electrolysis (green hydrogen) only hit ∼0.03% (∼30 kilotons (kt)) of globally dedicated H2, indicating that the current green hydrogen amount is far from being able to satisfy our energy demands as a clean energy solution. H2 produced from fossil fuels currently represents the most economical option, while water electrolysis is rather a costly approach. The levelized cost of hydrogen (LCOH) production made using different sources is shown in Fig. 2b. For H2 produced from natural gas, the LCOH ranges from 0.5 to 1.7 US dollars (USD) per kg without the CCUS technology, while it increases to ∼1.0 to 2.0 USD per kg coupled with the CCUS technology in 2020. When it comes to H2 made via renewables, the state-of-the-art LCOH fluctuates from 3.0 to 8.2 USD per kg, which is around 2 to 4 times higher than that produced by fossil fuels. Moreover, the projected LCOH in 2030 and 2050 made by fossil fuels and renewables is also presented. The LCOH made via renewables is estimated to decrease to 1 to 3.1 USD per kg, implying very strong price competitiveness compared with H2 produced by fossil fuels with or without CCUS.44,52 The increased H2 production cost made by fossil fuels is in great part due to pricing CO2 emissions (e.g., through carbon prices).44,55 The decreased LCOH of using renewables prompts us to further discuss the possible factors related to water electrolysis, which is beneficial for the development of low-cost green hydrogen. Simply speaking, renewable electricity and the electrolyzer stack are the top two cost factors for green hydrogen production. Increasing the electrolyzer stack and photovoltaic modules are two efficient ways to reduce LCOH, which is why large-scale water electrolyzers and numerous PV systems are preferably adopted in the construction phase. Fig. 2c shows the planned global electrolyzer capacity installation for the next 10 years. In terms of the capital cost of AEM water electrolyzers, it is rather challenging to give very specific data due to the limited research and different comparison methods. The truth is that the major components of the capital cost of AEM water electrolyzers are almost identical with those of PEM water electrolyzers, which include electrolyzer stacks, balance of systems, electricity, installation, contingency factors, site preparation, operation, maintenance, etc.56,57 After a careful review of the literature on the cost analysis of these factors, the four decisive factors are listed in Fig. 2d. Due to the complex and expensive PV solar energy and wind energy upstream, the electricity is the dominant cost for green H2 production. Next to it is the balance of plant (BOP), which denotes subsystems to provide auxiliary functions for the electrolyzer. This includes the power supply system, the water circulation system, hydrogen processing and separation, cooling, etc. The electrolyzer-stack cost includes the balance of stack, bipolar plate, framework, porous transport layer, and catalyst-coated membrane. The stack cost analysis of the three different types of water electrolysis technologies (e.g., AWE, PEMWE, and AEMWE) is depicted in Table 1. One can see that the AWE still possesses higher cost competitiveness over the other two types of technologies, which is why we should keep studying to further reduce the stack cost of these technologies. The fourth part is the operation cost, which is highly dependent on the operation time. The other influential factors, including contingency, maintenance, etc., take up very little shares. One should note that the stack cost and BOP are highly correlated with the scale of the electrolyzer system. The BOP cost of a 5 MW-scale AEM water electrolyzer (∼$ 291) is approximately half the cost of a 1 MW-scale one (∼$ 542).58 The H2 production cost as a function of operating hours and investment is depicted in Fig. 2e and f. Given that the investment in an AEM water electrolyzer is estimated to be US$ 770 per kW, approximately 8760 hours of working time is required.38,59 The lower the electrolyzer investment cost, the higher the cost competitiveness of green H2 production than blue H2 will be for an identical working time, and this is true for the renewable electricity price. Therefore, deploying a large-scale water electrolyzer system, strengthening the renewable energy industry, and prolonging the electrolyzer operation time are the three dominant avenues to attain cost-competitive green hydrogen.
Fig. 2 (a) Global production amounts of grey-, blue-, and green-H2 in 2020. (b) Levelized cost of H2 production via five different technologies in 2020 and in the Net Zero Emission Scenario for 2030 and 2050. (c) Globally planned installed capacity of water electrolyzers from 2021 to 2030. Reproduced with permission from ref. 44. Copyright 2021 IEA. (d) Approximate capital cost analysis for the 1 MW-scale AEM water electrolyzer system (electricity > balance of plant (BOP) > stack cost > operation > others). (e and f) H2 production cost as functions of investment, electricity price, and operation hours. Reproduced with permission from ref. 38. Copyright 2021 IRENA.

2.2. H2 utilization involving AEM fuel cells

Fig. 3a gives a comparison of the value of trade in energy commodities in 2020 and 2050.1 As two of the most important sectors in the green H2 supply chain, the estimated market value for water electrolyzers is likely to reach US$ 50–60 billion, while fuel cells approach US$ 21–25 billion by the middle of the century.1 It has been ascertained that H2 energy will be more competitive than oil and gas with a further reduction of green H2 production costs. H2 also represents one of the most typical energy combustion types, together with bioenergy and electricity, by 2050, compared to oil, gas, and coal in 2020. Also, the global H2 demand is likely to increase from ∼90 million tons (Mt) in 2020 to more than 200 Mt in 2030, and further jump to roughly 530 Mt by 2050, based on the announced pledges of carbon neutrality.3,60 By 2050, green hydrogen produced via water electrolysis is assumed to account for 60% of the global amount (∼318 Mt), and blue hydrogen made from natural gas coupled with CCUS will take up almost 40%.3 This implies that the global electrolyzer capacity will increase to 3,600 GW by 2050, of which there is approximately 0.3 GW today, again manifesting the high necessity of developing green H2 production technology due to its huge demand potential.3 The estimated H2 demand can be broadly grouped into onsite production within industrial facilities and merchant H2, which is usually for national/international trade (Fig. 3b). As shown in Fig. 3c, the produced H2 will be mainly utilized in heavy industry, the transportation sector, and households in accordance with the degree of H2 maturity to decarbonization as well as utilization efficiency. Generally, around 50% of H2 demand will be directly used in the heavy industry and transport sectors. This includes ammonia production for fertilizers, oil refineries, and methanol production for use as vehicle fuel. In addition, 30% of H2 will be converted into H2-based fuels, including ammonia for shipping, synthetic kerosene for aviation, and synthetic methane for blended gas networks.61 The maturity of H2 as the solution to decarbonization follows an increasing trend from household usage to industrial applications, owing to their rather centralized features. Consequently, governments and countries are adopting a range of policies and actions to accelerate the deployment of H2 technology in industry, transportation, and households. Currently, fuel cell electrical vehicles (FCEVs) are undergoing the most obvious development, and a large number of countries have invested huge amounts in the supply chain of FCEVs, including refueling systems, storage, transportation, etc. Meanwhile, FCEVs offer many principal advantages compared to internal-combustion engine vehicles (ICEVs) and battery electrical vehicles (BEVs). First, the fuel cell system is environmentally friendly, since no carbon dioxide, nitrogen oxides, carbon monoxide, and/or other materials will be produced compared with the ICE system. Second, the energy efficiency of the fuel cell system is approximately 30% higher than for the ICE, which means that FCEVs with 1 kg of hydrogen could achieve around double the kilometers in comparison to ICEVs with 1 US gallon of gasoline. Third, the refueling time for a FCEV is minutes compared with at least several hours of battery recharging for BEVs, enabling highly improved travel efficiency. Fourth, the FCEVs can be more rapidly warmed up than BEVs in cold weather and hence can produce full power in a short period. Specifically, BEVs are suited to smaller and lighter vehicles traveling short distances, while FCEVs present strong advantages for heavier vehicles as well as those used for much longer traveling distances, including heavy-duty vehicles, trucks, buses, etc.61,62 The deployment of FCEVs has largely been restricted in view of the lack of widespread refueling infrastructure, the short working life of the fuel cell stack, low volumetric energy density, and relatively high cost. To fill these gaps, governments across the world are initiating refueling infrastructure, purchase incentives, and tax deductions to boost the fast progress of FCEVs, while the scientific community is dedicated to further improving the overall performance of fuel cell stacks. Fig. 3d shows that the global number of hydrogen refueling stations (HRS) has reached 540, which are located in Korea (9%), the United States (12%), China (16%), Japan (25%), Germany (17%), and the rest of the world (RoW) (21%). Meanwhile, the number of FCEVs has reached 34,800 with most of them being distributed in Korea (29%), the United States (27%), China (24%), Japan (12%), and Germany (3%).63 Of the almost 35000 FCEVs, around 75% are passenger light-duty vehicles (PLDVs), and they are mainly deployed in Korea, the United States, and Japan. Buses and trucks take up 15% and 10%, respectively, of the total amount of FCEVs, which are all nearly exclusively in China. Fig. 3e gives the announced automotive fuel cell production capacity from 2020 to 2030. Global fuel cell manufacturing capacity has apparently reached 200,000 systems by 2021 and will grow rapidly to reach a capacity of more than 1.2 million by 2030. It can be ascertained that the great momentum in the H2 supply chain initiated by governments and parties will accelerate the fast progress and practical utilization of green H2.

(c) Maturity of H2 as a clean energy solution to decarbonize household/transportation/industry applications. (d) The global number of fuel cell electric vehicles and refueling stations by 2020. 3. Overview of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

3.1. Fundamental principles of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

The AEM water electrolysis and AEM H2–O2 fuel cell configurations are illustrated in Fig. 4a and b. Renewable energy can be stored or converted by water electrolysis, and then be utilized through fuel cells in the form of electricity. The detailed electrode reaction equations (eqn (3.1), (3.2), (3.3), (3.4), (3.5) and (3.6)) in AEMWEs and AEMFCs are presented here, with the standard potential, E0.
Fig. 4 Schematic illustration of the major components and working principles of anion-exchange membrane (AEM) (a) water electrolyzers and (b) fuel cells.

AEMWEs:

 
Anode: 4OH− → O2 + 2H2O + 4e−, E0 = 0.40 V(3.1)
 
Cathode: 2H2O + 2e− → H2 + 2OH−, E0 = −0.83 V(3.2)
 
Overall reaction: 2H2O → O2 + 2H2, E0 = −1.23 V(3.3)
In the AEMFCs:
 
Anode: H2 + 2OH− → 2H+ + 2e−, E0 = −0.83 V(3.4)
 
Cathode: O2 + 2H2O + 4e− → 4OH−, E0 = 0.40 V(3.5)
 
Overall reaction: O2 + 2H2 → 2H2O, E0 = 1.23 V(3.6)
Both the AEMWEs and the AEMFCs are composed of bipolar plates, gas-diffusion layers (GDLs), catalyst layers (CLs), and a polymer anion-exchange membrane (AEM). The membrane electrode assembly (MEA) is normally fabricated by pressing the GDL, CL, and AEM together at a high temperature and pressure. A single cell is formed by placing two bipolar plates to cover the MEAs. The polymer AEM substitutes for the liquid alkaline electrolyte and provides hydroxide-conducting channels. In the AEMWEs, water is circulated from the anode to the cathode side while the hydroxide ions proceed in the opposite direction. At the cathode, water is reduced by the addition of two electrons from the external power supply and generates H2 and hydroxides. The hydroxides, moving to the anode area, are oxidized to form water and oxygen by losing electrons. In contrast, the electrochemical process in the AEMFCs is the reverse of what happens in the AEMWEs. Oxygen molecules (O2) are reduced in the cathode area with the formation of hydroxides that are then transported across the AEM to the anode side. Meanwhile, the H2 molecules are oxidized at the anode and combined with the hydroxides to generate water molecules. The electrons generated from the redox reactions that occur in the two electrodes flow into the external circuit and power the needed devices. Theoretically, the thermodynamic water splitting voltage is 1.23 V at 298 K, but additional potential (overpotential) is required to overcome the sluggish electrode kinetics and the ohmic resistance of the electrolyte and other components. Correspondingly, the output voltage of the AEM H2–O2 fuel cell is much lower than 1.23 V due to the sluggish electrode kinetics as well as internal resistance. To achieve efficient production and utilization of green hydrogen energy, common requirements on the AEMFCs and AEMWEs are reduced cost, improved durability, and high efficiency.

3.2. Practical working conditions of water electrolyzers and fuel cells

3.2.1. AEMWEs. A simplified system overview of AEMWEs coupling from the upstream renewable electricity generation to H2 separation is shown in Fig. 5a. The renewable electricity from upstream directly powers the electrolyzer stack after the processes in the transformer and rectifier. The electrolyte is supplied to the anode via a gas–liquid separator system and passes through a circulation pump, heat exchanger, and ion exchanger to complete the cycle.36,64 The O2 that is formed comes with the circulated water and enters the gas–liquid separator system for further separation and use. On the cathode side, the generated H2 diffuses into the water and arrives to the gas–liquid separator system (cathode side). Subsequently, H2 can be distributed for further use after processing in the deoxidizer and dryer. A practical electrolyzer stack is shown in Fig. 5b, and the basic composition of a single cell is shown in Fig. 5c. The bipolar plate, porous transport layer (PTL), quaternary ammonium polysulfone (QAPS) membrane, catalyst coated membrane (CCM), O-ring, and end plate collectively constitute a single cell. The cell voltage–current (I–V) relationship as a function of three key factors (temperature, fed electrolyte, and membrane) (Fig. 5d–f) could best characterize the electrochemical behavior of a single cell. Fig. 5d gives the I–V curves at 25 and 80 °C. Specifically, the cell overpotential can be divided into the activation overpotential (Vact), ohmic overpotential (Vohm), and concentration overpotential (Vcon), corresponding to the energy barrier for the reaction kinetics, the sum of ohmic voltage losses, and the concentration voltage loss associated with the insufficient mass transfer at a high current density. Thermodynamically, the reversible voltage Urev (also the water splitting voltage in Section 3.1) reflects the minimum voltage required to dissociate water molecules under standard temperature and pressure (298.15 K and 1 atm), which is calculated according to eqn (3.7).
 
(3.7)
Here, the change in the Gibbs free energy ΔG0 is 237.21 kJ mol−1, n is the number of transferred electrons (2), and F is the Faraday constant (96485 C mol−1).31 The derived value of Urev is 1.23 V. Given that the Gibbs free energy varies with temperature, the empirical polynomial equation for the reversible voltage Urev as a function of temperature is shown in eqn (3.8).65
 
(3.8)
Consequently, the increase in temperature decreases the reversible voltage Urev, and Urev is calculated to be 1.167 V at 100 °C. This is why the reversible voltage for water-splitting at 80 °C (Fig. 5d) is lower than that at 25 °C. Meanwhile, high temperature favors ion transportation in the solution and electrode reaction kinetics; hence, it decreases both the ohmic and the activation overpotentials.66,67 Conversely, higher temperature accelerates the degradation of membranes and results in decreased overall cell performance. Therefore, based on the consideration of energy efficiency and cell durability, the practical working temperature for AEM water electrolyzers is designed to be 40 to 70 °C. The second factor affecting the performance relates to the feeding types and concentrations. Research on the feed electrolyte in an AEMWE is focused on diluted KOH (1 M), Na2CO3 (1 M), NaHCO3 solution (1 M), and pure water. As mentioned before, a good electrolyte could ensure sufficient shuttling of ions between the anode and the cathode while delivering the least ohmic loss and the best membrane compatibility. By comparing the cell polarization curves with different feeding electrolytes, it was found that the cell performance follows the order of KOH (1 M) > 1% K2CO3 > 1% K2CO3 + HKCO3 > HKCO3 > Deionized (DI) water (Fig. 5e). This is attributed to the decreased electrode and cell resistances stemming from the enhanced concentration of hydroxide ions when using 1 M KOH rather than the other electrolytes.68,69 Any further increase in the concentration of KOH electrolyte will cause CO2 contamination and easily degrade the membranes, let alone the higher cost. In contrast, the use of K2CO3 and HKCO3 solutions could favor enhanced stability for AEM and cell materials, although at the price of lower reaction kinetics and greatly reduced ionic conductivity.70–73 In the case of DI water, its availability and lowest cost might make it the most ideal feeding electrolyte, but the reduced hydroxide conductivity and the choice of ideal bipolar plates need to be further optimized. Fig. 5f shows how the anion exchange membrane affects the cell performance of water electrolyzers. Specifically, Sustanion® (Dioxide Materials, FL, USA), A-201 (Tokuyama, Japan), and AEMION™ (Ionomr, Canada) were employed to fabricate MEA for the evaluation of water electrolysis performance under identical testing conditions. A-201 delivered a relatively higher current density in the low current range (<∼0.8 A cm−2) while Sustanion® exhibited the best cell performance at a higher current density (>∼0.8 A cm−2). The results indicate that the physicochemical properties of membranes (e.g., ionic conductivity, water uptake, thickness) are intrinsic factors that affect the cell performance of water electrolyzers.74 The underlying determining factors are briefly illustrated, and these challenges will be further discussed in the following section.
Fig. 5 (a) Overview of the basic components of AEM water electrolyzers at the system level. (b and c) Images of a normal water electrolyzer stack and the components inside. Working polarization curves of AEM water electrolyzers impacted by (d) temperature. (Reproduced with permission from ref. 75 Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society.) (e) Different electrolytes. (Reproduced with permission from ref. 68 Copyright 2017 Elsevier.) (f) Different membranes. (Reproduced with permission from ref. 74 Copyright 2020 Elsevier.)
3.2.2. AEMFCs. As a typical H2 utilization device, a system overview of the AEM fuel cell stack, encompassing gas/water/heat management systems, is illustrated in Fig. 6a. The fuel cell stack is usually composed of a large number of single cells to achieve the required power and/or energy density. Inlet gases including H2 and air are manipulated to a certain pressure, temperature, and humidity through the H2 and air management system before entering the fuel cell stack. Due to the complexity of the water movement in AEMFCs, an additional water management system is usually coupled with the gas management system to jointly tune the water content in the cell stack.76,77 Furthermore, a thermal management system is required to alleviate the stack overheating problems.78 For an electrical vehicle powered by fuel cells, the major components include, but are not limited to, the fuel cell stack, electric motor, hydrogen tank, etc., in which a rather complex fuel cell management system is needed to achieve a smooth power output. Owing to the strong cost competitiveness of AEM fuel cells over their PEM counterparts, it is expected that AEMFCs will dominate the market for fuel cell electric vehicles (FCEVs), although currently PEMFCs are mounted in commercial FCEVs (e.g., the Toyota Mirai).79 For an AEMFC, the effects of a range of operation conditions on the cell performance, including the inlet gas and electrolyte ions are illustrated in Fig. 6c and d. The open circuit voltage (OCV) in AEM fuel cells can be expressed viaeqn (3.9).80
 
(3.9)
where R = 8.3145 J mol−1·K−1 is the gas constant. Given that the operating temperature for AEMFCs normally ranges from 50 to 90 °C, the higher working temperature than standard conditions and partial H2 crossover during the startup mean that the initial OCV is usually lower than the reversible cell potential (1.23 V), as shown in Fig. 6c. Meanwhile, the activation, ohmic, and concentration potential losses stemming from the respective sluggish reaction kinetics, cell resistance, and mass transfer resistance make the cell potential gradually decrease. In addition, another common influential factor in cell performance is the CO2 concentration in the inlet gas stream, where accelerated degradation of cell voltage profile in the presence of 300 ppm CO2 (Fig. 6c) has been reported.82 The adverse effects of CO2 for AEMFCs have been widely documented,83 and the bicarbonate/carbonate ions that are formed possess remarkably lower mobility than OH− (∼1/5 and 1/3 of the mobility of OH−),84,85 thus leading to the loss of membrane conductivity. Moreover, the bicarbonate/carbonate ions tend to accumulate on the anode side, resulting in a pH gradient across the membrane, as depicted in Fig. 6d. This pH gradient would cause concentration overvoltage and indirectly decrease the catalyst reaction kinetics.86 Consequently, many interesting studies on reducing the effects of CO2via gas stream management, the development of a CO2-resistant catalyst, and beyond are highly desirable and will be discussed in the following section. Fig. 6e presents the polarization curve and peak power density profile of an AEMFC (50 °C, H2/O2 as fed gas) with the use of PGM as a hydrogen oxidation reaction (HOR) catalyst (0.5 mgPt cm−2) and Fe–Nx–C as an oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) catalyst (3 mgnon-PGM cm−2).81 Obviously, the power density of 155 mW cm−2 (current density, j = 240 mA cm−2 and E = 0.65 V) for this AEMFC is far lower than that for a PEMFC (∼440 mW cm−2) employing non-PGM/PGM based ORR/HOR catalysts, let alone the state-of-the-art PEMFCs (∼1000 mW cm−2) using PGM-based catalysts.87,88 On the other hand, the US Department of Energy (DOE) has set a 2025 target for PGM catalyst in PEMFCs to further increase its activity and durability while reducing the cost. On account of these incentives, it is highly imperative to develop non-PGM-based AEMFCs to compete with or even replace their PEM counterparts, so that the extremely high production cost of PEMFCs can be fundamentally overcome. To this end, an in-depth understanding of the key challenges in AEM fuel cells will be carefully discussed in the following section in an attempt to find plausible solutions for low-cost high-performance AEMFCs.
Fig. 6 (a) Overview of the basic components of AEM fuel cells at the system level. (b) Illustration of the key components for an electric vehicle powered by an H2–O2 fuel cell and the cell stack architecture. (c) Polarization curves of AEM fuel cells impacted by CO2 concentrations in the feeding gas. (d) The effects of (bi)carbonate ions on the AEM during operation. (e) Performance of the Fe–Nx–C type cathode catalyst-based AEMFCs with a loading amount of 3 mg non-PGM cm−2. Reproduced with permission from ref. 81 Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society.

3.3. Key performance indicators (KPIs) for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

Based on the challenges for and key technological breakthroughs in water electrolyzers in the past 200 years of development, the International Renewable Energy Agency (IRENA) has roughly classified this period into five generations of electrolyzers.38 Specifically, for the current 5th generation (post-2020) water electrolyzers, the key challenge lies in how to translate the water electrolyzer from the niche to the GW scale to attain its expected role in green H2 production. Meanwhile, the detailed target for 5th generation water electrolyzers is a trade-off between cost (<US$ 200 per kW), durability (50000 h), and efficiency (∼80% lower heating value (LHV)).38Table 2 gives a comparison of key parameters of AEM water electrolyzer technology in 2020 and outlines the projected targets for 2050. It is foreseen that great improvements in durability and capital cost are highly needed for AEM water electrolyzers to achieve the 2050 target. Moreover, the 2050 target can be used to provide guidelines for industry and academic researchers for the rational design and development of key components, working management, and operation monitoring to accelerate the practical deployment of AEMWE technology before 2050. Apart from AEM water electrolyzer technology, AEM fuel cells also achieved fast progress, so KPIs of AEMFC key components and evaluation parameters are very much needed. To this end, the KPIs of AEMFCs in 2020 and the targets in 2030 are illustrated in Table 3. Considering that the currently reported AEMFCs have mostly employed PGM-based materials as catalysts, the specific targets for PGM-based and PGM-free catalysts are both provided. Generally speaking, the ultimate goal of AEMFCs is to achieve a comparable power density to PEMFCs with the use of PGM-free catalysts.43,89,90 Therefore, researchers are encouraged to refer to Tables 2 and 3 to have a better understanding of the targets for AEM technologies, which will guide and accelerate the design and advancement of key components and the whole devices.
