3.4. Efficiency of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

To make a reliable and accurate comparison, the efficiencies in describing AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells including system electrical efficiency and/or cell stack efficiency need to be critically reviewed and presented.
System electrical efficiency. Before we discuss the efficiency of AEM devices, the heat of combustion will be introduced. Here, LHV, denoted as the lower heating value, is a term known in industry to measure the released thermal energy in the combustion of a fuel based on the forming of water vapor. The HHV (higher heating value) is a higher value for describing the thermal energy of a fuel combusted based on forming liquid water. For AEM fuel cells, the electrical efficiency is usually determined by dividing the produced electricity by the HHV or LHV of the fuel used.91 The HHV and LHV of H2 are 39.4 kW h kg−1 (141.7 MJ kg−1) and 33.3 kW h kg−1 (120 MJ kg−1), respectively.20,92 Consequently, the system electrical efficiency of AEM fuel cells can be described viaeqn (3.10). For AEM water electrolyzers that are the reverse of the fuel cells, the system electrical efficiency can be inverted as shown in eqn (3.11).
 
(3.10)
 
(3.11)
One should note that the system electrical efficiency is widely known in industry and applied to describe the cell systems that include a range of individual components (e.g., power conditioners, controls, cell stacks, humidifiers, fuel processors, etc.). It is suggested to indicate whether it is calculated based on the HHV or the LHV when quoting electrical efficiency.
Voltage efficiency for a single cell or cell stacks. In contrast, the voltage efficiency for a single cell and/or cell stacks is more suitable for fundamental researchers to assess the efficiency of the two devices. Here, the voltage efficiency of a single cell and/or cell stacks is expressed viaeqn (3.12).
 
(3.12)
The thermodynamic voltage can be calculated viaeqn (3.9). As mentioned before, the thermodynamic voltage of water electrolyzers/H2–O2 fuel cells operating at standard temperature and pressure is 1.23 V. For fuel cells, the operating voltage is lower than the thermodynamic voltage due to the overpotential coming from reaction kinetics, ohmic resistance, etc.; hence, the voltage efficiency for AEM fuel cells is less than 100%. For AEM water electrolyzers, the voltage efficiency is higher than 100% because under standard temperature and pressure, splitting one mole of liquid water to produce one mole of H2 requires 285.8 kJ of energy that includes 237.2 kJ as electricity and 48.6 kJ as heat.65,91 Calculating the voltage efficiency of water electrolyzers viaeqn (3.12) does not consider the required heat and hence causes a value higher than 100%. Consequently, the water splitting energy (285.8 kJ mol−1) is rather reasonable to be employed to calculate the thermoneutral voltage considering H2 and O2 are produced with 100% thermal efficiency without any waste heat. The thermoneutral voltage (E) is calculated to be 1.481 V and a rather reasonable voltage efficiency for AEM water electrolyzer single cell and/or cell stacks can be determined viaeqn (3.13).
 
(3.13)

3.5. Challenges of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

3.5.1. Scientific challenges. A view of the architecture of the membrane electrode assembly (MEA) for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells is presented in Fig. 7a, which is known to play a dominant role in the durability and performance of water electrolyzers and fuel cells. As illustrated in Fig. 7a, the membrane electrode assembly is composed of an anion exchange membrane, two catalyst layers, and two gas diffusion layers, respectively, which form the well-known 5-layer MEA. The gas-diffusion layer, intended to supply high gas permeability and electronic conductivity while preventing water flooding, is composed of a substrate and a microporous layer (MPL) that are normally prepared from Teflon-treated carbon cloth and Teflon-modified carbon powder, respectively. To reach a balance between mechanical strength, chemical stability, and mass transfer efficiency, the thickness of the GDL usually ranges from 200 to 400 μm. Apart from the GDL, electrocatalysts and ionomers are usually dispersed in isopropyl alcohol (IPA), which is then coated onto the anion exchange membrane to form a catalyst-coated membrane (CCM) to maximize the reaction kinetics and narrow the electron/hydroxide transportation pathways.93,94 In addition to the CCM, catalyst ink (prepared from catalysts and ionomers) can be also deposited in the gas diffusion layer to form a gas diffusion electrode (GDE), which is also known as a catalyst coated substrate (CCS).94–96 The central part of an MEA is the membrane, which not only needs to have fast conduction of ions and water molecules, but also needs to withstand harsh working conditions such as higher pressure, voltage, and working temperature.38 A single cell of an AEM water electrolyzer or fuel cell is at least composed of an anion-exchange membrane, anode and cathode catalyst layers, two gas-diffusion layers, and two bipolar plates, respectively. In a practical cell assembly, gaskets and sealing materials are often added to increase the airtightness and decrease gas crossover, since both are important to improve cell safety and efficiency. The fundamental challenges and technological obstacles for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells are illustrated in Fig. 7b and c, where the fundamental issues normally relate to the problems of each component in AEM devices, which need to be individually addressed, while technological obstacles usually have to do with the challenges faced at the system level from MEAs to single cells or even MW-scale cell stacks. According to the KPIs of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells reported by the IEA, state-of-the-art water electrolyzers face challenges from the niche to the mainstream level, from the MW to the GW scale, and from potential to reality. Furthermore, the levelized cost, durability, and working efficiency of future water electrolyzers are projected to be lower than 200 US$ kW−1, higher than 50000 hours, and approach 80%, respectively.38,97 For AEM fuel cells, the currently employed PGM-based electrocatalyst is expected to be fully replaced with PGM-free catalysts, and the peak power density will be maintained at no lower than 600 mW cm−2, in addition to a stable operation time of more than 100000 h.90,98 The overall requirements on AEMWEs and AEMFCs also lead to specific demands on cell components, and the challenges and benefit analysis for each component are depicted in Fig. 7b. The most challenging as well as the most profitable aspect for AEM technology comes at the membrane and triple-phase boundary (TPB) level, which includes membrane durability, chemical binding, and mechanical stability of the ionomer/catalyst, as well as the optimal ionomer for building the TPB. Next to it, the moderately challenging part on the one hand relates to electrocatalyst kinetics and durability. On the other hand, it is necessary to decrease the membrane resistance and gas permeability. These two moderate challenges are expected to bring identically high profits. Third, developing a low-cost GDL, reducing the interface resistance from catalyst to GDL, and controlling the oxidized state of the catalyst on the electrode are considered three moderate challenges but will bring medium benefits.
Fig. 7 (a) Overview of the architecture of the membrane electrode assembly with three key components, namely, the membrane, the catalyst layer, and the gas-diffusion layer. (b) The degree of difficulty and profit analysis for the development of fundamental components in AEMWEs and AEMFCs. (c) Key problems and technological obstacles for the development of AEMWEs and AEMFCs at three different levels, the 3-layer MEA level, the single cell level, and the cell stack system level.
3.5.2. Technological barriers. The technological obstacles from moderate to high for AEM devices are presented in Fig. 7c. The electrochemical performance of AEMWEs and AEMFCs is heavily dependent on the MEA, and hence, the fabrication method for the MEA will play a decisive role in AEM devices. Consequently, finding optimal fabrication methods (CCM or CCS) to build a robust and high-performance MEA is most important for the 3-layer MEA, which is a relatively moderate obstacle. For a single cell, bipolar plates and gas diffusion layers are two key components of the cell. The moderate fundamental challenge of developing a low-cost GDL has been discussed before; how to set a balance between different key components to achieving cell performance and durability is critical. The thickness of each component is roughly described in Fig. 7a, but the geometrical size of each layer is a compromise between the reaction kinetics and transport properties on the one hand and the production cost on the other, which is highly dependent on the raw materials. For instance, higher OH− conductivity can be accessed by narrowing the thickness of the membrane and enhancing its water uptake; unfortunately, this will, in turn, weaken its mechanical strength and chemical durability. Meanwhile, a thinner membrane usually implies a higher gas crossover, which also causes potential safety problems and decreases cell efficiency. In addition to a delicate balance between every cell component, the cell assembly needs to maintain high quality and avoid any safety problems to ensure the cell's efficiency and lifespan. These two aspects compose the most crucial technological obstacles for a single cell, which is remarkably higher than for the 3-layer MEA. Last but not least, overwhelming obstacles come at the level of the cell stack system. For water electrolyzers, a suitable electrolyte is necessary to ensure high cell activity and durability. For fuel cells, water management is extremely complicated due to the unbalanced water production and consumption at the anode and cathode areas. In addition, the cell system is required to mount gas management, heat management, and balance of plant systems to reduce the problems of gas crossover and overheating, which is expected to improve the system's efficiency and safety.

4. Key components of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

4.1. Membranes for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

4.1.1. Chemistry of the anion-exchange membrane. The anion-exchange membrane (AEM) of AEMWEs and AEMFCs is a thin-layered polymer matrix that permits the shuttling of anions (e.g., OH−, CO32−, Cl−, etc.) and water molecules but blocks the electrons and gases. In the fabrication of cell stacks, the membrane is in direct contact with the catalyst layer that will be further assembled with gas-diffusion layers and bipolar plates. During operation, the redox reactions occur at the catalyst/ionomer/membrane interface and form a triple-phase boundary (TPB). The critical demands on mass transfer, electron transfer, anion shuttling, water flow, durability, robustness, etc. for building excellent water electrolyzers and fuel cells make the anion-exchange membrane the most critical part. This requires a membrane with excellent chemical, mechanical, and thermal stability, in addition to good ionic conductivity. Based on the above-mentioned requirements (Fig. 8a), AEM is usually made of a polymer matrix that is composed of polymer backbones as the main chains and cationic groups as the side chains. Meanwhile, organic linkers are usually needed to connect the cationic groups and the polymer main chain. The cationic groups provide the anchoring sites for anion transportation, and the investigated cationic groups include quaternary ammonium (QA) ions, phosphonium, ligand–metal complexes, sulphonium, tertiary diamines, etc. On the other hand, the polymer backbones include polyphenylene oxide (PPO), polyphenylene, poly(styrene), ethylene tetrafluoroethylene (ETFE), polyvinyl alcohol (PVA), etc. We next discuss the fundamental physicochemical properties of the membrane (Fig. 8b) and the interplay of these features in building membranes with desirable OH− conductivity, chemical stability, mechanical stability, and gas permeability.
Fig. 8 (a) Composition-structure analysis of an anion-exchange membrane (AEM), including the polymer backbones and cationic groups. (b) Fundamental properties of the AEM and (c) performance targets of the AEM through trade-off of the fundamental features. (d) A comparison of key features of commercially available membranes and academically reported membranes. BTMA: benzyl trimethylammonium.

Ion exchange capacity (IEC). Ion exchange capacity (IEC) is generally identified as the moles of fixed cationic groups per gram of dry polymer. It is usually determined by aqueous or non-aqueous titration or nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR).99,100 For hydroxide or chloride exchange membranes, IEC can be measured by back-titration in HCl (∼0.1 M) or KCl (0.1 M) solution, respectively. Specifically, the hydroxide exchange membrane, together with a control sample without a membrane can be immersed in HCl (∼0.1 M) solution to achieve a complete exchange of the hydroxide ions. The HCl solutions are then respectively titrated with standard KOH solutions, in which the difference in the consumed volume of the standard KOH solution (ΔV) in titrating the HCl solution can be used to calculate the number of hydroxide ions.99 The membrane is dried in a vacuum oven to obtain its dry weight (Wdry) after titration.99 The IEC (mmol g−1) is calculated according to eqn (4.1). Here, ΔVNaOH and CNaOH are the volume and the concentration of the titrant solution, respectively.
 
(4.1)
Water uptake (WU), hydration number (λ), and swelling ratio (SR). Anion-exchange membranes possess a strong affinity for water. Water uptake (WU) is the relative weight ratio of the adsorbed water with respect to the dry membrane.100 The WU of a membrane is essentially determined by the polymer backbone, and the testing conditions (e.g., hydrothermal treatment and drying temperature) would affect the tested value. To be specific, the weights and surface areas of the wet membrane (equilibrated in deionized water at a series of temperatures) and the dry membrane (dried in an oven (60–80 °C)) are suggested by measuring three times with no more than ± 5% deviation. The obtained values are used to calculate the water uptake, hydration number (λ), and swelling ratio of the membrane according to the following equations (eqn (4.2), (4.3) and (4.4)).
 
(4.2)
 
(4.3)
 
(4.4)
Tensile/strain strength (TS) and elongation at break (EB %). Tensile/strain strength is the ability of the membrane to withstand maximum tensile stress while being stretched without failing or breaking.101 The tensile/strain strength of a membrane can be obtained from a stress–strain curve that is usually recorded using a tensile tester.102 Further increasing the tensile stress would result in membrane deformation. Elongation at break (EB) is the ratio between the changed length at breakage (Lbreak) and the initial length (Linitial) of the membrane. The tensile/strain strength and elongation at break of a membrane are expressed by the following equations. Fmax is the maximum stress at the membrane breaking point, and S is the cross-sectional area of the membrane along the direction of the applied stress (eqn (4.5) and (4.6)).
 
(4.5)
 
(4.6)
Ion conductivity (σ), chemical and mechanical stability, and gas permeability. Ion conductivity (σ) is one of the most significant parameters of the membrane, which is essentially determined by the physicochemical properties of the membrane. The ion conductivity of a membrane can be calculated according to eqn (4.7).103 Here, d, S, and R are the thickness, surface area, and resistance of the membrane, respectively. Degradation of ionic conductivity of the membrane can be measured by the chemical stability, which can be obtained by testing the σ before and after a certain amount of operation time. The mechanical stability of the membrane is intrinsically determined by the polymer backbone, while the water content also plays a significant role. In general, an excess amount of water uptake usually makes the membrane fragile, thus reducing the mechanical and chemical stability of the membrane. Moreover, the tensile/strain strength, elongation at break, Young's modulus, etc. have been widely employed as standard parameters to evaluate the mechanical stability of a membrane. Last but not least, the gas permeability of a membrane, the ability of the membrane to allow gases to pass through it, is theoretically determined by the polymer matrix.104 First, different types of gas molecules possess varied solubility and diffusivity in the polymer, and hence, they exhibit different permeation in a membrane.105 Second, the structure, polarity, orientation, fillers, humidity, etc. of the membrane also have a huge influence on gas permeability. For AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, ideal membranes should also block the H2/O2 permeation to maximize the efficiency of the device. The gas permeability is usually quoted in units of Barrer (1 Barrer = 3.35 × 10−16 mol (m s Pa)−1).106
 
(4.7)
We carefully reviewed the state-of-the-art performance of AEMs from the literature with reference to proton-exchange membrane performances in PEMWEs and PEMFCs, and the desired parameters of an excellent AEM can be projected as shown in Fig. 8c. The membrane parameters here are given as a reference for the research community for fast, reliable, and accurate AEM screening and developing. Specifically, an AEM should possess excellent OH− conductivity (>100 mS cm−1), outstanding chemical stability (with OH− conductivity retention of over 80% after 50000 h operation and thermal stability > 100 °C), strong mechanical stability (50000–100000 h), and lower gas permeability (<10 × 10−17 mol (cm s Pa)−1). It is challenging to satisfy the above-mentioned requirements simultaneously, and the trade-off of the fundamental properties of the membrane must be carefully designed. In Fig. 8d, the physicochemical properties of commercial AEMs vs. academically reported AEMs are summarized. The poly(fluorenyl aryl piperidinium) (PFAP)-based membrane has been reported with higher OH− conductivity and excellent mechanical stability compared to commercial membranes,103,107 but the durability might not compete with some of the commercial membranes (e.g., Sustainion® X37-5). Consequently, the durability of the reported membranes and the membrane production costs need to be further improved for practical electrolyzers and fuel cells.
4.1.2. Transportation dynamics of anion-exchange membrane. Understanding the transportation mechanism and degradation pathway of the anions in AEMs is a prerequisite to developing low-cost high-performance AEMs. Initially, the proton transportation mechanism in the PEM was well studied, and currently, the dominant proton shuttling theory includes the Grotthuss mechanism, diffusion, migration, and convection.108,109 In addition, the experimentally measured proton conductivity is also highly dependent on specific conditions, such as temperature, relative humidity (RH), and pressure.99,110 Interestingly, pioneering researchers found that the conductivity of hydroxide ions in AEMs is influenced similarly by these factors, with the consequence that the shuttle mechanism of hydroxide ions in AEMs is thought to be equally dominated by the Grotthuss mechanism, diffusion, migration, and convection, as shown in Fig. 9a.110,111 For the transportation of protons (H+) in PEM, the Grotthuss mechanism is based on the formation and concomitant cleavage of covalent bonds with neighboring molecules. In the AEM context, research has revealed that OH− exhibits Grotthuss behavior as H+ does in an aqueous solution, so that the movement of OH− is identically associated with hydrogen bond formation and cleavage along a chain of water molecules.112,113 In addition, diffusion and migration in the transport of OH−, following the concentration and/or electrical gradient in aqueous solution, also occur. Convective transport implies that the diffusion of water molecules through the membrane is imposed by the hydraulic pressure gradient, stemming from the unbalanced water concentrations on both sides of the membrane, induced by the dragging of water molecules by OH−. Currently, the Grotthuss mechanism, diffusion and migration, and convection are considered as the most possible mechanisms for OH− transport in AEMs.103,110,114,115 Apart from experimental verification, exploring the ion shuttling dynamics from the viewpoint of theoretical chemistry is another important way to find the truth. In view of this, Voth and co-authors proposed a multiscale reactive molecular dynamics (MS-RMD) model to investigate the solvation structure and ion transport dynamics of hydroxide ions in AEMs.116 The proposed MS-RMD model enables the capture of the bonding configuration of the studied element following the changes in the environment, which is based on the multistate empirical valence bond algorithm with higher efficiency117 and overcomes the problem of modeling Grotthuss shuttling, which has not been resolved by conventional classical molecular dynamics. In this work, poly(vinyl benzyltrimethylammonium) (PVBTMA), bearing hydration of 14, was adopted to study the water dynamics in the fully hydrated state. By building and comparing the hydroxide models of MS-RMD-OH (bulk water system) and MS-RMD-PVBTMA (PVBTMA membrane system), the solvation structure and hydroxide mechanism in the PVBTMA can be reasonably deduced. In Fig. 9b, the QA+ group at the end of a side chain is shown to attach to the polymer backbone. The distance between QA+ groups from two adjacent side chains was determined to be 6.5 Å, while the first peak in the radial distribution function (RDFs) for the N–O* configuration ranges from 3.5 to 6.1 Å, suggesting that part of their first coordination shells is well overlapped, as shown in the dark blue area in Fig. 9b. Meanwhile, the overlapped regions and the first coordination shells of N–O* for the QA+ groups form a continuous surface channel, where the hydroxide ions have a higher preference to stay, freely migrate, and be transported. Moreover, a significant higher “continuous” mean square displacement (MSD) component in comparison with a “discrete” MSD component was demonstrated for the PVBTMA system, indicating that the dominant contribution was from a vehicular mechanism (related to the “continuous” component) as compared to the Grotthuss hopping pathway (related to the “discrete” component). Finally, the activation energy (ΔEa) of hydroxide diffusion in an AEM is verified to be larger than the ΔEa for proton diffusion in a PEM, manifesting that elevated temperature could promote the transport kinetics of OH− in AEMs considerably more than for H+ in PEMs. One should note that the mechanism proposed by Voth was derived from the study of a block copolymer with fully hydrated hydrophilic domains, which might not be suitable to describe the water dynamics in the nonblocky polymers with a relatively low degree of functionalization. In light of this, Bedrov's group selected poly(p-phenylene oxide) (PPO)-based AEMs and discussed the OH− transport mechanism in AEMs made of nonblocky polymers.118 Here, the reactive ReaxFF and nonreactive Atomistic Polarizable Potential for Liquids, Electrolytes, and Polymers (APPLE&P) force fields were employed in a joint molecular dynamics (MD) study to gain in-depth insight into the water transport behaviour.119–121 As shown in Fig. 9c, the shuttling of hydroxide ions along the sub-nanometer wide water channels is provided, where the mobility of OH− is highly alleviated by the narrow bottlenecks. The transport of OH− through the narrow bottlenecks requires balancing the vehicular and Grotthuss hopping mechanisms. This is because the vehicular mechanism requires the partial dehydration of OH−, which is thermodynamically unfavourable and consequently leads to higher activation energy. On the other hand, Grotthuss hopping works very efficiently, so that OH− moves from one side of the bottleneck to the other with negligible loss of the hydration structure and minimized activation energy. This work highlights the importance of the trade-off between the vehicular and Grotthuss hopping mechanisms in the membranes that are made of nonblocky polymers. It is interesting to note that the water and OH− transport behaviour is heavily dependent on the water content inside the membrane, which is also highly associated with the molecular architecture of membrane ion-conducting channels.122–124 Deciphering the water and OH− dynamics in relation to membrane hydration is another scientific issue that remains to be addressed. Based on this, Lyonnard and co-authors proposed the employment of quasi-elastic neutron scattering (QENS) to thoroughly investigate the polymer–water interaction and OH− transport mechanism over a range of temperatures, hydration levels, and timescales.125 In this work, a commercial anion-conducting membrane (Fumasep, FAD-55) was studied. As described in Fig. 9d–g, elastic scattering intensities were analysed using elastic fixed window scans (EFWS) of FAD-55 membranes that had been chemically and isotopically substituted. The profile of EFWS as a function of temperature can be classified into four regions. In region 1 (T < 200 K), a regular loss in the EFWS intensity for λ = 4 can be assigned to the fast rotation of –CH3 groups attached to the polymer. In region 2 (200 K < T < 250 K), a rapid decrease in EFWS intensity accompanied by a sharp increase in m.s.d is observed for all samples (λ = 4 and λ = 13), which can be ascribed to the activation of first polymer side-chain dynamics that takes place within a few picoseconds. Regarding region 3 (250 K < T < ∼270 K), a continuously sudden drop of EFWS intensity starting from 250 K appears across the slower to fast timescale measurements (Fig. 9d–g), which can be attributed to the rotational–translational water dynamics activated within hydrated AEM samples. With regard to region 4 (T > ∼270 K), another regular decrease in EFWS intensity associated with increase in m.s.d. is observed for λ = 4 and λ = 13, implying the activation of a further dynamics process occurring on a much slower timescale. Based on the TGA analysis, this can be assigned to the ion hopping process that is accelerated by the remaining water molecules bridging two OH− groups. The EFWS intensity is translated into water and OH− dynamics and mechanisms, as illustrated in Fig. 9h and i. The water distribution within the cell is uneven between the anode and cathode, and the hydroxide transport mechanism is highly dependent on the water uptake. Under fully hydrated conditions (λ = 13), the OH− shuttling process is mainly occupied by vehicular transport, so the self-diffusion coefficient of translationally mobile species is comparable to the total diffusion coefficient of the membrane. The simulations suggest a long-range component that is comparable to the previously reported value, implying that the Grotthuss mechanism contributes to ∼15% of the total process. For a membrane bearing a moderate hydration (λ = 8) level, the self-diffusion coefficient is 50% lower than that under fully hydrated conditions. In the case of lower hydration conditions (λ ≤ 8) (Fig. 9i), the water-mediated OH− hopping dominates the hydroxide transport mechanism, while the contribution arising from the vehicular mechanism takes only a limited share. This work provides a systematic investigation of the water and hydroxide dynamics within a fast timescale at different temperature ranges, and for the first time, reveals the OH− shuttling mechanism under different water hydration conditions, which will provide constructive guidance for the design of functional anion-exchange membranes.
Fig. 9 Schematic illustration of hydroxide ion transfer dynamics in an AEM. (a) A combination of the Grotthuss mechanism, diffusion and migration, and convection. Reproduced with permission from ref. 110 Copyright 2010 IOP Publishing. (b) Understanding hydroxide solvation and transport in the AEM. Reproduced with permission from ref. 116 Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. (c) Grotthuss transition of hydroxide ions in an AEM. Reproduced with permission from ref. 118 Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. (d–g) Elastic and inelastic scattering intensity (EFWS) and mean square atomic displacement (m.s.d.) values of the FAD-55 membrane. (h and i) Water dynamic mechanism and diffusion coefficients of anionic species. Reproduced with permission from ref. 125 Copyright 2022 Springer Nature.
4.1.3. Degradation of anion-exchange membrane. Given that the anion-exchange membrane is composed of polymer backbones, cationic groups, and polymer-cationic-group linkages, all of them are prone to suffering from degradation under practical operation, leading to reduced ion conductivity, and weakened mechanical and chemical stability. The degradation mechanism of each unit varies according to the specific polymer composition. In the case of a commercially available AEM (benzyl trimethylammonium (BTMA)), the cationic groups (alkyl ammoniums) are supposed to be attacked by hydroxide ions, leading to the formation of alcohol and trimethylamine (Fig. 10a).40,126 The nucleophile substitution (SN2 substitution) mechanism has been well studied and is considered the prominent degradation mechanism in the QA head groups.126,127 Besides SN2 substitution, ylide formation and Hofmann elimination were also reported as possible degradation pathways for quaternary ammonium cationic groups mainly dependent on the presence or absence of β-hydrogen atoms.128–132 In addition to QA groups, pyridinium and imidazolium are typical representatives of N-based cyclic cationic groups that have been widely investigated. In Fig. 10a, pyridinium is very likely to undergo nucleophilic addition and displacement at the 2- and 4-positions, as well as further oxidation with the formation of amide by-products.128 In the meantime, diazabicyclooctane (DABCO)-based cations were also studied. Their cyclic structure in the vicinity of cationic nitrogen impedes the nucleophilic attack of hydroxide ions while making the degradation of DABCO follow the nucleophilic displacement reaction.130 In contrast to QA, the stability of phosphonium and sulfonium cationic groups is less stable and tends to suffer from faster degradation.127,133,134 The OH− attacking the cationic S sites results in a Stevens rearrangement of benzyldimethylsulfonium, as shown in Fig. 10a. Considering the increasing amount of cationic groups in the membrane studies, a precise understanding of the degradation mechanism for each group is challenging. It has to be noted that the degradation pathway for most of the reported membranes (e.g., QA, imidazolium, guanidinium, pyridinium, tertiary sulfonium, phosphonium, etc.) is usually initiated by nucleophile (OH−) attack, followed by substitution, rearrangement, etc. On the other hand, stability issues of polymer backbones associated with the OH− attack also cause the degradation of AEM, which needs to be investigated to obtain highly durable AEMs. Following the concept of wisely choosing cationic groups, the studied polymer backbones range from polysulfones and fluorinated polymers to polyolefins, polyaromatics, etc.135–138 Polysulfones and fluorinated polymers are susceptible to suffering from the ether and quaternary carbon hydrolysis and dehydrofluorination, respectively, stemming from hydroxide attack.139,140 Notably, in Fig. 10b, the carbon skeleton of partially fluorinated polyolefins tends to be attacked by OH−, triggering the generation of CC double bonds and further chain scission.140,141 Meanwhile, the aryl ether linkages (C–O–C bonds) existing in polysulfones and/or quaternary-ammonium-tethered poly(arylene ether)s are easily attacked by OH−. It was reported that the electron-withdrawing groups and certain types of side chains both increase the accessibility of OH− and intensify the degradation, accelerating the aryl ether cleavage, as shown in Fig. 10b.126,142 Kim and co-authors employed DFT calculations to further demonstrate that the energy barrier (85.8 kJ mol−1) of aryl-ether cleavage is significantly reduced than that of the aryl–aryl cleavage (223.8–246.0 kJ mol−1), again indicating the excellent stability of aromatic polymer backbones made only of carbon–carbon bonds.143 In contrast, the alkaline stability of non-fluorinated polyolefins and fully fluorinated polyolefins is remarkably better, in which negligible backbones can be detected after several hundred hours of operation.137,144 Polybenzimidazoles have also been commonly used as polymer backbones in addition to working as tethered cationic groups.
Fig. 10 Degradation pathways and mechanisms for specific compositions of an AEM. (a) Cationic groups, (b) polymer backbones, and (c) membrane linkages.

Although the polybenzimidazole backbone has been recognized as quite stable, the degradation triggered by nucleophilic attack at the benzimidazole C2 position is hardly unavoidable (Fig. 10b), where the chain is broken with the formation of free amino and carboxylate residues.145 Apart from polymer backbones and cationic groups, the polymer-cationic-group linkages or linkers (Fig. 10c) also face degradation problems arising from hydroxide ion attack.

4.1.4. Design and fabrication of anion exchange membranes. An understanding of the ion dynamics and degradation mechanisms of AEMs offers guidelines for the design and optimization of anion-exchange membranes with high performance. In Fig. 11a–c, the membrane fabrication methods and the modulation strategies are displayed. Specifically, the fabrication avenues for the anion-exchange membranes can be grouped into direct polymerization and post-polymerization functionalization, depending on whether the cationic groups have been installed on the polymer backbones. A comprehensive introduction to a variety of synthetic strategies for AEMs has recently been reported.146,147 Several polymerization methods, including copolymerization, sol–gel, grafting, plasma polymerization, etc., are presented in Fig. 11b. Furthermore, the membrane modulation strategies according to our understanding of membrane degradation mechanism are presented in Fig. 11c, and we will give detailed examples on how an AEM is rationally designed and optimized from the viewpoint of the polymer matrix, steric hindrance, and electron-donating groups in the next chapter.
Fig. 11 (a) Synthetic approaches and (b) specific methods for anion-exchange membranes. (c) The commonly employed AEM design and tuning strategies.
4.1.5. Advances on anion exchange membranes. Developing functional membranes with high anion conductivity, chemical durability, mechanical robustness, and low gas permeability is the target for membrane scientists. To this end, the state-of-the-art progress of AEMs guided by the mentioned design and tuning strategies will be discussed in this section. Finding superior polymer matrices for membrane backbones and positive groups, as well as understanding the underlying rationale, are the first and foremost considerations. Inspired by this, Choe and Bae did a systematic alkaline stability study on a variety of different polymer backbones.148 Specifically, four different types of polymer structures, including Udel polysulfone (PSU), poly(phenylene oxide) (PPO), poly(biphenylalkylene) (PB), polystyrene-b-poly(ethylene-co-butylene)-b-polystyrene (SEBS), and their counterparts modified by electron-withdrawing groups (PSU-CH2Cl/F, PPO-CH2Br, bromoalkyl-tethered PB (PBA), and SEBS-CH2Cl) were processed under different alkaline conditions (KOH and NaOCH3 solutions). Meanwhile, gel permeation chromatography (GPC) and 1H NMR spectroscopy were employed to conduct a quantitative analysis of the degradation of the polymer chains. As shown in Fig. 12a, by comparing the average molecular weight (Mn) of the four polymer structures and their counterparts, the degradation behaviour of the four types of polymer structures was obtained. The four polymer structures exhibited higher anti-degradation capability than their counterparts, and SEBS showed the best performance, while PSU-CH2Cl delivered the poorest chemical stability toward base media. The small molecule model and experiments confirmed that the presence of aryl ether bonds in a polymer matrix showed reduced chemical stability under alkaline treatment. Furthermore, the electron-withdrawing groups such as sulfone and benzyl trimethylammonium (BTMA) could accelerate the chemical degradation by decreasing the energy barrier of aryl ether bond cleavage. Consequently, a consensus was increasingly reached that higher alkaline stability when using all-carbon backbones to fabricate AEMs can be obtained compared with those heteroatom-containing backbones.146,149 The earlier investigated positive groups have been discussed in several excellent articles.126,150,151 In terms of commercial BTMA, the susceptibility to degradation via nucleophilic substitution and ylid elimination restricts the long-term operation of BTMA in AEMs, although it features easy fabrication and decent hydroxide ion conductivity.152 Hence, an increasing number of researchers aimed to propose a universal protocol to make a quantitative assessment and comparison of a variety of cationic groups across the membrane research community.152–155 Coates et al. carefully considered all the possible parameters affecting the evaluation of membrane degradation, such as type of solvent, base and cation concentration, the hydrogen/deuterium exchange process, and the reaction vessel, and proposed the protocol illustrated in Fig. 12b.156 Based on this unified protocol, the remaining cation percentages after a 30 day alkaline treatment for 20 organic cationic model compounds were obtained as a general cation stability trend for cation design and development. Piperidinium and tetraalkylammonium were the most stable cations, while the chemical stability of simple imidazolium, pyridinium, and guanidinium remains to be improved.157,158 Apart from the commonly studied types of the polymer matrix, the discovery of unique functional polymers is ongoing. Lee and co-authors proposed poly(fluorene-co-terphenyl N,N′-dimethylpiperidinium) (PFTP-x, where x gives the molar ratio of the fluorenyl piperidinium segment in the copolymer) as an AEM in view of its excellent hydroxide conductivity and rigidity.107 The molecular structure of PFTP is shown in Fig. 12c, and an optical image of the thin membrane film (7–20 μm) together with its cross-sectional scanning electron microscope (SEM) image is also attached. By tuning the water uptake and the swelling ratio of PFTP through changes in the ratio of fluorenyl piperidinium in the membrane, the ideal ionic conductivity can be derived. Consequently, PFTP-13, bearing the lowest swelling ratio, exhibited an OH− conductivity of 208 mS cm−1 at 98 °C. By using PFTP-13 as the membrane, Pt-Ru/C as the anode catalyst, and a 75%/100% anode/cathode RH, the peak power density (PPD) of the AEMFC reached 1.67 W cm−2 (0/0 bar back pressure and fed with H2–O2) and 2.34 W cm−2 (1.3/1.3 bar back pressure and fed with H2–O2) at 80 °C. The OH− conductivity of PFTP-13 and the PPD when using the PFTP-13 membrane indicate strong competitiveness over conventional membranes (BTMA-based) and AEMFCs (Fig. 12d). The ∼20% ionic conductivity degradation after testing in 5 M NaOH (2000 h and 80 °C) for PFTP-13 membrane further demonstrated its strong durability and superiority in practical applications (Fig. 12e). Furthermore, the research on many other functional polymers to fabricate AEMs with superior performances has been reported in some very recent articles.159–163 In addition to the judicious choice of cationic groups and polymer backbones, conferring steric hindrance shielding on cationic groups and linkers is also an effective approach to regulating the AEM. Based on this, Zhang et al. proposed to incorporate an alkyl extender to strengthen the chemical stability and improve the hydroxide conductivity of AEMs.164 As shown in Fig. 13a and b, the introduction of different types of spacers and extenders into the polymer backbone is presented, and their performances in water uptake, swelling ratio, and conductivity as functions of the spacers and extenders are comparatively studied. Dual quaternary ammonium attached to the main chain via a spacer was denoted as SDQ. Alkoxyl-extender-containing dual quaternary ammonium attached to the main chain with a spacer (SDQEO), and alkyl-extender-containing dual quaternary ammonium attached to the main chain via a spacer (SDQEC) were introduced into identical polymer backbones (poly(phenylene oxide)) to obtain AEMs. As a proof-of-concept demonstration, the ionic conductivity for the AEM bearing a spacer and a hydrophilic extender in the side chain (SDQEO) increased remarkably because the introduced alkoxyl extender helped to improve the hydrophilicity and the formation of hydrophilic domains. Therefore, the hydroxide conductivity for AEMs with hydrophilic spacers is far higher than for those that do not have hydrophobic spacers, as shown in Fig. 13b. In addition, the chemical stability of SDQEO was remarkably higher, since the steric hindrance arising from the alkoxyl extender can provide additional protection for cationic groups in the membranes. Continuing this concept, a much similar work has been done to improve the membrane properties through modulation of steric hindrance in polymer backbones and cationic sites.165,166 To determine the comprehensive effects of cationic groups and steric hindrance on membrane performance, He et al. did a systematic study aided by DFT calculations and molecular dynamics (MD) simulations.167 In this work, polysulfone (PSF) was selected as the membrane backbone, and trimethylammonium (QA), quaternary phosphonium (QP), and 1-methylimidazolium (Im) were employed as cationic groups. As presented in Fig. 13c and d, it was found that the water sorption of the membrane is mainly correlated with three key factors, i.e., the degree of hydration of the ions, the strength of the interchain interaction, and the location of the cationic groups. Initially, the interchain interactions of the membrane were determined to be of three different types, which are hydrophilic–hydrophilic interactions among QA groups, hydrophilic–hydrophobic interactions between QA and the sulfone groups on the PSF backbone, and hydrophobic–hydrophobic interactions between the sulfone groups. Apart from the two aforementioned strategies, introducing electron donating groups (EDGs) has also recently been employed to optimize the anti-oxidation stability of the membrane. Toward this end, He and co-authors did lots of excellent studies on incorporating electron donating groups into membrane backbones to improve the overall properties ((Fig. 14a).168,169 Specifically, He et al. selected a typical membrane backbone (poly(arylene ether ketone)s, PEAK) and proposed to transform electron-withdrawing CO groups into the electron-donating C-NH2 links to alleviate the aryl-ether cleavage.169 Hence, the CO group in the PEAK is substituted by a C-NH2 to form poly(aryl ether amine) (PEAM). Meanwhile, tethered QA groups were grafted on the para position of the benzylic carbon. In Fig. 14b, the electrostatic potential (ESP) of the as-prepared AEMs (QA-PEAM and QA-PEAK) is provided, where the lower value of ether-aryl phenyl-C for QA-PEAM in comparison with QA-PAEK validates the less likelihood of nucleophilic attack by OH−. Furthermore, the lower Mulliken charge of the linker C for QA-PEAM compared with QA-PAEK again demonstrates the weakened interaction between linker C and OH−, proving the strong hindrance effects coming from the para position of QA groups. Consequently, the lower ESP and Mulliken charge arising from the grafting of the C-NH2 linkage protects the QA groups from nucleophilic attack, which is evidenced by the higher energy barrier of aryl-ether cleavage for QA-PEAM as compared with QA-PAEK (Fig. 14c and d) regardless of whether the degradation followed either the SNAr or the SN2 mechanism. This is very likely the reason for the enhanced alkaline stability of QA-PEAM tested in a 1 M KOH alkaline solution at 80 °C for 192 h. Following this concept, He's group achieved a decent progress in the optimization of AEM through the incorporation of the electron-donating groups on the polymer backbones or groups.168,171 Lee's group presented a detailed study on the alkaline stability of 24 NHA groups under three hydration conditions (λ = 4.8, 7, and 10) to elucidate how the electronic effects of the substituents determine the degradation mechanism of the membrane.170 Taking BTMA and trimethylammonium (TMA) as benchmarks, it is found that the alkaline stability of NHAs bearing electron-withdrawing substituents (e.g., phenyls and heteroatoms) delivered a much reduced alkaline stability. In contrast, the NHAs with electron-donating substituents (–CH2–, –CH3, benzyl, or aliphatic chains) exhibited higher stability than that of the benchmark. Notably, the degradation mechanism of NHAs tethered with two different types of groups was demonstrated by DFT calculations as well as 1H NMR analysis. Specifically, the SN2 reaction rather than the ring-opening E2 reaction dominates the degradation mechanism of most NHA groups under three hydration conditions according to the 1H NMR analysis. As illustrated in Fig. 14e and f, the degradation pathways of N,N-dimethyl piperidinium (DMP), diphenyl-substituted DMP (DP-DMP), 6-azonia-spiro[5.5]undecane (ASU), and diphenyl-substituted ASU (DP-ASU) are found to be dominated by SN2, which can be evidenced by their lower Gibbs free energy values in comparison with E2. In particular, DP-DMP displayed rather lower free energy in the three mentioned types of degradation pathways compared with DMP, indicating that the introduction of electron-withdrawing substituents in the γ-position of NHA decreases the degradation energy barrier to promote the degradation of NHA rings. ASU and DP-ASU show higher Gibbs free energy for the SN2 pathway but lower free energy for E2, suggesting that ASU exhibits a higher alkaline stability than the DMP series.
