4.2. Ionomer for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

4.2.1. Chemistry of ionomer and the design criteria. An ionomer is essentially a type of polymer matrix featuring a similar structure to anion-exchange membranes but playing a distinct role.172,173 In cell configuration, an ionomer works as an ion-conductive binder to form a mechanically stable catalyst layer (catalyst/ionomer) that is sandwiched between the anion-exchange membrane and the gas-diffusion layer (GDL). Many previous studies usually employed identical polymers to fabricate the ionomer and the anion-exchange membrane – an increasing consensus is that these studies in great part overlooked the different roles of the ionomer and AEM in the two devices. For instance, the high gas diffusion capability of the ionomer conflicts with the low gas permeability required for an AEM; hence, the direct employment of the same polymers for the AEM and the ionomer not necessarily leads to satisfactory results. Toward this end, the explicit role of the ionomer in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells is discussed from the following three aspects (Fig. 15a and b). First, the ionomer is required to possess high ionic conductivity, fast water diffusion capability, and quick gas permeability, because the ionomer is dedicated to building a triple-phase boundary (TPB) where the catalytic reactions (HER/HOR and OER/ORR) occur. More critically, the transport of gases, ions, and water should be balanced to avoid technical problems such as water flooding, drying out, etc. as well as ensure efficient power output. Second, the overall stability, including anti-oxidation stability, thermal stability, durability, and mechanical stability, is highly desirable to build robust ionomers for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Last but not least, the ionomer is required to possess excellent binding capability toward the catalyst and feature superior dispersion in the solvent. The strong adhesion capability with catalysts as well as the strong binding capacity between the GDL and the AEM is also one of the crucial properties of ionomers that differentiate it from the AEMs. The three aspects are general requirements for the ionomer for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, which can be used as guidance for the ionomer design and preparation. Considering that the ionomer shares analogous polymer compositions with the AEM, the properties of the ionomer are essentially determined by the chemical features of the polymers, as discussed in Fig. 8b (hydration number, water uptake, tensile/strain strength, etc.), in which a trade-off between these fundamental features is required to achieve high-performance ionomers for AEM devices. Please note that the coated catalyst and the specific reaction that occurs at the TPB also exert huge influences on the ionomers, which, in turn, determine the overall performance of water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Based on this, the development of ionomers associated with catalyst systems and reaction types will be discussed to illustrate the ionomer screening rationale for water electrolyzers and fuel cells. As shown in Fig. 15c, Kim's group studied the influences of different ionomer backbones on the performance of AEMFCs combined with an experimental analysis and DFT calculations.174 Specifically, poly(biphenyl alkylene) with 45% quaternization (BPN45), BPN100, para-poly(terphenyl alkylene) with 100% quaternization (p-TPN), and meta-poly(terphenylene) (m-TPN) poly(fluorene) with 55% quaternization (FLN55) were selected as polymer backbones to study the interaction between catalyst metal surfaces and aromatic components of the ionomer. The peak power density of the membrane electrode assemblies (MEAs) employing five different ionomers decreases in the sequence of FLN55 > BPN45, BPN100 > m-TPN, p-TPN, indicating that the performance of MEAs is highly related to the ionomer backbone structure. Meanwhile, the use of PtRu/C as an anode catalyst in MEA outperformed an MEA with Pt/C as the anode catalyst, again manifesting that the AEM performance is affected by the ionomer–catalyst interactions. On the other hand, the adsorption energy of different aromatic fragments on the Pt surface is calculated to be in the order of p-terphenyl ≥ m-terphenyl > biphenyl > diphenyl ether > benzene ≥ o-terphenyl > 9,9-dimethyl fluorene. This adsorption trend is in close agreement with the MEA performance sequence employing different ionomer backbones, demonstrating that the interaction between the polyaromatic backbone and the electrocatalyst could be a prominent reason for the difference in MEA performance. As a typical ionomer fragment, benzene is verified to form strong hybridization of the π-orbital with metal orbitals of the electrocatalyst; therefore, finding alternative ionomers without aromatic backbones and developing catalysts featuring weakened phenyl adsorption (PtRu/C in this work) might be effective strategies. In the meantime, Kim and many other researchers further confirmed the highly adverse effects of the adsorption of phenyl groups on the HOR kinetics in AEM fuel cells.175–177 In terms of the ORR side of the AEMFCs, the sluggish cathode reaction kinetics, rapid cathode ionomer dry-out, and complex mass transport issues usually lead to enhanced challenges that need to be addressed. Based on this, Dekel et al. employed computational methodology to elucidate the role of the ionomer on the cathode side for AEMFCs.178 To make a reliable comparison, the cell configurations and specific operational parameters, including temperature (60 °C), working current density (0.6 A cm−2), catalyst loading (0.5 mgPt cm−2), catalyst layer thickness (10 μm), membrane thickness (15 μm), etc., were considered. In this case, the cell was modeled under the above conditions, and the voltage loss as a function of the x position in the membrane electrode assembly is presented in Fig. 15d. The voltage loss can be mainly classified into three parts, namely, anode overpotential from point A to B, membrane overpotential (ohmic loss) from B to C, and cathode overpotential from C to D, respectively. The cathode overpotential dominates the overall potential loss for the cell with a cathode ionomer ion conductivity of 25 mS cm−1. Also, it was found that increasing the ionomer conductivity from 25 mS cm−1 to 180 mS cm−1 (25, 50, 100, and 180 mS cm−1) not only leads to a much higher limiting current density (from ∼1.2 to ∼2.3 A cm−2), but also results in a five-fold increase in durability (from 500 h to 2500 h). The overpotential distribution profile along different cell components was further calculated and is illustrated in Fig. 15e. It was found that, with the cathode ionomer conductivity of 180 mS cm−1, the overpotential loss arising from the anode kinetics and the anode ionomer is negligible, while the overpotential from the cathode ionomer gradually increases and becomes significant as the operation time reaches 3000 h. This study demonstrates that the high ionic conductivity of the cathode ionomer benefits the catalyst utilization and reduces the internal resistance, thereby contributing to a higher cell limiting current density and overall durability. In addition to fuel cells, investigations of ionomers in AEM water electrolyzers for the design rationale have also been reported.179–184 One should note that the overpotential loss associated with oxygen electrocatalysis dominates the overall overpotential of the cell, as has been increasingly demonstrated in AEMWEs and AEMFCs.178,184–186 To attain superior cell performance and durability, simultaneously studying and/or modeling the anode and cathode ionomers from the viewpoint of the whole cell need more research. To this end, an optimal ionomer design concept for high-performance AEMFCs and AEMWEs is discussed in Fig. 15f. First, ionomers should be designed with varied ionic conductivity at the anode and cathode, not only because the ORR/OER contributes considerably to the overpotential range, but also because of different mass transfer requirements. Second, with regard to anti-oxidation capability, ionomers tend to be oxidized in certain types of reactions (e.g., the HOR and OER) that are very likely to intensify the degradation. Kim and co-authors confirmed the oxidation of phenyl groups in a practical AEM water electrolyzer test when using biphenylene as an ionomer.180 The formation of phenol induced by phenyl oxidation is likely to occur at the catalyst–ionomer interface, where the phenol would stick on the interface, decrease the local pH, slow the reaction kinetics, and adversely impact the whole electrolyzer performance. Third, targeting the complicated and varied water requirements on the anode and cathode for AEMFCs, the ionomers for AEMFCs should be designed with different water-withstanding capabilities to avoid dry-off and water flooding. Fourth, delicate experimental designs and computational simulations are needed to provide a comprehensive analysis on specific parameters of ionomer (e.g., ion conductivity, weight ratio to catalyst, etc.) for attaining a trade-off between high performance and long durability for AEMWEs and AEMFCs.
Fig. 15 (a) Schematic illustration of the role of the ionomer in building a triple-phase boundary (TPB). (b) Detailed description of the role of the ionomer in the TPB. (c) The linear correlation between AEMFC performance (peak power density) and the calculated phenyl adsorption energy on the ionomer backbone structure employed on the anode side. Reproduced with permission from ref. 174 Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (d) The simulated cell overpotential profile across the membrane electrode assembly as a function of the position. (e) The modeled overpotential distribution versus cell operation time and a schematic illustration of the overpotential loss across the cell components. Reproduced with permission from ref. 178 Copyright 2022 Elsevier. (f) Proposed ionomer design concept for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells.
4.2.2. Advances on ionomers for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. We next discuss the recent studies on ionomers and the catalyst–ionomer interaction under the premise of the employment of non-PGM-based electrocatalysts for applications in AEMFCs and AEMWEs. As we mentioned earlier, one of the critical advantages of using AEM devices is their compatibility with non-PGM catalysts; hence, developing ionomers targeting non-PGM catalyst environments for AEMFCs and AEMWEs is of great significance. Mamlouk et al. employed NiCo2O4 as a cathode electrocatalyst and concluded that a robust catalyst–ionomer interface, a suitable catalyst loading amount, and strong binding properties are key factors for developing a high-performance AEM water electrolyzer.187 Later, Kim and co-authors reviewed the previous ionomer design criteria for AEM water electrolyzers and proposed an ionomer fabrication strategy.188 In Fig. 16a, trimethyl ammonium functionalized polystyrene ionomers (TMA-x, where x denotes the molar percentage of quaternized benzyl ammonium) were synthesized. From the ionomer structure, it was found that the phenyl groups and long non-ionic alkyl chains were not included in the polymer backbone, which would minimize the risk of phenyl adsorption and maintain suitable polymer hydrophilicity. Meanwhile, the phenyl groups in the polymer side chains were replaced with ammonium and/or amine groups to further reduce phenyl adsorption and maintain high pH conditions. As shown in Fig. 16b, the performances of assembled MEAs with TMA ionomers, IrO2 as anode catalyst, and PtRu/C as cathode catalyst are displayed. For the PGM-based catalysts, the most suitable ionomer content is found to be 9 wt%. In the meantime, a higher current density for an MEA is obtained with increasing amounts of quaternized benzyl ammonium in the TMA ionomer. Notably, an MEA employing TMA-70 as an ionomer exhibited a current density of 1,360 mA cm−2 at a potential of 1.8 V, which is 1.7-fold higher than that of the TMA-53-bonded MEA. In Fig. 16c, the polarization curves of the non-PGM-bonded MEAs are presented. Changing the cathode catalyst from NiMo alloy to PtRu alloy for NiFe anode-catalysed MEAs results in an improved current density (twofold) and so do changes in the feeding electrolyte from pure water to 0.1 M and again to 1 M NaOH solution. Particularly for NiFe anode-catalysed MEAs fed with 1 M NaOH solution, the current density at 1.5 V potential (in the kinetics region) is more than two times greater compared with PEM MEA with IrO2/Pt as the anode/cathode catalyst fed with pure water. Moreover, the authors demonstrated that the MEA employing TMA-53 as the ionomer exhibited remarkably enhanced durability at 60 °C in comparison with TMA-70 because the reduced binding strength of TMA-70 cannot sustain a stable catalyst–ionomer interface for a long period of operation. This work proves that very competent performance of non-PGM catalysed AEM water electrolyzer vs. PEM water electrolyzer can be attained through careful architecture of the ionomer structure, ionomer content, feeding electrolyte, and catalyst–ionomer interaction. In addition to this, Faid and co-authors employed Ni–MoO2 as the cathode catalyst in AEMWEs and emphasized how the cationic ions (species and concentration) from the electrolyte and/or ionomer affect the catalytic performance.189 Specifically, the authors found that increasing the K+ concentration usually leads to a negatively shifted potential of zero free charge (pzfc) value, thereby reducing the interfacial electrical field, softening the double-layer, and consequently resulting in faster OH− transport kinetics as well as enhanced HER activity. Moreover, the use of benzyltrimethylammonium hydroxide (BTMAOH) in comparison to tetramethylammonium hydroxide (TMAOH) causes a rather strong electrode interaction between the electrode surface and the benzyl groups, where the mobile QA+ would form a compact double layer at the interface, blocking the shuttling of OH− and inhibiting the HER kinetics. This work highlights the importance of rational selection of ionomer cationic groups in affecting the performance of AEMWEs. Apart from the catalyst–ionomer interaction, the architecture of the anode and/or cathode layer of AEM devices also faces many challenges associated with the conventional fabrication route, such as (1) inhomogeneous catalyst ionomer distribution and low catalyst utilization; (2) inefficient electron transfer networks arising from low ionomer–catalyst adhesion; and (3) sluggish mass transfer due to the dense and thick catalyst layer, particularly for the anode layer, which has consequently inspired studies of unified and/or integrated electrodes in AEM devices.190–192 As shown in Fig. 16d, an illustration of an integrated electrode (IE) for AEMWEs proposed by Wang et al. is presented, in which an ultrasonic spray of ammonia poly(N-methyl-piperidine-co-p-terphenyl) (QAPPT) ionomer into a self-supported FeNi layered double hydroxide (LDH) anode was employed.192 Benefiting from the accelerated OH− and electron transfer kinetics, improved AEMWE performance is shown in Fig. 16e. Specifically, the pure water-fed AEMWEs with the use of 20 wt% ionomer delivered the highest current density (1900 mA cm−2) at a voltage of 1.9 V, which was 2 times that of the IrO2-catalysed AEM (950 mA cm−2), again manifesting the high necessity of rational design of the ionomer content for achieving balanced mass/charge transfer capability, surface wettability, aerophobicity, etc. More importantly, the IE and the ultrathin ionomer avoid the need for an interface between the catalyst layer and the liquid/gas diffusion layer, thereby improving the transfer of electrons, hydroxide ions, and gas/liquid, thus contributing to optimal performance of the AEMWEs. The ion exchange capacity (IEC) of the membrane and ionomer before and after 170 h of operation is presented in Fig. 16e, in which a ∼37% IEC loss is observed for the ionomer compared with a negligible loss for the membrane. The ex situ IEC test for the ionomer and membrane clarifies the origin of the degradation of AEMWE performance, which will guide the rational design of high-performance ionomers, electrodes, and AEMWEs. In the case of AEMFCs, the carefully designed architecture of the electrode structure, including ionomer content, catalyst–ionomer interaction, etc. is also of importance for the activity and durability of the cell performance.176,193–195 In particular, by simultaneously tuning the ionomer and electrode, an exceptional high-performance AEMFC was achieved by Mustain et al.196 Based on previous studies, Mustain's group proposed to synthesize three tetrablock poly(norbornene) ionomers (GT32, GT64, and GT78) with tuneable IEC, molecular weight, dispersity, and water uptake, followed by coupling with symmetric and asymmetric ionomers in the electrode to screen the optimal ionomer properties and suitable electrode configuration. The IECs and molecular weights of GT32, GT64, and GT78 were 1.88 meq g−1/114.9 kg mol−1, 3.37 meq g−1/50.96 kg mol−1, and 3.78 meq g−1/103.6 kg mol−1, respectively, through control of the amount of quaternary ammonium groups. Similarly, the water uptake (WU) of GT32, GT64, and GT78 was measured to be 63%, 90%, and 163%, respectively. By incorporating each of the ionomers with the anode and cathode (symmetric configuration) in the AEMFC, the authors found that the GT32- and GT78-based AEMFCs generally exhibited better peak power density, while the GT64-based AEMFC showed very fast decay, which was highly related to its lowest hydroxide mobility. Therefore, the requirements of the anode and cathode on the ionomer are very likely different, so GT32 and GT78 were further assembled asymmetrically in AEMFCs and tested as shown in Fig. 16g and i. Obviously, in Fig. 16g, the cell with GT78 as an anode ionomer and GT32 as a cathode ionomer delivered better PPD, while the other cell configuration (GT32 as anode ionomer and GT78 as cathode ionomer) suffered from ohmic losses and mass transport losses, indicating that the higher water uptake and IEC of GT78 could benefit the cell performance via promoting water back diffusion to the membrane and to the anode gas. As shown in Fig. 16h, changing the cathode ionomer from GT32 to GT78 led to reduced cell performance at a higher current density (>4 A cm−2), which can be attributed to the insufficient water as the reactant at the cathode due to too fast water distribution for the GT78 ionomer. Consequently, adding back pressure could allow additional water content to be maintained for the reaction by suppressing convection evaporation, contributing to a remarkable PPD of 2.7 W cm−2. In addition to the electrode configuration, the back pressure and types of gas diffusion electrodes were further studied to elucidate their influences on the cell performance. As a result, it was found that the cathode prefers a more hydrophobic status than the AEM to hold enough water at the membrane/catalyst layer interface as well as avoiding too much water throughout the catalyst layer, which might lead to flooding. With this optimal configuration (GT78 as the anode and GT32 as the cathode), the cell delivered a 2000 h durability at 600 mA cm−2 with very minor degradation (15.36 μV h−1) (Fig. 16i). These studies demonstrate that AEM devices with high performance in their activity and durability can be achieved through carefully designing the ionomer, catalyst–ionomer interaction, electrode configuration, back pressure, etc., which will shed light on the rational design of ionomers and beyond.
Fig. 16 (a) Molecular structure and ion exchange capacity of the TMA ionomer. (b) Polarization curves of MEAs employing various TMAs as ionomers. (c) Polarization curves of an AEM electrolyzer employed with different catalysts under corresponding operating conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 188 Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. (d) Schematic illustration of an integrated electrode (IE). (e) Polarization curves of pure-water-fed AEMWEs containing IEs with different ionomer contents. (f) IEC tests of 20 wt% IE before and after durability test, respectively. Reproduced with permission from ref. 192 Copyright 2022 John Wiley and Sons. (g) Polarization curves and peak power density profiles of AEMFCs employing GT32 and GT78 as ionomers. (h) Polarization curves and peak power density profiles of AEMFCs employing GT32 and GT78 as ionomers without and with 200 kPa backpressure (BP). (i) Cell durability test with GT32 and GT78 as ionomers under corresponding conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 196 Copyright 2020 John Wiley and Sons.

4.3. Electrocatalysts for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

4.3.1. Earth-abundant hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysts. As illustrated in Fig. 17a, two types of fundamental reactions are involved in AEMWEs (HER and OER) and AEMFCs (HOR and ORR), respectively. Currently, there are many reports on AEMWECs/AEMFCs employing noble metals/noble metal oxides (e.g., Pt, Pd, Ir, IrO2) as hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysts to achieve comparable a power density to that in PEMWEs/PEMFCs.197,198 They to some degree overlooked that one of the biggest benefits of using AEMWEs and AEMFCs is their capability to utilize non-noble-metal-based catalysts; therefore, the study of non-noble based electrocatalysts is rather significant and represents a great advance toward the practical deployment of these devices. Meanwhile, the costly noble metals and unsteady metal exports and supply would inevitably impede their long-term application.199,200 In principle, the development of advanced catalysts is highly dependent on the mechanical understanding of hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysis chemistry.201–203 Unfortunately, an accurate understanding of the reaction kinetics of the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, together with the specific role of cations and ions in these reactions, poses a great challenge for researchers.204–208 To this end, we follow three steps to present a practical catalyst development concept, including a clear understanding of the electrochemical processes of the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, the establishment of a universal activity descriptor, and the construction of catalyst discovery scheme, as depicted in Fig. 17b. Given that the HER and HOR are reversible processes in hydrogen electrocatalysis, the HER and HOR will be discussed together to give a clear understanding of the chemistry of hydrogen electrocatalysis as well as the activity descriptor in the HER/HOR. Meanwhile, an outline of oxygen electrocatalysis chemistry and the activity descriptor for the OER/ORR will also be given. In terms of the scaling relationship, Sabatier's principle suggests that an optimal catalyst should possess moderate, neither too strong nor too weak, binding energy toward reaction intermediates (e.g., atoms, molecules, etc.), and this correlation is normally plotted as a function of the Gibbs free energy that is usually obtained by means of theoretical calculations (Fig. 17c).209,210 To make it quantitative and effective to guide the catalyst design on the experimental scale, the scaling relationship can be further replotted as a function of bulk physiochemical features such as the degree of 3d filling, eg electrons, electronegativity, etc.211 Although the bulk feature cannot provide very precise relations with intermediates as the surface properties do during adsorption/desorption, it offers a chance to explore the underlying mechanisms, which can be examined by theoretical simulations. In this regard, finding the scientific principles of hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysis, understanding the chemical reaction descriptor, and the screening of efficient catalysts are of pivotal importance. For the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, we focus more attention on non-noble-metal-based electrocatalysts composed of earth-abundant elements to summarize their theoretical and experimental advances, accompanied by many fascinating strategies, including defect engineering, single-atom confinement, heterostructure building, etc. (Fig. 17d).
Fig. 17 (a) Polarization curves and potential range for hydrogen (HER/HOR) and oxygen (OER/ORR) electrocatalyses. (b) The development scheme for low-cost and high-performance electrocatalysts. (c) Scaling relationship translation for hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalytic reactions, from a linear relation to a volcano relation. (d) Electrocatalyst engineering strategies.
4.3.2. Chemistry of hydrogen electrocatalysis and HER/HOR activity descriptor. HER/HOR processes are two-electron-coupled electrode reactions involved in water electrolysis and H2–O2 fuel cells. The alkaline HER/HOR mechanism is a combination of three elementary steps: the Volmer step, the Heyrovsky step, and/or the Tafel step (eqn (4.8), (4.9) and (4.10)).
 
Volmer step: H2O + e− H* + OH−(4.8)
 
Heyrovsky step: H2O + e− + H* H2 + OH−(4.9)
 
Tafel step: H* + H* H2,(4.10)
where H* represents an adsorbed H atom (Had) on the surface active site, and the alkaline HER proceeds via the Volmer–Heyrovsky or Volmer–Tafel step, while the HOR follows the Tafel–Volmer or Heyrovsky–Volmer mechanism. The catalyst design approaches as well as the discovery of optimal catalysts are highly dependent on having an in-depth understanding of the fundamental HER/HOR process. Deciphering the scientific issues for the pH-dependent HER/HOR kinetics as well as finding a universal catalytic activity descriptor, therefore, is significant for the further development of high-performance electrocatalysts. Fig. 18 gives a clear overview of the key stages during the development of the HER/HOR activity descriptor. The pioneering studies simply concentrated on the HER branch, and the researchers extracted a linear relationship between the HER exchange current density on monometallic surfaces and the metal–hydrogen binding energy, thereby generating a volcano plot (Fig. 18a).212,213 The volcano plot suggests that the ideal HER catalysts should possess balanced hydrogen binding energies for the Had adsorption and desorption, which fits well with the Sabatier principle.214 Later, with the assistance of DFT calculations, Nørskov et al. obtained a similar volcano curve of the hydrogen chemisorption energies on metal surfaces and experimental exchange currents, which demonstrated the correlation between the hydrogen binding energy and the HER exchange current.215 Since then, the metal–hydrogen binding energy (HBE) is widely accepted as an HER activity descriptor for a variety of metals and alloys.215–217 When the electrolyte changed from acidic to alkaline, slower HER kinetics by 2–3 orders of magnitude was observed for a wide range of electrocatalysts (PGMs) irrespective of their HBEs, implying that the alkaline HER kinetics might be impacted by other parameters.218,219 In view of this, Yan et al. ascribed the decreased HER/HOR kinetics to the stronger hydrogen binding energy in an alkaline electrolyte, which is associated with the sluggish Volmer step in the HER/HOR.220–222 Notably, the HBE here proposed by Yan et al. is an experimental value, which correlates with the peak potential of the oxidation of underpotential-deposited hydrogen (HUPD) (Fig. 18b). The pH-dependent kinetics of Pt, Ir, and Pd electrodes are all linearly related to the position of the HUPD desorption peak, directly proving the correctness of the HBE concept. From the derived equation (eqn (4.11)):
 
HBE or Emetal-H = –Epeak × F,(4.11)
where F is the Faraday constant and Epeak is the peak potential of the oxidation of HUPD. The energy difference could vary by 12.5–13.5 kJ mol−1 from pH = 0 to pH = 13, and the acid/alkaline reaction rate ratio (γacid/γalkaline) is in the range of 120 to 200, which is in agreement with the experimentally observed reaction difference.223 Later, Yan et al. proposed a refined HBE theory and claimed that it is the apparent Gibbs free energy of hydrogen adsorption, ΔGH,app, rather than hydrogen adsorption ΔGH, that is correlated with the HUPD desorption peak.219 According to Yan et al. (Fig. 18c), ΔGH,app can be calculated using the equation ΔGH,app = ΔGH − ΔGH2O, where ΔGH and ΔGH2O are the intrinsic Gibbs free energy and the Gibbs free energy of water adsorption, respectively. In the refined HBE concept, changing the electrolyte (in going from acids to bases) simply weakens ΔGH2O, while ΔGH remains constant. Consequently, ΔGH,app increases with increasing electrolyte pH and matches the shifted HUPD desorption peak. On the other hand, recent molecular dynamics modeling on Pt(100) that was based on quantum mechanics also demonstrated the weakened water adsorption under high pH conditions, in agreement with the refined HBE.224 The inconsistency in the HBE or the refined HBE concept, however, is that the pH-dependent HBE trend is not observed for the Pt(111) electrode.225 In addition, the good HER activity but unsatisfactory HOR activity observed for MoS2 contradicts the idea that the HBE should control the HER and HOR in a similar way.228,229 These results manifest that the HBE or refined HBE might not be a universal descriptor. Later, it was found that the HOR/HER activity can be enhanced by decorating Pt(111) with oxophilic metals (e.g., Ni, Co, Ru, etc.).226,230,231 Accordingly, Markovic's group proposed that both Had and OHad species are reactant intermediates, which is also known as bifunctional theory (Fig. 18d). In terms of the HER, the incorporation of oxophilic species promotes water dissociation with the generation of Had, which accelerates the Volmer step and contributes to the enhanced overall reaction. With regards to the HOR, the oxophilic species promote the adsorption of reactive OHad, which is vital for the Heyrovsky or Volmer step. In general, the overall HER/HOR kinetics is rationalized by the balance between water dissociation, Had, and OHad adsorption. Considering that the multiple adsorption intermediates are greatly impacted by the catalyst composition and surface structure, the researchers constructed single-crystal electrodes with controlled Had binding strength and fine-tuned OHad binding strength, and further found that the HER activity is correlated with the hydrogen and hydroxide binding strength.232 Apart from the bifunctional theory, the potential of the zero (free) charge (pzfc) theory, the 2B (bifunctional and hard acid soft base (HASB)) concept, etc. have emerged as an HER/HOR activity descriptor (Fig. 18e).225,227,233–239 So far, the aforementioned assumptions do describe the HER/HOR activity somehow, but the underlying science and inconsistencies remain to be addressed in further investigations. More direct observations and dynamical monitoring of the multiple reactant species (e.g., OHad, H2O, AM+, etc.) during the reaction, and the determination of the explicit role of additives might benefit the construction of a universal descriptor in the future.
Fig. 18 Overview of the development of hydrogen electrocatalysis descriptors. (a) Hydrogen binding energy theory proposed by Nørskov et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 215 Copyright 2005 IOP Publishing. (b) Hydrogen binding energy theory derived from experimental results and proposed by Yan et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 220 Copyright 2016 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (c) The refined hydrogen binding energy proposed by Yan et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 219 Copyright 2018 IOP Publishing. (d) The bifunctional theory proposed by Markovic et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 226 Copyright 2012 Springer Nature. (e) The newly developed pzfc and 2B proposals by Jia et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 227 Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society.
4.3.3. Chemistry of oxygen electrocatalysis and OER/ORR activity descriptor. The oxygen evolution and oxidation are two reversible reactions coupled with the four-proton-electron transfer processes that take place at the anode and cathode of AEMWEs and AEMFCs, respectively. The overall reaction equation of the OER/ORR under alkaline media can be written as (eqn (4.12)):
 
OER/ORR: 4OH− ↔ 4e− + 2H2O + O2,(4.12)
where the overall equation can be classified into four elementary steps (eqn (4.13), (4.14), (4.15) and (4.16)):
 
OH− ↔ OH* + e−(4.13)
 
OH* + OH− ↔ H2O + O* + e−(4.14)
 
O* + OH− ↔ OOH* + e−(4.15)
 
OOH* + OH− ↔ H2O + O2 + e−.(4.16)
Here, the OH*, O*, and OOH* are reactant intermediates and * represents a surface-active site. From the viewpoint of transition state theory (Fig. 19a), the reaction starts from the initial state and then goes to the transition state before ending with a final state.240 During the transition state, electrochemical free energy is required to drive the reaction to pass through each reaction pathway. Here, the presence of a catalyst can significantly reduce the reaction energy barrier through the interaction between the reaction intermediates and the catalyst active sites. Unlike the HER/HOR reaction with only one intermediate (H*), the three intermediates (Fig. 19b) in the OER/ORR are strongly correlated, which makes it rather challenging to construct scaling relations. Furthermore, the very complicated surface compositions and chemical states of each category of electrocatalyst intensify the difficulty in building a universal OER/ORR activity descriptor. Benefiting from our comprehension of oxygen electrocatalysis chemistry and advanced characterization techniques, the OER/ORR activity scaling relations on metals, metal single atoms (graphene-based support), metal oxides, perovskite oxides, etc. have almost been established, which will be discussed in detail. For metals and metal alloys, Nørskov and co-authors employed DFT-based calculations and calculated the Gibbs free energy of two key intermediates (O and OH) on the surface of Pt(111).241,242 Furthermore, this model was further extended to calculate the binding energies of O*, OH*, and OOH* for a range of other metals, where a volcano correlation between the calculated binding energy of O* and the ORR reactivity was obtained (Fig. 19c). Moreover, the relationship between ΔGOOH* and ΔGOH* was depicted as ΔGOOH* = ΔGOH* + 3.2 ± 0.2 eV (Fig. 19d), which can be found to apply universally to both close-packed (111) and open-packed (100) facets of metals and their alloys. Metal single atoms have achieved significant success in the past several years in the OER/ORR field, owing to their high catalytic activity and economic viability for practical applications.243–245 Similar to single-atom catalysts (SACs) in the HER/HOR, the tremendous research on SACs for the OER/ORR has also stimulated researchers to explore the underlying structure–activity relationships. Based on this, Zeng's group summarized the behavior of many transition metal single atoms on graphene and derived a scaling relation of adsorption free energies ΔGOH*, as shown in Fig. 19e–f.246 After a critical analysis of the adsorption energy, the authors employed more realistic concepts (valence electrons in the occupied d orbital of the metal element (θd) and electronegativity (E)) to predict, screen, and guide the rational design of new catalysts.246
Fig. 19 (a) Transition state theory for illustrating the distinctive role of a catalyst in decreasing reaction barriers for a catalytic reaction. Reproduced with permission from ref. 240 Copyright 2015 RSC Publishing. (b) Gibbs free energy diagram for the oxygen evolution and reduction reactions. (c) ORR volcano plot for metals. (d) ORR theoretical limiting potential plot for face-centred cubic (fcc) (111) and (100) facets of metals and alloys. Reproduced with permission from ref. 242 Copyright 2017 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (e) Scatter plot of the adsorption free energy of OH versus the descriptor φ for all single transition metal (TM) atoms supported on macrocyclic molecules. (f) Scatter plot of adsorption free energy of H versus the descriptor φ for all single TM atoms supported on macrocyclic molecules. Reproduced with permission from ref. 246 Copyright 2018 Springer Nature.
4.3.4. Recent progress on the earth-abundant hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysts. Although the alkaline HER/HOR activity descriptor is debatable, a consensus has been reached that the interfacial water molecules and Had/OHad play an unavoidable role in the HER/HOR processes.225,227,229,239,247–249 Also, this is true for studying the OER/ORR activity descriptors. To this end, a series of typical metals, metal single atoms, metal compounds (e.g., oxides, dichalcogenides, phosphides, etc.), metal alloys, metal-based heterostructures, etc. will be thoroughly covered and compared. On the one hand, the explicit role of small molecules, ions, and species (e.g., Had, OHad) in the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, as well as a better understanding of the related innovative mechanisms will be elucidated. On the other hand, some excellent catalyst design strategies and a range of easily scaled catalyst manufacturing methods for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells will be discussed.
4.3.4.1. Single-atom catalysts (SACs) for HER/HOR and OER/ORR. Single-atom catalysts (SACs) have achieved significant progress in heterogeneous catalysis, such as CO oxidation, the water–gas shift reaction, electrocatalytic reactions, etc., owing to their efficiency in full atom utilization and excellent activity.243,250–253 As shown in Fig. 20a, the three important compositions in the SACs are the metal central ions, the surrounding ligands, and the host materials.250,254,255 By changing the central metal atoms, the coordination environmental, and/or the host materials, the catalyst properties (e.g., activity, selectivity, and stability) can be modulated accordingly. A typical example is that researchers mainly concentrated on oxide hosts for the earlier SACs (e.g., FeOx, CeO2, or TiO2) because of their strong chemical stability in thermal catalytic reactions (e.g., CO oxidation).256,257 Later, carbon-based materials were widely selected as hosts, stemming from their high electronic conductivity, good resistance to acids/bases, and abundant coordination environment.258–260 Since then, carbon-based SACs have been widely reported with excellent activities for HER, HOR, OER, ORR, etc.250,261Fig. 20b gives some good examples of SACs for HER from the C3–Ni bonding to Ni–C6 configuration,262,263 and then to Ni–N3–O2 coordination,264,265 following the tuning concept of coordination chemistry. On the other hand, non-carbon based supports (e.g., metal carbides, metal oxides, metal hydroxides, etc.) also emerged to build SACs to boost electrochemical performances.266–277 The metal atoms in these compounds might induce structural distortions and charge redistributions, which are expected to bring vacancies, phase transformations, or strains that trigger a considerable improvement in catalytic properties. Ru-doped Ni5P4 (Ni5P4–Ru) was verified with the formation of Ru–P/Ru–Ni bonds (Fig. 20b), and electrons were transferred from Ni/P to Ru, leading to enhanced HER activity because water adsorption is more favorable on Ni5P4–Ru (Ru top sites) than on bare Ni5P4 (Ni top sites).268 Introducing Co and Ni atoms into MoSe2 would substitute them for Mo atoms in the basal plane and give rise to the formation of Co–MoSe2 and Ni–MoSe2.278 Interestingly, compared with Ni–MoSe2, Co–MoSe2 possesses similar adsorption energy towards H2O and Had due to the minor difference in ΔGH2O and ΔGH, but much reduced HER kinetics, in turn demonstrating that OHad adsorption and OH− desorption are enhanced on Ni–MoSe2 and are critical to the alkaline HER process. Modulations of the local atomic configuration, defect engineering, support effects, etc. are commonly employed approaches that have been demonstrated to be effective.279–281 It is worth noting that these typical SACs reported with decent HER activity have not been actively studied in the HOR, and/or their HOR activity is unsatisfactory, even though HER/HOR is a reversible reaction, which poses a question that the activity descriptor of the HER/HOR for SACs has not been identified. To this end, Li's group employed atomic Ni to manipulate the electronic structure of Ru nanocrystals and discussed its influence on HOR activity.274 In RuNi1 nanocrystals (NCs), the Ru–Ni bonding in RuNi1 suggests that the electronic structure of Ru is well-tuned with the incorporation of atomically dispersed Ni. As expected, the HOR mass activity of RuNi1 (2.7 A mg−1) at 50 mV overpotential is much higher than those of the Ru–Ni bimetallic alloy (0.427 A mg−1) and Pt/C (0.426 A mg−1) (Fig. 20c). In Fig. 20d, DFT-based calculations demonstrate that the incorporation of atomic Ni could significantly reduce the hydrogen adsorption energy, particularly when compared with the pure Ru catalyst. Moreover, the energy barrier of the Volmer rate-determining step (rds) was decreased from 1.067 to 0.966 eV, which also boosted the fast HOR kinetics. These studies suggest the inevitable role of Had and OHad in the alkaline HER/HOR, although typical single atom configurations with HER and HOR that can be both carefully and comparatively investigated to examine the intermediates are very few. Pacchioni and co-authors proposed a new model to explain the activity descriptor in the HER.282 Based on the assumption that SACs can be considered as surface analogues of organometallic complexes, Nørskov's theory here is not well suited to the intermediate model of SACs, where the dihydrogen and/or dihydride complexes are easily formed. In contrast, the adsorptions of both the first H atom (ΔG0H(1)) and the second H atom (ΔG0H(2)) need to be considered because HMH dihydride complexes (M is a metal) are usually formed and observed on a series of single atoms including Pt atoms on N–Gr (where a carbon atom of the graphene (Gr) lattice is replaced by a N atom), MoS2, defect-rich graphene, etc.283 Consequently, the HER kinetics of SACs is highly associated with ΔG0H(1) and ΔG0H(2), implying that moderate adsorption energies towards H1 and H2 need to be satisfied simultaneously to attain an ideal HER activity, as shown in Fig. 20e. The exchange current as a function of ΔG0H(1) and ΔG0H(2) gives rise to the formation of a three–dimensional volcano plot in Fig. 20f. Taking metal single atoms anchored on N-doped graphene (M@N-Gr) as an example, their HER kinetics predicted by MH intermediate theory (Nørskov's theory, Fig. 20g, I) and the two-intermediate model (Fig. 20g, II) is illustrated. Here, i0/imax is defined as the descriptor, where i0 denotes the exchange current and imax is the maximum exchange current. Apparently, according to the two-intermediate model, V, Cr, Co, Ni, Cu, and Pd do not exhibit good HER activity as they did in the MH intermediate theory. In conclusion, this work reconsiders the rationale of Nørskov's theory in the determination of the HER activity. The pH-dependent kinetics of SACs and the rationality of this concept in the prediction of HER/HOR activity remain to be answered in future studies. SACs have also achieved tremendous progress in oxygen electrocatalysis owing to their many fascinating features, among which, Fe–N4 has been demonstrated to be the most promising PGM-free catalyst to substitute for Pt or Pt-based alloys, given its relatively high activity and four-electron selectivity towards the ORR.284–287 The current reported ORR activity of Fe/N/C catalysts (0.02 A cm−2@0.9 ViR-free), however, is far behind the 2025 target (0.44 A cm−2@0.9 ViR-free) for fuel cell technologies set by U.S. Department of Energy (DOE) to achieve the economic viability of fuel cells in light-duty vehicles.286 Based on the previous studies, the critical challenges for SACs in the ORR lie in their low electrochemical site density (ECSD) and inferior durability, which result in unsatisfactory power/energy density and very limited lifespan.288–290 Given that the dynamical reaction process and very complicated electron-transfer steps involve oxygen-related species, improving our mechanical understanding of oxygen electrocatalytic chemistry and designing high-performance ORR catalysts are still major tasks in the next stage. As mentioned in Fig. 20a, the ORR activity of SACs is mainly determined by the metal center, host, and ligand. Aside from the judicious choice of metal centers, ligand manipulations of SACs were widely reported. Most of the earlier reported metal–N4 single atom configuration relates to metal atoms that are bonded by four pyridinic N atoms,259,291,292 while Wu et al. demonstrated that pyrrolic-type Fe–N4 coordination can also boost the ORR activity and achieve good fuel cell performance.293 Following this concept, Chen and co-authors designed nitrogen and phosphorus dual-coordinated iron active sites (Fe–N3P) and confirmed that more efficient O2 molecule adsorption and *O2 dissociation to *OOH can be achieved in contrast to the normal Fe–N4 model (Fig. 21a), thus significantly improving their catalytic performance in zinc-air batteries compared to the Pt/C benchmark.294 Meanwhile, Ma's group employed the concept of “Janus materials” and synthesized a dual single atomic site composed of Ni–N4 and Fe–N4 on the inner and outer walls of graphene hollow nanospheres (GHSs).295 Wang and co-workers reported single-site Ru cation coordination to manipulate the local ligand environments and the electronic structure via fabricating an Ru/LiCoO2 SAC with atomically dispersed Ru–Co pair sites (Fig. 21a).296 Many other interesting works in terms of metal center tuning and ligand tuning in SACs have been published.284,297–301 In this regard, an atomic-level understanding of the catalytic mechanism of the ORR might provide some new thoughts. As described in Fig. 21b, Liu and co-authors studied the mechanism of Fe–N4 from the viewpoint of electron spin.302 Specifically, the authors synthesized Fe single atom catalysts and identified that the Fe–N4 moieties can be classified into Fe–N4C12 (D1), Fe–N4C10 (D2), and N–Fe–N4C10 (D3), respectively, by using 57Fe Mössbauer spectra. Based on the values of the isomer shift (IS) and quadrupole splitting (QS), the spin state of Fe2+ in D1, D2, and D3 were assigned to low, intermediate, and high spin, respectively. The high correlation between the ORR mass activities of single-Fe-atom catalysts and the D3 moiety content strongly suggests that N–Fe–N4C10 is the critical component for high ORR activity. In the initial stage (polarized at 0.9 V), the operando57Fe Mössbauer spectra and Raman spectra confirmed that the D3 content was decreased with an increase in the relative D1 content, indicating O2 adsorption on D3 sites and the generation of O2−–FeN5 intermediate. During this process, the spin state of Fe2+ in D3 evolves from high spin to low spin, accompanied by a decreased isomer shift (IS) from 0.77 to 0.39 mm s−1. When polarized at 0.7 V, the D3 content was increased with a reduction in the relative D1 content, manifesting O2 adsorption on D1 sites and the formation of O2−–FeIIN4 intermediate. Meanwhile, both QS and IS showed an increase from 0.80 to 2.46 mm s−1 and 0.37 to 0.77 mm s−1, respectively, suggesting that the Fe2+ spin state in D1 changed from low spin to high spin, with Fe2+ moving out of the N4 square plane. Polarization at a higher bias (0.5 V) resulted in a continuously increasing content of O2−–FeIIN4 intermediate, again proving the dynamic ORR activity of D1 at higher biasing. It has to be noted that the preferential adsorption of O2 molecules on D3 near the onset potential demonstrates that N–Fe–N4C10 is a more active site towards the ORR in alkaline media. A fully dynamic ORR cycle on N–Fe–N4C10 moieties is presented in Fig. 21b. The O2 molecule adsorption on N–Fe–N4C10 sites stimulates the movement of the central Fe atom partially and completely to the N4-plane with the formation of OH− and *O2 intermediates, respectively. The interaction between the Fe 3d orbital (dxz) and side-on coordinated O2 π* orbital promotes the d-orbital splitting and results in the conversion of high spin Fe2+ to low spin Fe2+. The DFT results further confirmed that the energy barrier of orbital interaction between D3 and O2 is remarkably lower than that in the case of D1 and D2. On the other hand, Yu and co-authors, for the first time, depicted the relationship between the single atomic inter-site distance (dsite) of Fe–N4 and its ORR activity.303 By carefully controlling the metal loading content, Fe SAs with inter-site distances ranging from ∼7.8 to ∼0.5 nm were generated. Fig. 21c gives four typical EXAFS spectra of Fe–N4 samples, and the dsite of each sample estimated from STEM images is presented. Given that surface interrogation scanning electrochemical microscopy (SI-SECM) has been proved to offer insight into the first O2 adsorption kinetics on transition metal sites, the rate constant (k′) for oxygen binding on Fe(II)–N4 as a function of dsite was calculated via SI-SECM and is shown in Fig. 21d. A sudden jump appeared at the dsite values between 1.2 and 0.7 nm in the plot, which indicates a remarkable improvement of the oxygen activation activity, which is also in agreement with the TOF values. Furthermore, the authors deepened the study and employed 57Fe Mössbauer spectra and found that the catalytic activity of Fe–N4 was correlated with FeN4C12 (D1) and FeN4C10 (D2). The ORR activity difference of dsite can be ascribed to the difference in the ratio of D1 to D2, originating from the Fe spin states. As the two Fe atoms become closer, the energy level of the Fe dz2 orbital is downshifted, resulting in a higher degree of filling of the partially occupied d orbitals and eventually leading to weakened bonding strength of the over-strongly adsorbed hydroxyl intermediates. The dsite-dependent ΔG*OH plot in Fig. 21e acquired from DFT calculations demonstrates this interpretation. Also, the derived volcano plot of ORR overpotentials against ΔG*OH provides a new avenue to design and prepare single atom catalysts with reasonable metal loading. In contrast to SACs in the ORR, the fundamental challenges of SACs in the OER mainly originate from the unstable structural stability and inferior activity induced by ultrahigh overpotential. Even though carbonaceous materials possess many competitive features in electrocatalysis, their susceptibility to corrosion in the OER potential range in alkaline media would intensify catalyst dissolution,304,305 which calls for the development of other support materials such as metal oxides, hydroxides, etc., to improve the overall structural stability of SACs. To this end, Thomas et al. employed transition metal carbides (TMCs) as supports to design SACs because of their very robust hardness, thermal stability, chemical corrosion resistance, and high electrical conductivity. Fig. 22a presents a schematic image of the WCx-FeNi catalyst with the Fe and Ni atoms immobilized on the WCx support. The HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding intensity profile along the (−12−1) plane (Fig. 22b and c) further validate the existence of Ni and Fe single atoms, which was demonstrated by the weak bonding (Ni–W and Fe–W). As expected, WCx-FeNi exhibited 237 mV overpotential towards the OER at 10 mA cm−2, and this value further decreased to 211 mV when the atomic loading content increased from 0.38 to 1.14 mg cm−2. The reaction pathways are illustrated in Fig. 22d, where the adsorption free energy values toward OH* and O* on WCx-FeNi and WCx-FeNi–OH (surface oxidized) are reduced. Apart from the support engineering, Lu and co-authors synthesized a Co1/TaS2 catalyst where Co SAs were confined in the TaS2 monolayer, and they discussed the OER activity from the viewpoint of electron spin-density.306 The intercalated Co SAs were found to occupy the high-spin (HS) sites (bonding at the hollow site, CoHS) and Ta sites (Co replacing Ta atoms, CoTa), although the CoHS sites were calculated to have smaller formation energy. The CoHS and CoTa sites correspond to the Co2+ and Co3+ species, and the Co1/TaS2 catalyst with tunable CoHS and CoTa sites was obtained by controlling the content of Co SAs. DFT calculations revealed that the CoHS sites have much reduced energy barrier than CoTa sites and the pristine TaS2 surface, so they serve as the real OER active centers. In Fig. 22e, the adjacent CoTa sites were validated to favor the formation of O*, while the neighboring CoHS sites result in a larger energy barrier from OOH* to O2. Furthermore, the CoHS–CoTa sites were calculated to have a medium magnetic moment (2.100 μB) for the CoHS atom, which consequently benefits the formation of CoHS–O single bonding and leads to optimal O* adsorption/desorption as well as excellent OER activity and durability. As shown in Fig. 22f, the correlation between the OER activity and the Co spin density value of the Co1/TaS2 catalyst as a function of the doped Co content is established. These studies of SACs anchored on non-carbon-based supports for the alkaline OER,307–309 open an avenue to diversify the single-atom catalyst design strategy and strengthen the activity and durability of such catalysts toward the alkaline OER via support engineering, electron spin state, etc., which also provides insights, knowledge, and principles for the followed catalyst design.310,311 Based on the aforementioned studies, Li et al. employed a series of metal single atoms (Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Ag, and Mo) that immobilized on the carbon support and aimed to propose a universal thought in the determination of HER/HOR activity. It was found that changing the metal centers would result in a different water adsorption behavior as well as OH binding energy. Hence, the authors concluded that the hybridization between M 3d and O 2p orbitals might be a dominant reason for the varied species adsorption and HER/HOR activities. For Mn–N–C, Mn t52ge0g, with an unoccupied eg state, it is easier to possess strong overlaps with O 2p orbitals and hence higher adsorption capability. For Cu–N–C, Cu t62ge4g, the full eg orbitals of Cu lead to negligible H2O/OH adsorption (Fig. 22g). This thought is based on the analysis of crystal field theory and square pyramidal geometry for the configurations (Pt–O–M–N4) in this study.249,312–314 For other metal atomic configurations, crystal field theory with different geometries and molecular orbital theory may be employed to clarify the hybridization between eg orbitals of metal centers and O2p orbitals, and beyond. For oxygen electrocatalysis, readers are suggested to access several excellent review articles to have a detailed study of the activity descriptors in MN4 metal complexes for the ORR.246,315,316 The metal single atoms immobilized on non-carbon-based supports and the activity descriptors for the OER need to be further studied.

4.3.4.2. Transition metal-based heterostructures for the HER/HOR and OER/ORR. Heterostructure materials have attracted increasing research attention because of their fascinating physicochemical properties based on the hybridization of two or more components.25,318–323 By incorporating distinct compositions, the heterostructures can be altered to achieve balanced adsorption of reactant intermediates, as well as acquiring decent HOR/HER and OER/ORR activity.321,323 Specifically, the combination of two or more components normally generates an interface accompanied by a local electrostatic potential perturbation, where the strength of the interaction between components is closely associated with the type of perturbation. The generated heteroatom bonds, the induced strain effects, and the derived electronic interactions across the interface are the prominent aspects for optimization of the electronic structures of heterostructure materials as well as their enhanced adsorption capability toward reaction intermediates, leading to improved electrocatalytic reaction rates. Therefore, in this section, we concentrate on the recent progress on a variety of metal-based heterostructures encompassing carbon-based, non-carbon-based, and metal-based components that are active towards alkaline hydrogen and oxygen electrolyses, with a particular emphasis on the electronic modulation approaches as well as interpretations of the mechanism. As mentioned earlier, carbonaceous materials usually serve as catalyst supports owing to their large specific surface area, high electronic conductivity, strong stability in acidic/alkaline media, ready availability, etc.324–326 Immobilizing metals, metal oxides, metal sulfides, etc. on carbon-based supports is a widely employed strategy, because the stability is enhanced and the spatial distribution can be controlled.327 Meanwhile, the high electronic conductivity of carbon promotes the charge transfer kinetics in the heterostructure materials. Moreover, the generated interface would give rise to interesting metal-support interactions, which might exert profound effects on the electronic structure of the catalysts as well as the adsorption of the reactant intermediates.328,329 One important study that illustrates how the Ni–carbon heterointerface contributes to the enhanced HOR activity compared to pure Ni metal was reported by Yan's group in 2016.330 In this work, the Ni particles (∼20 nm) were immobilized on N-doped carbon nanotubes (Ni/N-CNT) with a loading content of around 70 wt%. Ni particles supported on CNTs (Ni/CNT) and pure Ni particles were also synthesized for comparison. The N-CNT support exerts geometrical effects that tend to decrease the strong hydrogen binding energy sites with Ni, while, on the other hand, the electronic interaction between the edge N atoms and Ni causes a further upshift of the d-band center of Ni and activates their adjacent sites for the HOR, which contribute synergistically to the improved HOR activity. Following this concept, a large number of unique metal–carbon heterointerfaces were tailored and generated by strain engineering, geometrical engineering, and incorporation of heteroatoms in the carbon skeleton (e.g., B, P, S, etc.), which contribute to improved hydrogen electrocatalysis.331–348 The success in modulating metal–carbon heterointerfaces also inspired researchers to explore the support effects coming from non-carbon-based substrates, with transition metal dichalcogenides,349–352 hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN),353–356 MXenes,357–359 cerium(IV) oxide (CeO2),360–363 and titanium(IV) oxide (TiO2),364–366 as classical representatives. Lu and co-authors synthesized a unique LixNiO/Ni heterostructure via a Li metal-mediated redox reaction, shown in Fig. 23a.367 In the O 1s XPS spectra (Fig. 23b), a dramatic ∼2.0 eV positive shift is observed for LixNiO/Ni as compared with NiO or annealed NiO/Ni, implying that the incorporation of Li+ contributes to totally different O compositions. Likewise, the Ni 2p XPS spectra also exhibit a positive shift (∼2.0 eV), and the binding energy of Ni2+ changes to 855.9 eV, which is closely associated with the Ni defects and the unique O compositions. Moreover, the O 1s XANES spectra of LixNiO/Ni compared with NiO or NiO/Ni present a pronounced adsorption peak located at 534 eV, which can be ascribed to the strong Brønsted basic oxygen. Meanwhile, a dramatic pH change (from 6.60 to 8.67) is observed on dispersing 10 mg LixNiO/Ni into 3.0 mL water, in contrast to dispersing NiO/G and Ni/G, where G stands for graphene, demonstrating that the presence of the strong Brønsted basic oxygen species could promote the dissociation of H–OH bonds. As shown in Fig. 23c, LixNiO/Ni exhibits enhanced HER activity in alkaline media (pH = 13.8) compared with that in a neutral electrolyte (pH = 7.0) because of the significantly accelerated water dissociation step due to the presence of strong Brønsted basic oxygen species. Meanwhile, the HOR performance of LixNiO/Ni in 1 M KOH is also better than for most of the reported electrocatalysts, again demonstrating the capability of the unique LixNiO/Ni interface in boosting hydrogen electrocatalysis. Apart from the LixNiO/Ni interface, CeO2 was also employed to build Ni/CeO2 heterostructures by Yang and co-workers.368 In this work, a CeO2-decorated Ni hybrid with different Ni ratios was immobilized on carbon (XC-72) to obtain CeO2(r)–Ni/C. When used as an HOR catalyst, the CeO2(r)–Ni/C-1 (52.3% Ni content) exhibited the highest kinetic current density (12.28 mA mgNi−1) at 50 mV as well as the highest ECSA normalized exchange current density (0.038 mA cmNi−2). The role of abundant oxygen vacancies in the CeO2 support in the HOR was illustrated by XPS, Raman spectroscopy, and DFT-based calculations. In Fig. 23d, the decreased hydrogen adsorption energy for CeO2(r)–Ni (−0.065 eV) as compared with pristine Ni (−0.302 eV) validates the tremendous hydrogen adsorption on CeO2(r)–Ni sites after optimization. Moreover, an increased OH* adsorption was also obtained for CeO2(r)–Ni, as its adsorption energy for OH* is –0.924 eV, in contrast to –0.163 eV for pristine Ni. The electronic interaction between CeO2(r) and Ni modulates the electronic structure of Ni, endowing CeO2(r)–Ni with improved H* and OH* adsorption capability as well as enhanced HOR performance. Very recently, Stamenkovic et al. studied the HOR performance of Pt–RuO2 heterojunctions from the viewpoint of RDE and membrane electrode assembly (MEA) investigations.369 The Pt–RuO2 heterostructures were synthesized by annealing carbon-supported PtRu8 nanodendrites, and the scaled-up synthesis (1 g per batch) was successful. The HAADF-STEM and energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) images proved that the Pt clusters and atoms were uniformly distributed in RuO2. The HOR performance of the Pt–RuO2 heterostructures was evaluated in NaOH solution and benzyl trimethylammonium hydroxide (BTMAOH). When the electrolyte changed from 0.1 M NaOH to 0.1 M BTMAOH, there was a reduced HOR current on the Pt/C Tanaka Kikinzoku Kogyo (TKK) catalyst, while only a negligible difference in the kinetics could be found for Pt–RuO2. This demonstrates that Pt–RuO2 heterostructures are less likely to be poisoned by the phenyl groups in the BTMAOH solution compared with Pt/C, suggesting its strong practical feasibility in MEAs that contain phenyl groups. Notably, the Pt mass loading in Pt–RuO2 is only 3.5 wt% as opposed to 19.4% wt% for commercial Pt/C, signifying the excellent HOR performance of Pt–RuO2 heterostructures. Furthermore, the HOR performance tested under MEA conditions is compared in Fig. 23e. The peak power density of the MEAs when using the Pt–RuO2 anode catalyst reached 0.77 W cm−2, far beyond 0.28 and 0.55 W cm−2 for Pt/C and PtRu/C, respectively. Furthermore, the peak power density as a function of catalyst cost is illustrated in Fig. 23f, showing that the Pt–RuO2 heterostructure is a promising approach to meet the U.S. Department of Energy (DOE) fuel cell cost and performance target. Some other interesting studies on the elucidation of heterointerface effects in hydrogen electrocatalysis, including for Ni/Ni3N, Ru@RuO2/TiO2, Pt/Ni3N–Mo2C, etc., have been presented in detail, and the remarkable role of heterointerfaces in improving bonding configuration and species adsorption is demonstrated.370–372 In addition to hydrogen electrocatalysis, heterostructure engineering also plays an unavoidable role in oxygen electrocatalysis.202,354,373–381 Given the multiple reaction steps and sluggish reaction kinetics for oxygen electrocatalysis compared with hydrogen electrocatalysis, a classical catalyst design concept is to immobilize noble metals on support materials, which not only induces improved catalyst activity through strong electronic interaction, tensile or compressive strain, and confinement effects, but also results in enhanced structural stability. Based on this, Sun's group investigated the electronic effects of employing nitrogen-doped carbon, Ni(OH)2, LiCoO2, h-BN, and g-C3N4 as supports to immobilize a range of noble metals (e.g., Pt, Ir, Ru, etc.) and how the electronic effects affect oxygen evolution and reduction activity.320,354,382–384 It is interesting to find that, although Pt is not considered to exhibit oxygen evolution activity, Pt could promote the reduction of Co ions as well as the formation of O vacancies and under-coordinated Co centers, which results in weakened adsorption toward OH*/OOH* and contributes to higher oxygen evolution.320 On the other hand, h-BN and g-C3N4 both exhibit exceptional co-catalytic activity for Pd and Ir in the oxygen reduction and evolution reactions, respectively.354,382 h-BN not only increases the hydrophobicity of h-BN/Pd owing to its super hydrophobicity, which promotes gas transfer kinetics, but also induces downshifting of the d-band center of Pd due to strong compressive strain effects, thereby leading to weakened adsorption of O* as well as enhanced reaction kinetics. In-depth studies of h-BN and g-C3N4 as catalyst supports in building heterostructures have revealed their unique role in providing B, N, and C coordination environments, supplying surface wettability, and offering overall structural stability, which will yield a solid foundation for the design and construction of other heterointerfaces with wider applications. Unlike the role of h-BN in confining Pd, Dong and co-worker designed Pd/Mo2C interfaces by depositing β-Mo2C nanoparticles (NPs) into atomic layers of Pd (AL-Pd/Mo2C).385 As illustrated in Fig. 24a, the positively shifted Pd 3d peak manifests the strong interaction between Pd and Mo2C. Furthermore, the projected density of states (PDOS) of bulk Pd and AL-Pd/Mo2C shows a downshifted d-band center away from the Fermi level for AL-Pd/Mo2C. The strong electronic interaction of the Pd/Mo2C interface would result in weakened adsorption toward oxygen intermediates and faster ORR activity. Consequently, as shown in Fig. 24b and c, the half-wave potential of AL-Pd/Mo2C is 0.935 V, far better than that of most of the reported ORR electrocatalysts as well as commercial 20% Pt/C. In addition, disk and ring currents were measured, and the electron transfer number is calculated to be 3.9, suggesting that AL-Pd/Mo2C follows the four-electron reaction pathway to reduce O2 and that the H2O2 yield is negligible. Inspired by considerable work that employed noble metal species as active components in heterostructures, an increasing number of research studies are aimed at exploring the unique interface structures arising from non-noble metal species for oxygen electrocatalysis.386–388 To this end, Park et al. reported heterostructures comprising La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ and MoSe2 that induced a local phase transition from 2H MoSe2 to the 1T phase, thereby achieving exceptional stability of over 1000 h of operation at a current density of 100 mA cm−2.389 As shown in Fig. 24d, the electron transfer from Co to Mo is found to alter the electron configuration of Mo from the occupied 4dz2 level to the incompletely filled dxz, dyz, and dyx, to induce the phase transition as well as improve the electronic conductivity. Furthermore, the charge transfer also upshifts the d-band center of Co resulting in enhanced adsorption toward OH− as well as faster oxygen evolution kinetics (Fig. 24e). DFT calculations further confirm the electron transfer across the interface and the 0.1 eV upshift in the d-band center for Co in La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ. The exceptional durability of the La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ/MoSe2 heterostructure can be verified by the chronopotentiometric profiles in Fig. 24f, where negligible degradation in current density can be observed, even when captured at 100 mA cm−2 with an operation time of 1000 h. On the other hand, investigations of heterostructures comprising non-metal-based components in oxygen electrocatalysis have been paid particular attention to.390–392 In particular, Chen's group proposed employing carbon nanotubes (CNTs) and thienothiophene–pyrene covalent organic frameworks (COFs) to construct one-dimensional van der Waals heterostructures (1D vdWHs).392 As shown in Fig. 24g, a solution-based in situ wrapping was applied to assemble coaxial CNT/COF heterostructures, where the COF shell thickness could be controlled by changing the precursor mass ratio of COF to CNTs.
