(f) AEMFC peak power density comparison as a function of anode catalyst cost. Reproduced with permission from ref. 369 Copyright 2020 RSC Publishing.

Fig. 24 (a) XPS spectra of Pd 3d peaks for AL-Pd/Mo2C heterostructures and Pd NPs, and PDOS plots for Pd sites in AL-Pd/Mo2C and bulk Pd. (b) Mass and specific activities of AL-Pd/Mo2C towards the ORR at 0.9 V (vs. reversible hydrogen electrode (RHE)). (c) Rotating ring disk electrode (RRDE) polarization curves of AL-Pd/Mo2C and comparison between the disk current density (solid line) and the ring current density (dashed line). Reproduced with permission from ref. 385. Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society.(d and e) Schematic illustration of the charge transfer along the La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ/MoSe2 interface and the accelerated water splitting kinetics. (f) Chronopotentiometric stability profiles of La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ/MoSe2 measured at 100 mA cm−2 for 1000 h. Reproduced with permission from ref. 389. Copyright 2019 Springer Nature. (g) The fabrication process for the one-dimensional van der Waals heterostructures 2D-COFs/CNTs. (h) The calculated oxygen intermediate adsorption geometries. (i) Polarization curves towards the ORR and OER. Reproduced with permission from ref. 392. Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society.

The S-pentacyclic domains in the thienothiophene–pyrene COF and the strong π–π interactions in the heterointerface were found to play a critical role in oxygen electrocatalysis. On the one hand, the band gap of COF/CNT vdWHs was found to decrease with the reduction of COF shell thickness, promoting the charge transfer. On the other hand, the C-S moieties were calculated to be the solely catalytic active sites for initial O2 adsorption as well as OOH* adsorption. Specifically, as shown in Fig. 24h, the O2 activation at a C atom in the vicinity of an S atom, the OOH* adsorption at an S atom, and the O* adsorption at an S atom, etc. can be illustrated with the corresponding vibration peaks of S-, as evidenced by operando FTIR. In contrast, the pristine thienothiophene–pyrene COF was characterized with a band gap of 2.08 eV and no obvious change in the vibration bands, further indicating that the efficient band-gap reduction and optimal oxygen intermediate adsorption originate from the heterointerface between the thienothiophene–pyrene COF and the CNTs. Consequently, as shown in Fig. 24i, the half-wave potential of COF/CNT vdWHs (CC-3) is 0.828 V, and the transferred electrons of CC-3 were calculated to be ∼3.86, proving its excellent ORR activity. In addition, CC-3 also delivered the lowest overpotential of 389 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2, much better than that of the bare TAPTt-COF (prepared by self-assemblies of thienothiophene–pyrene without CNTs) and CNTs. The development of non-noble-metal-based heterostructures not only promotes the findings of unique interface effects in oxygen electrocatalysis but also advances AEM technologies.


4.3.4.3. Transition metal alloys for HER/HOR. Alloy engineering normally involves modifications of the electronic structure of the parent metal through ligand effects or strain effects by incorporation of a second or even more metal phases.393 The varied electronic structures of the parent metal would give rise to a shift of the d-band center in relation to the Fermi level, inducing a changed interaction between the metal and the adsorbed molecules according to the d-band center theory.393,394 Many catalysts with enhanced HER/HOR activity and durability can be obtained by changing the electronic structure through alloy strategies.395–404 On account of the question whether oxophilic effects play different roles in alkaline HER and HOR processes, Sun's group synthesized Pt3Ni/C and acid–Pt3Ni/C to rationally compare their Had and OHad affinities, which were reflected by their CO stripping test results and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves.405 Specifically, the Pt–OHad binding energies follow the sequence of Pt/C < acid–Pt3Ni/C < Pt3Ni/C, while the Pt–Had binding energies decrease in the order of Pt3Ni/C ≈ acid–Pt3Ni/C < Pt/C. Interestingly, the acid–Pt3Ni/C exhibits medium HER activity but delivers the best HOR activity among these three samples. The current densities at the overpotential of 50 mV (without the influence of H2 mass transfer) for Pt/C, acid–Pt3Ni/C, and Pt3Ni/C are 1.3, 6.2, and 8.3 mA cm−2, respectively, suggesting that the surface Ni species could boost the HER performance via adsorbed OHad. On the other hand, the HOR current densities (at 50 mV) for Pt/C, acid–Pt3Ni/C, and Pt3Ni/C are 1.5, 4.3, and 4.7 mA cm−2, respectively, and no apparent enhancement in the exchange current density can be observed for Pt3Ni/C as compared with acid–Pt3Ni/C, in turn demonstrating the slight effects of adsorbed OHad on the HOR. Analysis of the charge transfer kinetics based on electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) plots further confirms that the reaction mechanisms of the HER and HOR are different, which is reflected by their inequivalent transfer coefficients. For the HER process, the Ni species are used to cleave water molecules as well as to reduce the Pt–Had binding energy. With regards to the HOR, the role of Ni is to weaken the Pt–Had binding energies. The DFT calculations give hydrogen adsorption profiles as a function of the reactant intermediates in the HER/HOR process. The HER activity descriptor is reflected by the sum energy of H–OH cleaving and Had adsorption, which follows the order of Pt(111) (0.99 eV) < Pt3Ni(111)@Pt skin (0.96 eV) < Pt3Ni(111) (0.78 eV). The HOR process is merely determined by Had adsorption, where the sequence is Pt(111) (0.76 eV) < Pt3Ni(111) (0.64 eV) < Pt3Ni(111)@Pt skin (0.32 eV). This work illustrates how the oxophilic effects play different roles in the HER and HOR processes, and deepens our understanding of the HER/HOR steps although more direct evidence of the OHad behavior as a reactant intermediate is missing. In the meantime, Li's group employed in situ electrochemical surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy (SERS) to clarify the role of hydroxyl species (OHad) in the HOR process, which might provide evidence of the dynamical reaction kinetics.406 In order to increase the surface detection sensitivity, Au metal was employed to build Au as cores with Pt or PtNi serving as shells (Au@Pt NPs vs. Au@PtNi NPs) as the main object of study. The in situ SERS spectra as a function of voltage for Au@Pt and Au@PtNi in alkaline KOH/KOD (deuterium isotopic experiment) are presented in Fig. 25a. During the HOR reaction, no Raman signals can be observed for Au@Pt, which is very likely due to the weak adsorption of OH− on the Pt surface. With regards to Au@PtNi, the peak at 778 cm−1 emerged at the potential of 65 mV and increased to a maximum intensity at 165 mV before decreasing. In terms of Au@PtNi in KOD, the peak at 778 cm−1 shifted to ∼668 cm−1, indicating that the adsorbed species are most likely to be OHad, based on analysis of the mass conversion formula.407 Moreover, DFT-based calculations were adopted to find the adsorption energy of OHad on different sites.
Fig. 25 (a) In situ SERS spectra of Au@Pt and Au@PtNi surfaces in 0.1 M KOH, and Au@PtNi surface in 0.1 M DOH during the HOR process. (b) Illustration of the Volmer steps on pure Pt and Pt3Ni(110) surface. Reproduced with permission from ref. 406. Copyright 2021 John Wiley and Sons. (c) HER/HOR polarization curves and (d) PDOS profile for alloyed samples. Reproduced with permission from ref. 408. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. (e) The HOR polarization curves of Ru/C, Ni/C, Pt/C, PtRu/C, and Ru7Ni3/C, and (f) their fuel cell performance. Reproduced with permission from ref. 409. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. (g) UPS spectra of MoNi4, WNi4, and Ni. (h) Schematic illustration of Had and OHad adsorption on Mo(W)Ni4 alloys and a comparison of kinetics currents. Reproduced with permission from ref. 410. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature.

Of them, the adsorption of OHad on surface Ni–Ni bridge sites was calculated to feature the lowest adsorption energy. Furthermore, the co-adsorbed configuration of OHad was simulated to give the O–H rocking mode at a frequency of 774 cm−1, which matches well with the experimentally observed peak at 778 cm−1, suggesting that OHad might be another HOR intermediate species. The Gibbs free energies of Had and OHad species were also obtained by DFT calculations to clarify the HOR process. As described in Fig. 25b, for the pure Pt surface, the HOR process is energetically unfavorable due to the unavailability of sites for OHad. For the PtNi surface, however, the oxophilic Ni facilitates the adsorption of OHad and enhances the reaction rate. Moreover, the authors also inferred from electrochemical SERS that the Pt–H binding energy is identical for Au@Pt and Au@PtNi, in turn confirming the definite role of OHad in the HOR process. This is the first report that elucidates the dynamical electrochemical process for the HOR by using in–situ techniques, although the explicit role of OHad in the alkaline HER process remains to be determined. Wang and co-authors discussed the alloying effects of Ru with different metals (Co, Ni, and Fe) and demonstrated their activity enhancement mechanisms for the HER/HOR and the OER/ORR.408 With regards to hydrogen electrolysis, the specific activities and mass activities of Ru0.95Co0.05/C, Ru0.95Fe0.05/C, and Ru0.95Ni0.05/C are much higher in contrast to Ru/C and Pt/C, with Ru0.95Co0.05/C being the best (Fig. 25c). As illustrated in Fig. 25d, the d-band center of RuCo decreases with the increasing ratio of Co in the alloy, leading to weakened Had adsorption as well as enhanced HER/HOR activity.411 The recent investigations of metal-based alloys have further promoted the development of high-performance electrocatalysts in AEMWEs and AEMFCs.409,410,412 Zhuang and co-authors fabricated the RuNi alloy, and the mass activity of Ru7Ni3/C was 21 times that of Pt/C.409 Remarkably, Ru7Ni3/C exhibited a high peak power density of 2.03 W cm−2 in an H2/O2 hydroxide exchange membrane fuel cell at 95 °C, outperforming the PtRu/C anode catalyst (Fig. 25e and f). According to the refined HBE theory, the authors ascribed the highly enhanced HOR activity to the optimized hydrogen binding energy and increased water adsorption. The Ru–H binding is weakened after the addition of Ni, which was reflected by the negatively-shifted electrochemical in situ attenuated total reflection surface-enhanced infrared absorption spectroscopy (ATR–SEIRAS).413 Furthermore, the O–H stretching vibration peak derived from ATR–SEIRAS within the HOR potential range (−0.15 to 0.2 V) validates the improved water adsorption ability on Ru7Ni3/C, which is closely associated with the surface oxidized Ni species.414 Very recently, Yu's group fabricated Ni-based alloys and discussed the role of hydrogen and hydroxyl species in activity enhancement.410 As determined by extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) analysis, heteroatom bonds (Mo–Ni) are generated within the MoNi4 alloy. The incorporation of Mo modulates the electronic band structure of the MoNi4 alloy, which is reflected in the ultraviolet photoelectron spectroscopy (UPS) spectra (Fig. 25g). Compared with pristine Ni metal, the d-band center of WNi4 and MoNi4 is downshifted away from the Fermi level, implying that the metal–H antibonding state above the Fermi level is increased, resulting in weakened Had adsorption.415 Apart from the electronic effects induced by the metal alloy, the oxophilic effects are also discussed, based on the CO stripping test. The CO stripping peaks of WNi4 and MoNi4 are negatively shifted in contrast to pristine Ni metal and Pt/C, manifesting the strengthened OHad binding energy. In Fig. 25h, DFT calculations also illustrate the remarkably enhanced hydroxyl adsorption and the greatly decreased hydrogen adsorption on MoNi4, which both contributed to the boosted HOR activity. Importantly, MoNi4 exhibited a geometrical current density j0 of 3.41 mA cm−2 and an ECSA normalized j0 of 0.065 mA cm−2, which is far beyond the performance of reported non-PGM based catalysts.


4.3.4.4. Transition metal-based oxides/(oxy)hydroxides for the OER/ORR. Metal oxides/(oxy)hydroxides have been considered promising electrocatalysts for the OER because of their robust structural stability, strong corrosion resistance, and decent intrinsic activity. The most promising category, NiFe oxides (i.e., NiFeOxHy), has long been employed as a family of benchmark OER catalysts given their exceptional activity and economic competitiveness, especially in contrast to noble metal oxides (e.g., RuO2, IrO2, etc.).416 Although the activity of NiFe oxides is well established, the nature of active sites and their working principles remain debatable. Meanwhile, the recent catalyst design approaches involving metal oxides for the alkaline OER concentrate on foreign-metal doping and binary or ternary metal oxide/(oxy)hydroxide construction, and numerous classical catalysts have been reported with very high activity.417–419 To this end, the major objective for researchers is to elucidate the fundamental mechanism behind the OER process on metal oxides/(oxy)hydroxides, find out the catalyst design principle, and further improve the catalysts’ performance toward the alkaline OER. It has to be noted that the OER is a dynamical process with applied potential and that complex compositions, morphological changes, surface reconstruction, etc. intensify the difficulty in probing the authentic active sites. Inspired by this, Chueh and co-authors employed operando scanning probe and X-ray microscopy techniques to establish the structure–activity correlation in the oxygen evolution reaction by selecting a single-crystalline β-Co(OH)2 platelet.420 Based on combining an electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) with electrochemical atomic force microscopy (EC-AFM), the height and mass difference of a single β-Co(OH)2 platelet could be determined as a function of applied potentials and are displayed in Fig. 26a. The particle height increased approximately 10% between the two applied potentials ( and ) and contracted back to its initial dimensions at . Meanwhile, the mass of the single β-Co(OH)2 platelet gradually decreased with and . According to the EQCM measurements, the structural changes associated with applied potential are shown in Fig. 26b, where the first redox transition (0.9 to 1.36 V) corresponds to hydroxide intercalation (from Co(OH)2 to α-CoO2H1.6·0.5H2O) and the second redox reaction at implies the removal of interlayer water and protons, and the formation of β-CoOOH. Furthermore, the author employed operando scanning transmission X-ray microscopy (STXM) to probe the Co oxidation state in the potential range of the OER (Fig. 26c), and the X-ray absorbance spectrum (XAS) with the corresponding Co oxidation state fitting is shown in Fig. 26d. The number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction was derived to be ∼0.54 ± 0.04 e− at (∼1.14 V) and ∼0.46 ± 0.04 e− at (∼1.58 V), respectively, in agreement with the redox peaks (Fig. 26c) and EC-AFM and EQCM results (Fig. 26a). Hence, the average oxidation states of Co were determined to be Co2.54+ at and Co3+ at . Based on the analysis of operando STXM data and microkinetic modeling,422 the Co3+ (β-CoOOH) sites are very likely to be the active sites of the rate-determining step for the OER. This work provides a fundamental viewpoint to establish the relationship between the OER rate-determining step (RDS) and the electronic structure of a single crystal by using advanced techniques (EC-AFM and EQCM). In addition to this work, Markovic et al. aimed to provide a catalyst design scheme from the viewpoint of activity–stability and address the metal dissolution issues over the OER potential range.421 By systematically comparing the OER activity between MOxHy (M = Fe, Co, Ni) and Fe-containing MOxHy clusters (Fe–MOxHy), the activity trend toward alkaline OER (Fe–NiOxHy > Fe–CoOxHy > FeOxHy) was derived, where the OER activity was found to correlate with the surface Fe average adsorption free energy on MOxHy sites (ΔGFe–M), and the stronger interaction of MOxHy with Fe usually leads to sufficient active sites as well as enhanced stability. The OER activity enhancement for Fe incorporated 3d transition-metal hydr(oxy)oxides is shown in Fig. 26e, in which Co, Ni, and Cu could significantly improve the Fe adsorption energy owing to weakened M–O bonding. Furthermore, doping on the Ni hydr(oxy)oxide with other 3d transition metals (Co, Ni, and Cu) was confirmed to improve the Fe adsorption energy and Fe coverage, consequently leading to better OER activity (Fig. 26e). This trend was further demonstrated by the linear relationship between OER activity and Fe surface coverage on NiM (Cu, Co, Mn) and Ni hydr(oxy)oxides. At the same time, the synergistic effects between Fe and Ni species for Fe-doped NiOOH ((NixFey)OOH) in the oxygen evolution reaction have been elucidated due to their exceptional catalytic activity. Goddard and co-authors adopted quantum mechanics (QM) methods (hybrid DFT and grand canonical-QM) to find that the surface high spin d4 Fe(IV) site contributes to the formation of active O radical species; meanwhile, the surface closed-shell d6 Ni(IV) sites serve as active centers for O–O coupling. These two synergistic steps result in a highly improved alkaline OER rate for (NixFey)OOH, which is in agreement with the experimental results.423 Following this concept, the author screened the OER activity of Fe-doped NiOOH ((NixFey)OOH) by replacing Fe with 17 other transition metals and predicted Co-, Rh-, and Ir-doped NiOOH to be efficient OER electrocatalysts because they promote the formation of the key intermediate (O radical species).424 Aside from theoretical investigations, Cao's group developed a universal strategy through ligand-induced conversion on 3d-transition-metal (hydro)oxides and successfully synthesized a series of metal oxides and metal LDHs with highly improved OER performances (e.g., NiCo LDH-terephthalic acid (TPA), 267 mV overpotential).425 Given the strong bonding between *OOH species and 3d block metals, this strategy was intended to weaken the intermediate bonding by shifting the d-band center of 3d block metals, which was achieved by organic ligand transformation (e.g., replacing a certain amount of OH− by terephthalic acid). Accordingly, the downshifted d orbitals of Co and the decreased Co 3d–O 2p orbital energy gap were validated by DOS calculations and consequently led to optimized *OOH bonding and improved OER activity. On the other hand, Liu's group developed a ball milling method to introduce tensile strength into the NiFe hydroxide lattice to tune the anti-bonding orbital filling and oxygenated intermediate adsorption, as well as OER activity.426 Apart from the fabrication of transition metal hydr(oxy)oxides at the laboratory scale, Yu et al. for the first time proposed a scalable method to synthesize amorphous ternary metal oxides and studied their catalytic properties towards the OER.417 Based on the facile supersaturated co-precipitation method, the authors employed metal-containing precursors and synthesized a range of ternary metal oxides up to 515 grams with high quality (e.g., CoFeMo, MnFeMo, and CuFeMo oxides). As demonstrated, the amorphous catalysts underwent a self-reconstruction process to generate metal oxy(hydroxides) with O-vacancies during the OER, in which the O-vacancies were confirmed to lower the adsorption energy toward *OOH, consequently leading to excellent OER activity. Recently reported excellent studies particularly focused on 3d block transition metal hydr(oxy)oxides, as can be seen from the following excellent reviews.207,427–429 Please note that surface reconstruction for metal hydr(oxy)oxides and many other metal compounds was found to heavily affect the catalyst structure and the authentic catalytic active sites especially for the OER. The studies on the surface reconstruction during the OER and the coping strategies have been systematically outlined by several review papers.207,430–432 Notably, Gao et al. emphasized the fundamental understanding of the mechanism of the surface reconstruction and summarized the strategies to tailor the reconstruction (e.g., doping, morphology tuning, vacancy, etc.) and characterization techniques for transition metal-based OER catalysts.430
Fig. 26 (a) The tested heights (top) and the EC-AFM current and isothermal (T = 25 °C) mass change (measured by EQCM) for a β-Co(OH)2 particle. (b) Illustration of the expansion and contraction of individual CoO2 layers with increasing voltage. (c) Voltage-dependent particle-averaged Co LIII-edge STXM-XAS spectra. (d) The Co oxidation state as a function of voltage. (e) Cyclic voltammogram (in 0.1 M KOH) of β-Co(OH)2 in an STXM electrochemical flow cell at a scan rate of 10 mV s−1 and a flow rate of 30 μl min−1. Reproduced with permission from ref. 420. Copyright 2021 Springer Nature. (f) OER activity enhancement trend for 3d transition-metal hydr(oxy)oxides incorporated with Fe. (g) OER activity of Ni (left) and NiCu (right) hydr(oxy)oxides in pure KOH and KOH containing 0.1 ppm Fe. (h) Linear correlation between the absolute OER activity of NiM (Cu, Co, Mn) and Ni hydr(oxy)oxides and average Fe coverage. Reproduced with permission from ref. 421. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature.

4.4. Gas diffusion layers and bipolar plates for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

Gas diffusion layers (GDLs) and bipolar plates (BPs) are another two critical components to build low-cost high-performance AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. As illustrated in Fig. 7a, the GDL is usually composed of a microporous layer (MPL) and a substrate, and is in direct contact with the inner catalyst layer and the outer BP. Meanwhile, the membrane, catalyst layer, and GDL are sandwiched by BPs, and the BPs act as a shield and provide strong mechanical support to the single cell. In addition, BPs serve as protective layers to allow the chemical reaction to take place smoothly while transporting water, gas, heat, electricity, etc. A schematic illustration of a commercially available bipolar plate and the zoomed GDL structure is presented in Fig. 27a. It is believed that the properties of GDL and BP in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells lead to very similar requirements to those of their PEM counterparts.433–436 Herein, the primary requirement on BPs in AEM devices is also to offer higher electrical and thermal conductivity to reduce the potential loss and internal heating, high mechanical strength to withstand the pressure in the cell stack, robust anti-corrosion capability to prolong the working lifetime, lower gas permeability to minimize the gas crossover, etc.433,437 In addition to this, the material density and the cost are another two factors that influence the overall function of the AEM devices. For instance, the density of the material for PEM BPs is suggested to be lower than 5 g cm−3, and the materials plus fabrication cost is believed to be lower than $0.0045 cm−2 to improve their portability and economic competitiveness.438,439 One major difference for the BPs in AEM and PEM water electrolyzers is that the BPs in AEM usually face fewer corrosion issues due to the working electrolyte; hence, the materials of the BPs and the coating layer in AEM device have a wider choice and lower production cost. For GDLs, their main function is to allow the reactants (e.g., water, gas, etc.) to pass through to the catalyst layer and, equally, let the products flow away efficiently. On top of this, higher electrical conductivity, mechanical strength, and anti-corrosion capability are desirable to improve the cell potential loss, structural stability of the membrane electrode assembly, and cell durability. The properties of the BP and GDL for AEM devices are displayed in Fig. 27b, and we next discuss how to make rational material designs for BPs and GDLs based on the requirements mentioned. There have been increasing research reports on BPs for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells,440,441 but there has been very little in-depth exploration on how the employment of various materials and/or flow channel designs determine the cell performance. In the case of the BPs in PEM fuel cells, the selection of different raw materials, ranging from graphite and metals (e.g., Al, Ti, Ni, etc.) to composites (e.g., stainless steel, carbon composites) has been comparatively studied.439 Furthermore, a variety of modification strategies, including changing the filler content and incorporation of protective coating layers to improve the conductivity and anti-corrosion capability of the BPs, have been widely adopted,433,442–444 which in turn indicates that relevant studies on the AEM devices are urgently needed. Moreover, the different operation conditions between AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells also call for comprehensive studies on the BPs to respectively develop ideal ones for the two devices. A typical example is that the BPs of AEM water electrolyzer anodes tend to suffer from severe corrosion in comparison to their counterparts in AEM fuel cells because the potential experienced at the anode can be often higher than 2.0 V. As shown in Fig. 27c, three respective flow channel designs, i.e., 1-channel serpentine, 5-channel serpentine, and parallel, are depicted. The flow channel transition from 1-channel serpentine to multipath serpentine reduces the flow resistivity and favors a high flow rate (Fig. 27c).445 Continuing this concept, parallel channels and modifications were accordingly proposed to reduce the hydraulic resistivity.446,447 A schematic illustration of a flow field channel from a PEM BP is shown in Fig. 27d to provide fundamental insight on how to achieve efficient BPs in AEM devices through the rational design of channel parameters. Therefore, from the viewpoint of experimental design and computational modeling, finding optimal channel parameters such as channel depth, flank angle, pitch distance, etc. for AEM devices is urgently needed. In addition to the basic channel parameters, a common problem of BPs is the increased interfacial contact resistance (ICR), which originates from the generated metal oxidation layer at the surface of BPs after a certain period of operation. In particular, the anodic film formation is rather severe because the potential is usually higher than +1.5 V vs. RHE. Targeting this problem, Proch et al. studied a series of coating materials used in the BPs for AEM fuel cells and explored how the coating layer helps to reduce interfacial contact resistance.448 The ICR was calculated through an established setup, and the BP surface compositions were detected by glow-discharge optical emission spectroscopy (GD-OES), the results of which are presented in Fig. 27e. Optical images of the surfaces of BPs and the ICR were found to show no difference before and after exposure to aqueous sodium hydroxide with pH 11 and pH 14.5. Furthermore, the GD-OES profile tested under the conditions of pH 11 showed that C and O signals were mostly dominant, which is almost the same as with the pristine one. When the pH of the electrolyte was changed to 14.5, the GD-OES profile exhibited an increased O signal, indicating obvious carbon oxidation. Interestingly, the ICR remained almost unchanged, even with severe carbon oxidation, suggesting that the carbon coating could well inhibit the generation of metal oxides. This work paves the way for the future design and development of robust BPs for AEM fuel cells and water electrolyzers. Next, we consider three classical experimental factors (i.e., pore structure, hydrophobic/hydrophilic conditions, and thickness) for which a GDL will be evaluated to study its performance in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, respectively. For a macroporous layer (MPL), Razmjooei and co-authors proposed to use a Ni-based MPL to further improve the performance of AEM water electrolyzers.450 Specifically, the Ni-based MPLs were synthesized by using the air-plasma spraying (APS) technique, in which a very porous Ni-based alloy layer with the thickness of ∼80 μm was deposited on a metallic porous transport layer (PTL), thus forming a Ni-based MPL. As shown in Fig. 28a, the total pore volume of the NiMPL-PTL was 11.29 mm3 g−1, and the pore size ranged from 0.1 to 90 μm, which are far better than those of an uncoated PTL (total volume of 0.85 mm3 g−1 with pore size ranging from 0.01 to 0.1 nm). The highly improved porous structure of NiMPL-PTL promoted gas and water transportation, leading to enhanced cell performance (Fig. 28b). The AEM water electrolyzer with NiMPL-PTL precious metal catalysts (Ir and Pt/C) delivered the lowest voltage (1.9 V) at a current density of 500 mA cm−2, which is much lower than that of the cell (2.19 V) employing a normal PTL and identical catalysts as well as of the cell using a commercial Ni mesh as the PTL. On the other hand, NiMPL-PTL also enables the reduction of interfacial contact resistance (ICR), which is another reason for the improved cell performance. Nyquist plots of cells with PTL/PTL and NiMPL-PTL/NiMPL-PTL, captured at the current density of 500 mA cm−2, are presented in Fig. 28b. The internal and mass transfer resistances of the NiMPL-PTL/NiMPL-PTL cell are both far lower than for the PTL/PTL cell, again manifesting that NiMPL-PTL could efficiently reduce the ICR and promote gas removal. In addition, the complicated water management for AEM fuel cells in comparison with their PEM counterparts makes the tuning of the hydrophobic conditions of the GDL rather significant and challenging. For this purpose, Yang et al. investigated the effects of the hydrophobic conditions of the gas diffusion layer on the AEM fuel cell performance through modulation of the amount of polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) within the GDL.451 In this work, two gas-diffusion substrates (GDSs: carbon paper functionalized with/without PTFE) and three gas-diffusion layers (GDLs, GDSs further coated with a MPL) were prepared and characterized accordingly. The pore size distributions (PSD) of the five samples are given in Fig. 28c, where GDS-0 shows a maximum distribution of the pore diameters at 33 μm when compared with GDS-30 and GDL-30. In addition, the intensity of the maximum distribution for GDL-30 decreases in comparison with GDS-30, indicating that the incorporation of MPL has occupied the pores on GDS and hence leads to reduced maximum distribution intensity. Furthermore, the porosity (%), air permeability, through-plane resistance, and mean pore diameter for GDS-0, GDS-30, GDL-0, GDL-10, and GDL-30 were calculated. The air permeability of GDL-0, GDL-10, and GDL-30 gradually increases while the through-plane resistance of the former two remains nearly identical (5.45 vs. 5.43 mΩ cm−2), and the latter is increased to 11.9 mΩ cm−2, suggesting that a thicker PTFE layer in the GDS results in better gas transfer capability but also causes higher resistance. The polarization curves and power density profiles of the cells assembled with different GDLs are presented in Fig. 28d. Specifically, the cell voltage and power density profiles of the cells show the trend of GDL-0/GDL-0 < GDL-40/GDL-40 < GDL-10/GDL-10 < GDL-30/GDL-30. This indicates that too high a PTFE content in the GDL does not necessarily lead to higher cell performance and that 30% PTFE functionalization on the GDL benefits water transport more than 10% PTFE does, which contributes significantly to cell performance. In addition to the two strategies, tuning the thickness of the GDL is another method to improve cell performance, because the thickness of the GDL would greatly affect the electrical conduction, the passage of reactants/products, and the framework of the cell. As illustrated in Fig. 28e, Zhang and co-authors employed a novel liquid/gas diffusion layer (LGDL) with much-reduced thickness and tunable pore morphologies.452 Compared with the conventional titanium foam GDL (e.g., mesh, foam, felts, etc.) with a thickness of ∼260 μm, the bare thin/tunable liquid/gas diffusion layers (TTLGDLs) feature a thickness of approximately 50 μm. Meanwhile, a ∼80 nm-thick gold layer was electroplated on the surface of TTLGDL (surface-coated TTLGDL (SC-TTLGDL)). The average pore size of TTLGDL was found to be 300 μm, which is far better than the commercial Ti foam GDL (∼30 μm). Consequently, an AEM water electrolyzer with the SC-TTLGDL delivered much enhanced current density compared with an SC-Ti foam-based cell at the same overpotential, whether operated under 0.1 M KOH electrolyte or deionized water (Fig. 28f), suggesting that the highly porous and thinner LGDL promotes mass diffusion and interfacial contact. Moreover, the decreased thickness of LGDL also helps to build a cell stack with reduced weight, product cost, and volume. On this basis, more in-depth studies on the GDL from the perspectives of pore size, hydrophobic conditions, and thickness are still needed to find the ideal elements for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells.

(c) Illustration of different types of flow channels in the BPs in PEMFCs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 449. (d) Cross-sectional view of the flow channel and the critical geometrical parameters. Reproduced with permission from ref. 439. Copyright 2020 Elsevier. (e) Optical images of stainless-steel grade 304L BPs of AEMFCs exposed to three electrolyte conditions and the GD-OES profiles. Reproduced with permission from ref. 448. Copyright 2020 Elsevier.

Fig. 28 (a) 3D structure of NiMPL-PTL and its pore size distribution profile. (b) Polarization curves of AEMWEs with the use of different cell configurations and impedance spectroscopy curves, with the equivalent circuit in the inset. Reproduced with permission from ref. 450. 4.5. Membrane electrode assembly (MEA)

The membrane, ionomer, electrocatalyst, gas diffusion layer, and bipolar plate have been carefully summarized, and now we move on to the assembly of these key fragments to understand the fabrication process for a single cell and cell stacks. A key step is the construction of the membrane electrode assembly (MEA), which is usually manufactured by two different pathways: catalyst-coated substrate (CCS) and catalyst-coated membrane (CCM).94,453 In the catalyst-coated substrate (CCS) the catalyst ink needs to be dispersed on the gas-diffusion layer to form a gas-diffusion electrode (GDE).454 MEA is then generated by hot-pressing a single layer of AEM sandwiched between two GDEs.455 In sharp contrast, the catalyst-coated membrane (CCM) is fabricated by hot-pressing a single layer of AEM sandwiched between two catalyst layers (CLs).94 Generally, the CCM-based MEA possesses a higher catalyst utilization efficiency and electrochemical reaction rate than the CCS-based MEA because the reaction that occurs on the carbon paper for the CCS-based MEA is a more lengthy process.93,456 Meanwhile, the studies of CCM-based MEAs in PEM fuel cells demonstrated that this type of MEA could efficiently minimize noble metal loading, improve cell power density, and increase overall performance.93,456,457 The employment of CCM-based MEAs would also benefit the catalyst loading and cell performance, which has also been validated in several studies on AEM fuel cells.457,458 On the other hand, the process of fabricating CCM is rather complicated, and obtaining high-quality MEAs is quite challenging because of the partial dissolution of the membrane with the catalyst ink solvent, especially for membrane thickness lower than 20 μm. In addition, membrane creep behavior was found to be present in CCM and reduce the durability of the membrane.459 Critically, the comparisons between CCS-based MEAs and CCM-based MEAs under the premise of employing non-noble-metal-based electrocatalysts still need to be studied to further clarify the most suitable MEA fabrication methods and the compatible non-noble-metal-based catalysts. Here, the manufacturing approach for the single cell and cell stacks based on the CCM-based MEA is presented in Fig. 29. After the fabrication of the CCM and the gas-diffusion layer (GDL), a 5-layer MEA is formed by hot-pressing CCM sandwiched between two layers of GDL. Gaskets and seals are usually assembled with the 5-layer MEA to avoid any gas and/or liquid leakage. Finally, a 7-layer MEA is generated for further assembly with the bipolar plate and end plate before obtaining a single cell as well as cell stacks.
Fig. 29 Manufacturing flow process for membrane electrode assembly and cell stacks for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. (a) Catalyst coated membrane, (b) gas-diffusion layer, (c) a single cell, and (d) cell stacks for AEMWEs and AEMFCs.

4.6. Electrochemical evaluation and state-of-the-art performance of AEM devices

4.6.1. Electrochemical evaluation. Electrochemical evaluation is the first and foremost step to determine if the cell components have been properly assembled and how well the cell is operated. In the past few decades, tremendous studies have concentrated on catalyst development and optimization, and most of the performance has been assessed in the three-electrode configuration (TEC) (Fig. 30a). The TEC can give a quick and unified measurement of the as-prepared catalysts, but it cannot reflect the precise working conditions in the MEA system (e.g., interfacial structure, reactant concentration, local and bulk pH, etc.).460,461 What is worse is that some electrocatalysts reported with superior performance in TEC do not necessarily deliver equivalent performance when assessed in MEA-based cells,462 further implying that a realistic measurement method to simulate the true working environment is a great necessity. In view of this, Kim et al. employed a microelectrode (ME) set-up to study the HOR and ORR reaction kinetics at the Pt/ionomer interface, and found that cell preconditioning benefits the HOR reaction kinetics of Pt in tetramethyl guanidinium (TMG) and benzyl trimethyl ammonium hydroxide (BTMAOH).463 Weber et al. proposed the pros and cons when using ME to investigate the solid-state electrolyte.464,465 Although a ME has competitive advantages when studying lots of variables (e.g., humidity, temperature, gas flow, etc.) in solid electrolyte membranes than TEC, it is rather complicated to screen cell components than MEA does. For the MEA system, the electrocatalyst and membrane have both achieved outstanding progress, and the current challenge is how to translate the developed high-performance catalysts from liquid electrolytes to the membrane-electrode assembly (MEA).81,466 Therefore, the catalyst evaluation method from TEC to MEA and to a practical water electrolysis cell/H2–O2 fuel cell can be a necessary transition, which could boost the optimization of a range of cell components and operational conditions.453 On this basis, the electrochemical evaluation based on a single cell and the testing advantages as well as the protocols are illustrated in Fig. 30b–d. Fig. 30b shows a MEA-based water electrolysis cell with a working electrode area of 1 cm2. Therefore, the as-prepared catalyst, membrane, ionomer, etc. can be evaluated under these MEA conditions. Currently, this type of single cell can be built at a laboratory and/or directly purchased.467 The electrocatalysts (e.g., 20% Pt/C, IrO2, etc.) as well as membranes (e.g., FAA-3-10 and X37-5) with reproducibility and reliability are commercially available.103 These premises make the establishment of a unified testing protocol for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells a high possibility.441,467 For instance, it is suggested that, for each test, it is better to select the other cell components except for the object of research (catalyst or membrane) from commercial benchmarks, in which a relatively reliable and reproducible polarization curve of a specific research object can be obtained for accurate evaluation. In Fig. 30c, the use of the MEA mimics the practical working environment (water electrolyzer stacks) for every component, which establishes a reliable and unified method to screen cell components and working parameters.462,468
Fig. 30 Translation of electrochemical evaluation methodology. (a) Three-electrode configuration (TEC), (b) Components and structure of a single cell with working electrode 1 cm2 in size. (c) Advantages and necessity of a single cell configuration evaluation in comparison with TEC. (d) Proposed single cell test protocol.

Based on the analysis of the literature, the testing protocol for an MEA-based single cell is illustrated in Fig. 30d.

4.6.2. State-of-the-art AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells have gained solid progress very recently, owing to the technological advances on electrocatalysts, membranes, ionomers, etc. On the other hand, the efforts toward the optimization of operational conditions and the cell configuration also contribute significantly to the high performance of AEM devices. Herein, AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells with state-of-the-art performances and a variety of cell tunings from the aspects of the operational conditions and engineering setup are presented. First, some typical durability curves of AEMWEs and their corresponding testing protocols are illustrated in Fig. 31a and b. For an AEMWE tested under a constant current density, the polarization voltage curve usually expresses the following different behaviors: type 1 to type 4, which correspond to a gradual increase in voltage, gradual decrease in voltage, stepwise increase in voltage, and spike type of voltage. The gradual increase in voltage is the most common type of degradation that appears in an AEMWE, which might be caused by the increase in cell resistance arising from the loss of cationic functional groups of the AEM. The opposite type 2 degradation is speculated to be due to AEM thinning, leading to hydrogen crossover, although the voltage profile does not reflect the true degradation process. The stepwise increase in voltage is associated with the loss of fast catalyst activity or a local pH change that result in rapid performance decay. Lastly, spike type 4 represents a recoverable degradation within a certain amount of working time, which is thought to be due to changes in the liquid electrolyte concentration or water transport issues. For the detailed durability curves and the possible assumptions, readers are suggested to refer Li's work.469 Compared with the intensive studies on the degradation of PEM devices and the durability tests,470,471 investigations of the AEM water electrolyzers are still in an early stage. As shown in Fig. 31b, Lim's group studied the effects of two different durability testing protocols (short periodic and long periodic modes) on the stability of AEM water electrolyzers.472 For protocol A, the cell is subjected to a bias of 1.95 V for 2–10 hours and rested for 12 hours at open circuit without solution feeding. As a comparison, the 1.95 V voltage bias was prolonged to ∼100 h after every 12 hour rest for protocol B. The AEM water electrolysis cells are operated under the two protocols for 1000 h; meanwhile, the impedance spectra of the cell at a bias of 1.8 V are obtained. Based on the analysis of the ohmic and non-ohmic resistances of the cell, it was found that the frequently rest processes led to an increase in the ohmic resistance. Furthermore, more rest times also led to a lower humidity level inside the cell and degraded the membrane. These findings might provide useful tips on the operation of practical AEM water electrolyzers, in that it is a necessity to feed the electrolyzer to protect the membrane from deterioration, even under regular rest. Sung and co-authors carefully investigated the possible factors influencing the performance of AEM water electrolyzers (Fig. 31c and d).473 The authors found that the CCM-based cells delivered a higher current density than the CCS-based cells with and without hot-pressing. In addition, the resistance analysis made it clear that the CCM-based cell showed minimum ohmic, charge transfer, and mass transfer resistance. In contrast, the CCS-based cell with hot-pressing showed similar ohmic resistance (∼0.2 Ω) but with higher charge transfer resistance, indicating that the CCM fabrication method could promote the electrochemical reaction kinetics. Furthermore, the CCS-based cell without hot pressing exhibited a much higher ohmic resistance (∼0.7 Ω) and rather higher charge transfer as well as mass transfer resistance. The evidence confirms that CCM is more suitable for use in the fabrication of MEAs. As shown in Fig. 31d, the cell fed with the 1 M KOH electrolyte at both the anode and cathode showed the best performance, while the cell fed with the 1 M KOH electrolyte at the anode and pure water at the cathode delivered much reduced current density at the same overpotential. The use of pure water as a reactant at the anode and at both electrodes led to unstable cell performance, manifesting that the supply of KOH at the anode and cathode results in improved performance. Following this concept, a range of careful tunings on the catalysts, membranes, MEA fabrication method, operation conditions, etc. can be accessed from lots of interesting works.476–479 In addition to the operation conditions and electrocatalyst, the durability of AEM water electrolyzers and the test protocol warrant further investigations.472,480–482Fig. 31e and f give two typical examples showing that record-high current density and durability have been achieved for AEM water electrolysis cells.474,475 In light of the consensus that the critical challenge for membranes is durability and mechanical stability, Meroueh selected Sustainion® X37-50 as the membrane and experimentally reinforced the membrane by the sol–gel method to validate its long-term durability.474 The cell using PTFE reinforced Sustainion® (Grade-T) membrane exhibited a record-high durability (Fig. 31e) with only ∼8 mV degradation within 12000 h of operation. The exceptional durability this reinforced membrane achieved demonstrates that an industrial-level lifespan for an AEMWE can be achieved through a rational membrane reinforcement strategy. According to the different requirements on ionomers in anodes/cathodes, Lee and co-authors employed poly(fluorenyl-co-biphenyl piperidinium) (PFBP) with a high IEC (3.43 mmol g−1) and high water uptake (∼350%) as cathode ionomer and adopted poly(fluorenyl-co-terphenyl piperidinium) (PFTP) bearing a high IEC (2.80 mmol g−1) and low water uptake (∼80%) as the anode ionomer, in which the water molecules from the AEM can be efficiently captured at the cathode.475 In addition, poly(fluorenyl-co-terphenyl piperidinium) (PFTP) was selected as the anion-exchange membrane.475 In particular, ionomers and membranes with a range of fluorenyl ratios were tested in the AEMWEs, from which the ideal fluorenyl ratios for electrode ionomer and membrane were acquired. As shown in Fig. 31f, a record-high current density of 7.68 A cm−2 at 2.0 V for an AEMWE was achieved by feeding 1 M KOH as the electrolyte, surpassing that of the state-of-the-art PEMWE (6 A cm−2 at 2.0 V). The current density of AEMWEs with the use of PGM-free electrocatalysts still reached a high level (1.62 A cm−2 at 2.0 V), suggesting the strong competitiveness and practicability of AEMWEs compared to the PEMWE.
Fig. 31 (a) Typical voltage degradation curves as a function of operation time for AEMWEs. (b) The proposed long-term durability test protocols for AEMWEs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 472. Copyright 2021 Elsevier. (c) AEMWE performance comparison as a function of the MEA fabrication method (CCM vs. CCS) and the resistance analysis. (d) AEMWE performance comparison as a function of the working electrolyte and the feeding flow rate. Reproduced with permission from ref. 473. Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (e) Record-high long-term durability of AEMWEs with a range of AEMs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 474. Copyright 2021 Elsevier. (f) Polarization curves of AEMWEs with specific cell configurations to achieve record-high current density, and the comparison of current density and durability between PFAP membrane and those reported membranes. Reproduced with permission from ref. 475. Copyright 2021 RSC Publishing.

State-of-the-art performances of AEMFCs with the employment of typical anode/cathode catalysts are depicted in Fig. 32. For AEMFCs, long-term cell durability (>1000 h) has rarely been reported; hence, understanding the cell degradation mechanism and whether or not the performance losses can be recovered by cell optimization is rather significant. To this end, Mustain et al. continued their previous study and investigated the cell durability mechanism and performance loss reversibility.196,483 As claimed in the previous work, the authors employed benchmark electrocatalysts (PtRu/C for anode and Pt/C for cathode), ionomers, and GDEs, and achieved a stable operation of 2000 h.196 Within this period of 2000 h, the cell average degradation rate nearly maintains at 15 μV h−1 (Fig. 32a). However, an apparent rise in high-frequency resistance (HFR) from 6 to 9 mΩ was observed in the time frame of 1050 h to 2000 h (∼3 μΩ h−1). The rising rate of HFR can be translated to a voltage degradation rate of 9.5 μV h−1, implying that the increased HFR took a majority of the voltage loss within this period. Between 2000 and 2800 h, the voltage degradation rate sharply increased to 118 μV h−1 accompanied by the rise of HFR (4.6 μΩ h−1). Notably, the HFR-induced voltage loss simply accounts for 13.8 μV h−1, suggesting that many other factors are involved in the degradation of cell performance. The authors hence ascribed the performance losses to two types of degradation. The first one is reversible degradation that is very likely induced by the cathode dry-off and water imbalance. This means that the reversible degradation can be recovered by cell optimization such as changing the electrode Dew point. Another part is an irreversible loss that is mainly caused by cell component degradation (e.g., catalysts, ionomer, membrane, etc.). To verify this assumption, the authors increased the Dew point of the cathode to improve the water conditions. At around 2630 h, the Dew point at the anode and cathode both increased obviously, the cell voltage slightly recovered, and the degradation rate reduced (Fig. 32a and b). The authors concluded that nearly half of the lost cell performance can be recovered in the period from 2000 to 2600 h by resolving the water balance issue. The cell with the optimized cell parameters also exhibited better PPD than those without operation optimization (Fig. 32c). This work demonstrates that attaining a longer and better cell durability is very promising by frequently cell optimization, which also opens an avenue to develop durable and robust AEM fuel cells in the future. Fig. 32d–i give the polarization curves and PPD for several AEM fuel cells with the employment of anode, cathode, and membrane. In Fig. 32d–f, three representative cathode electrocatalysts, i.e., Fe–N–C, Mn–Co spinel oxides, and Co@C/C, have been assembled with benchmark anode catalyst (PtRu/C) to test the cell performance.43,107,484 The AEM fuel cell with the use of Fe–N–C as the cathode electrocatalyst exhibited an excellent PPD of 1.44 W cm−2 and a remarkable mass transport limited current density of 4.3 A cm−2. To the best of our knowledge, this is the highest PPD of the reported AEM fuel cells with the employment of a non-precious metal cathode. Zhuang and co-authors prepared [Mn0.3Co0.7][Mn0.6Co0.4]2O4 (MCO) as the cathode and the assembled AEM fuel cell delivered a PPD of 1.1 W cm−2 and a higher mass transport limited current density of 3.5 A cm−2.484 In Fig. 32f, Chen focused on the development of poly(fluorene-co-biphenyl N,N′-dimethylpiperidinium) (PFBP) as ionomer and poly(fluorene-co-terphenyl N,N′-dimethylpiperidinium) (PFTP) as the membrane. The PFTP-based fuel cell with the use of cobalt catalyst (0.6 mg cm−2 Co@C/C, 40 wt% Co) delivered a PPD of 0.891 W cm−2.107 On the other hand, some typical precious metal-free anodes and the fuel cell performances are presented in Fig. 32g–i. Atanassov et al. synthesized Ni95Cu5 nanoalloy that immobilized on Ketjenblack as the anode material for an AEMFC.485 With the use of Pd/XC-72 as the cathode, the AEM fuel cell exhibited a PPD of ∼0.35 W cm−2 at 80 °C. Zhuang prepared Ni-based nanoparticles enwrapped by a 2 nm nitrogen-doped carbon shell (Ni@CNx) as the anode and employed Pt/C as the cathode (Fig. 32h). Specifically, the assembled AEM fuel cell exhibited a PPD of 0.48 W cm−2 at 80 °C. Meanwhile, the fuel cell presented quite stable durability for 20 h at a current density of 600 mA cm−2. Notably, changing the cathode catalyst from commercial Pt/C to [Mn0.3Co0.7][Mn0.6Co0.4]2O4 (MCO), the AEM fuel cell achieved a record high PPD of 0.21 W cm−2 (Fig. 32i). One should note, these works have slightly different catalyst loading amounts although most the electrode catalysts have selected benchmark materials, manifesting a unified comparison with identical cell components and working conditions is rather important and critical. The recent AEM water electrolysis and fuel cell performances are displayed in Tables 4 and 5.

5. Working management for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

5.1. Feeding electrolyte

Three different types of commonly employed feeding electrolytes for AEMWEs and their polarization curves have been briefly introduced in Fig. 5e. Simply speaking, the use of different types of electrolytes and electrolyte pre-feeding methods need careful tuning to achieve an optimal balance between cell performance and long-term durability.70,72,73,492–495 First, Park's group confirmed that adequate membrane wetness to ensure sufficient OH− doping in the membrane is required to decrease the membrane ohmic resistance and complete the membrane activation, which is significant for the initial operation of AEMWEs.492,493 Hence, they suggested that pre-feeding 0.5 M aqueous KOH into an AEM water electrolysis cell could maximize the cell performance. Razmjooei et al. found that an improved cell performance is achieved by changing the electrolyte solution from 0.1 M KOH to 0.3 M, although this performance increase is rather limited when the electrolyte solution further changes from 0.3 M KOH to 1.0 M KOH.496 This is because a minor increase in electrolyte concentration contributes to accelerated HER and OER kinetics, reduced ohmic resistance of the membrane, and fast ion mobility. Further increasing the electrolyte concentration (above 0.3 M KOH) leads to increased solution viscosity and blockage of catalytic active sites due to the formation of gas bubbles on the surface. As shown in Fig. 33a, Mustain and co-authors studied the cell performance and durability through tuning a series of KOH solutions with different concentrations. They found that diluted KOH solution benefits cell performance but very likely leads to reduced long-term durability compared with the use of DI water.495 The exceptionally low cell degradation for over 500 h (93.5 μV h−1) when feeding with DI water vs. higher cell performance when feeding with diluted KOH solutions (Fig. 33a) manifests that the delicate balance between durability and cell performance needs careful studies. On the other hand, Weber and co-authors elucidated the fundamental role of metal alkali solution in AEMWEs through experiments and mathematical models.494 As shown in Fig. 33b, the voltage loss of the AEM water electrolysis cells with three different electrolytes can be grouped into anode kinetics-intrinsic, anode kinetics-pH, anode kinetics-bubble, cathode kinetics, high-frequency resistance (HFR), catalyst layer ohmic resistance, and ion-exchange resistance by using a mathematic model. For the cell fed with DI water, the most dominant factors contributing to the voltage loss are anode kinetics-intrinsic, catalyst layer ohmic resistance, anode kinetics-pH, and HFR resistance. In terms of the cell using a 0.01 M KOH solution, these four factors still dominate the voltage loss but all with reduced proportions. By contrast, for the cell fed with 1 M KOH solution, anode kinetics-intrinsic was also regarded the most influential factor of the voltage loss while the others took up minor shares. This is a pioneering study where a mathematic model analysis method was employed to quantitatively compare all possible factors involved in cell voltage loss. Therefore, a more precise improvement strategy can be proposed to address the challenges such as increasing the intrinsic activity of the anode catalyst, facilitating membrane and ionomer transportation, and reducing the HFR. Besides, Weber's group also proposed to thoroughly investigate the effects of the ion type (cationic and anion), the cation/OH− ratio, and the presence of carbonates on the cell voltage and durability.497,498 As depicted in Fig. 33c–e, three important aspects, i.e., cationic ion type, anion ion type, and different cation/OH− ratios in determining the cell voltage and durability are presented. The employment of 1 M KOH, 1 M NaOH, and 1 M LiOH as electrolytes aimed to validate whether the AEM water electrolysis cell performance followed the trend of KOH > NaOH > LiOH as observed from the RDE configuration (half-cell). In this work, the hydration-free enthalpy and exchange time of a water molecule in a hydration shell for cations were determined, where Li+ appears to have a higher hydration-free enthalpy and water exchange time than those of Na+ and K+. Consequently, the author speculated that LiOH is very likely to precipitate close to the anode layer of the membrane and hence block the water supply. This is why the polarization curves (kinetics region (0 to 50 mA cm−2)) of the AEM electrolysis cells fed with 1 M KOH deliver improved voltage profiles compared to cells fed with 1 M LiOH, 1.45 M LiOH (equivalent conductivity with 1 M KOH), and 1.23 M NaOH (equivalent conductivity with 1 M KOH). For current density above 2000 mA cm−2, the precipitate further inhibits the catalytic active sites and causes an increased voltage. In terms of the better performance for the cell fed with LiOH in comparison to KOH in the ohmic region (250 to 1600 mA cm−2), it is assumed that the higher defect density and ionic mobility for the Li+-interfacial double layer (IDL) accelerate the mobility of OH− and result in lower overpotential. Following a similar mechanism, the cationic effect on the cell performance is concluded to exist in the order K+ > Na+ > Li+, in which an identical trend was observed in the RDE configuration. In addition, the authors employed electrolytes with identical pH values and conductivities (0.5 M KOH (13.65, 189 mS cm−1), 0.82 M K2CO3 (11.35, 189 mS cm−1), and 18 mM KOH (11.22, 7.4 mS cm−1)) to critically compare the cell performances (Fig. 33d). Notably, the cell voltage with the three electrolytes delivered the trend of 0.5 M KOH > 18 mM KOH > 0.82 M K2CO3 in the kinetics current region, while the voltage trend changed to 0.5 M KOH > 0.82 M K2CO3 > 18 mM KOH in the current density range of 700 to 2000 mA cm−2, indicating that the cell performance is not solely related to the conductivity or electrolyte pH. The authors ascribe these findings to the cation/OH− ratio, which is further discussed in Fig. 33e. In general, the increased cation/OH− ratio leads to increased overpotential. The weaker performance of the 0.82 M K2CO3-fed cell than the 18 mM KOH-fed cell was due to the higher cation/OH− ratio. These mentioned works give a detailed elucidation of the fundamental insights on the effects of using different types of electrolytes, electrolyte concentrations, ion species, etc. on the cell performance. Care needs to be taken to attain a balance between cell performance and durability, for which systematic work on the AEMWE cells to find the optimal feeding electrolyte and compatible cell components (e.g., non-noble-metal-based electrocatalyst, ionomer, GDL, etc.) is further required.
Fig. 33 (a) Voltage degradation profiles for AEMWEs operating under three different electrolyte feeding conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 495 Copyright 2022 IOP Publishing. (b) Voltage breakdown analysis for AEMWE cells feeding with three respective electrolytes: DI water, 0.01 M KOH, and 1 M KOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 494. Copyright 2021 IOP Publishing. Polarization curves of AEMWEs influenced by the types of (c) cations, (d) anions, and (e) cation/OH ratios. Reproduced with permission from ref. 497. Copyright 2021 IOP Publishing.

5.2. Water and gas management

As shown in Fig. 34a, water is solely generated in the cathode area (occurs via the ORR reaction) for PEM fuel cells. For AEM fuel cells, water serves as both the reactant and the product at the cathode and anode, respectively, implying that water shuttling between electrodes in AEMFCs is rather complicated than for their PEM counterparts.499,500 For every 4 moles of electrons that pass through the circuit, two moles of water molecules are consumed at the cathode, while 4 moles of water molecules are generated. In addition, accompanied by the movement of hydroxide ions from the cathode to the anode, a certain amount of water molecules is dragged by the OH− and transported through the membrane to the anode, which is also referred to as the electroosmotic drag. On the other hand, back diffusion, in which water molecules come across the membrane driven by the water concentration gradient between the electrodes also occurs. Importantly, Fig. 34b illustrates the significance of water balance within the AEM fuel cells from two critical aspects: the membrane and the catalyst layer. Too high a water content usually causes water flooding, and conversely, too low a water content leads to dry-off, both exerting badly hazardous effects on the cell components. Taking the membrane as a typical example, a higher amount of water usually increases the water uptake of the membrane, which would reduce its mechanical stability and durability. Too low a water content makes the membrane brittle and deactivates the ion transportation within the membrane. In terms of the catalyst, water flooding very likely blocks the catalytic active sites and is the main reason for the loss of catalytic activity. Furthermore, water flooding also causes improper transport of gas/water/heat within the MEA, which would lead to fast degradation and cell failure. The effects of imbalance of water management on the cell components would dictate the cell performance and durability. Consequently, suggestions from the viewpoint of cell components and operation conditions on how to make rational modulations to further improve water management in AEMFCs have been presented (Fig. 34b). As shown in Fig. 34c, Mustain's group has coupled neutron imaging and operando electrochemical measurements to give a clear summary of the water distribution within the cell components.501 It was found that with increasing operation temperature, a higher amount of liquid water accumulates in both the anode catalyst layer and the GDL, while almost no water content variation can be observed at the cathode CL and GDL. Furthermore, a linear increase in the thickness of the liquid water at the anodic GDL is observed as the working temperature changes from the dew point to the optimum +2 °C (Fig. 34d). Notably, an increase in liquid water content is accompanied by a reduction in the cell voltage, certifying that an increase in relative humidity (RH) contributes to increased cell voltage. It has to be noted that the authors employed two membranes (radiation-grafted ETFE-BTMA vs. perfluorinated AEM) with different water uptake capabilities to validate this concept. Without any doubt, both membrane-based cells exhibited almost an identical water accumulation trend in the anodic catalyst layer and GDL, further indicating the universality of this finding. Based on these findings, the authors proposed to improve the water capacity of the anode to optimize cell performance through manipulation of the anode catalyst content ratio between the ionomer, carbon, and Pt. As a result, with an increased carbon ratio in the anode catalyst layer and in the overall thickness, the capability of the anode to withstand water improved significantly, which can be evidenced by the greatly enhanced mass transport limiting current (5 A cm−2) and peak current density (1.9 W cm−2). Following this concept, Mustain further employed operando neutron imaging and X-ray computed tomography (CT) to illustrate the liquid water distribution under various operating conditions.502 For an AEM fuel cell operated at full humidity, the neutron image manifests that a large number of water molecules have accumulated in the membrane and electrode. In contrast, the accumulation of water content is significantly alleviated with a decrease in the dew point of reactant gases. In particular, the cell water distribution dependence on the operating current density and dew point is further illustrated in Fig. 34e. The AEM cell with a higher working current density is found to have a higher amount of liquid water in every part of the cell (anode/cathode GDL, anode/cathode CL, and membrane) compared with the cell operated at a low current density. This proves that, at a higher current density, water is very likely to be dragged from the anode to the cathode via back diffusion, indicating that the current density serves as a main driver for cell water accumulation. Based on this observation, the authors proposed to redesign the electrode with the employment of an optimal amount of hydrophobic PTFE to balance the water production rate while maintaining high RH of the gases. These works contributed by Mustain's group give us fundamental insight into the importance of the water balance in AEM fuel cells and how the cell architecture should be rationally designed to properly cope with this challenge. Apart from above-discussed works, many studies from the aspects of the membrane, ionomer, GDL, catalyst, etc. on making careful modulation of the water content in AEMFCs have been presented.503–508 The electrode parameters (e.g., GDL, ionomer content, catalyst loading amount, and the ratio of active material to carbon) and cell operation conditions (e.g., flow rate and gas dew point) are found to heavily affect the membrane hydration and electrode flooding/drying out, for which a delicate balance between all the parameters is needed to develop robust AEMFCs.499,504,507
Fig. 34 (a) Comparison of the water distribution in PEMFCs and in AEMFCs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 499. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (b) The common problems of water management in AEMFCs from the viewpoint of membranes and catalysts, and the tuning strategies for water management from the standpoint of cell components and operating conditions. (c) Operando in-plane neutron radiographic images of water in an ETFE-BTMA AEM-based AEMFC (anode/cathode: 50 °C/50 °C) vs. perfluorinated AEM (PFAEM)-based AEMFC (anode/cathode: 54 °C/51 °C). (d) Water distribution plots from the data in (c). Reproduced with permission from ref. 501. Copyright 2018 RSC Publishing. (e) Operando in-plane high-resolution neutron radiographic images of water distribution in ETFE-BTMA-based AEMFCs operated under four conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 502. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature.

As shown in Fig. 35a, Jang et al. employed the solvent-assisted molding (SAM) technique to functionalize membranes with dual-sided patterns to improve the capability in water management.507 Here, a commercial membrane FAA-3–50 (FuMA-Tech) was selected, in which a dual patterned hydrocarbon-based AEM was fabricated. As depicted in Fig. 35b, the modified membrane exhibits enhanced ionic conductivity and improved mass transfer of O2 and H2 gas, which contribute to higher AEMFC performance. In addition to the membrane, Yoo and co-workers proposed to manipulate water management from the standpoint of the catalyst material by improving its capability in water transfer via incorporating a thermo-responsive polymer.505 Specifically, poly-(N-isopropylacrylamide) (PNIPAM), showing the characteristic of hydrophobicity at the operating temperature, was employed to functionalize the electrocatalyst to enhance water management. The normal carbon black was modified with PNIPAM (with the product denoted as CTRP), and was then used as a support to load Pt to obtain Pt/CTRP. In Fig. 35c, the water distribution within the anode and cathode for four different catalysts (Pt/C, Pt/CTRP, Pt/C + C, and Pt/CTRP + C) is presented, where a balanced water content at the anode and cathode appears for the Pt/CTRP + C electrode, implying that PNIPAM does benefit the water back diffusion from the anode to the cathode. In addition, the enhanced hydrophobicity arising from the employment of PNIPAM is reflected by the increased water contact angle, as described in Fig. 35c. Consequently, Pt/CTRP could efficiently drive the water to move from the anode to the cathode, which cannot be achieved for a normal Pt/C electrode (Fig. 35d), even with a supply of humidified air at the cathode. The AEM fuel cells with the use of the Pt/CTRP + C electrode also exhibited higher PPD and max powder density (Fig. 35e and f). This work proposes a new avenue to manipulate water balance from the aspect of the catalyst, which is interesting and needs to be further studied. On account of the relative maturity of PEM technology over its AEM counterparts, hydrogen crossover has been mostly reported in PEM fuel cells and water electrolyzers.509–511 It usually refers to the permeation of two gases (H2 and O2 for H2–O2 fuel cells and/or water electrolyzers) across the polymer membrane during operation, in which the phenomenon is exacerbated with an increasing operating pressure.512,513 The concern of hydrogen crossover also appears for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, although the H2 pressure for the state-of-the-art AEMWEs (∼30 bar) may not be larger than for PEMWEs (30 ∼ 80 bar).33,36 This is because the H2 crossover rate is heavily dependent on a variety of factors, including membrane thickness, membrane permeability, current density, working temperature, hydrogen supersaturation, etc. Meanwhile, the distinct features of the AEM in comparison with the PEM (e.g., thickness and permeability) and different operation parameters dictate that H2 crossover in AEM devices might not follow the identical pathways as that of their PEM counterparts. For water electrolyzers, the gas crossover would decrease the purity of H2, pose safety issues, and cause a decrease in cell efficiency.511 In fuel cells, the H2 crossover also raises hazardous safety problems and generates additional heat, peroxides, and water molecules, eventually leading to reduced cell efficiency, accelerated thermal and chemical degradation, and reduced overall performance.509,510 The intrinsic factors that cause the H2 crossover of AEM devices are presented in Fig. 36a. First, the porous feature of the ion-conducting membrane implies that gas shuttling across the membrane is unavoidable. The H2 permeation caused by this factor takes up only a tiny minority. Second, the post-engineering processes including the fabrication of the CCM and MEA, the cell stacks installation, etc. usually incorporate a certain number of cracks and flaws into the membrane, which makes the hydrogen crossover easier. Third, the membrane degradation during operation makes it thinner and induces pinhole formation in the membrane, which intensifies the H2 crossover significantly. The three factors influencing H2 crossover in PEM devices have been widely documented, and the latter two are found to play a dominant role. For AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, the above-mentioned reasons are thought to account for the H2 crossover, and an in-depth understanding of each category needs to be further provided. The H2 crossover, in turn, causes degradation of the catalyst, the membrane, and the whole cell (Fig. 36b). The gases that have crossed will combust and release an amount of heat that will induce local hot spots in the membrane and membrane thermal degradation. Meanwhile, the radicals associated with gas crossover usually attack carbon or nitrogen branch points of the membrane and hence intensify the chemical degradation. In the case of the catalyst, the generated heat and radicals also cause the sintering and agglomeration of catalyst particles as well as loss of catalytic activity. For a detailed discussion of the origin of gas crossover, the consequences, and corresponding mitigation strategies, the reader is encouraged to refer to Ming's work.514 Based on the analysis of the factors determining hydrogen crossover in AEMs, it is reasonable to conclude that there are two aspects to mitigating the crossover. Increasing the membrane thickness, reducing the defects during the fabrication processes, and alleviating the membrane degradation during practical operation are widely employed. Guiver and co-authors developed microporous quaternized ammonium modified polymers of intrinsic microporosity (PIM) (QPIM-1) AEM bearing high hydroxide conductivity, and studied how the hydration state affects the crossover.515,516 As shown in Fig. 36c, QPIM-1 membranes with three respective thicknesses (34, 53, and 74 μm) were employed to fabricate MEAs, and the hydrogen crossover current densities at 50 °C at various relative humidity (RH) values were evaluated. There was an obvious rapid increase in the current density of H2 crossover up to 0.15 V, and a following gradual increase was observed for three QPIM-1 membranes. For the three membranes, decreasing the RH from 100% to 60% resulted in a negligible change in the H2 crossover current density, but a further decrease to 40% led to a rapid increase in the H2 crossover current density, indicating that the 60% hydration state might be a threshold for water to block the microporous structures in the QPIM-1 membrane. The water sorption analysis illustrates that the microporous structure can be occupied by water molecules; hence, changing the hydration status might be an effective approach to prohibit H2 crossover in AEMFCs (Fig. 36d).


Fig. 35 (a) The fabrication process for a dual-sided patterned membrane by the solvent-assisted molding (SAM) technique. (b) Illustration of the promotion effects in water management of the membrane. Reproduced with permission from ref. 507. Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (c) Change in the water content of the cell electrode, based on the improvement in water management using Pt/C, Pt/CTRP, Pt/C + C, and Pt/CTRP + C electrodes. (d) Water contact angle results for Pt/C and Pt/CTRP electrodes at 60 °C. (e) Cell polarization curves of MEAs with Pt/C, Pt/CTRP, Pt/C + C, and Pt/CTRP + C as anodes for AEMFCs. (c) LSV curves of QPIM-1 AEMs measured at 100% RH and 50 °C. (d) Schematic illustration of hydrogen crossover dependence on the humidity of hydrogen.
