5.3. Thermal management

For water electrolyzers and fuel cells, the additional heat mainly comes from two aspects. For one, ohmic heating mainly comes from the key components arising from the current passage through. For another, electrochemical heat (entropic heat) mainly derives from partial electrochemical energy conversion. The accumulated heat will cause a temperature gradient in the cell components and consequently, lead to fast cell degradation and failure.78,517 Therefore, a variety of cooling techniques including coolant fluid, cooling channels, heat pipes, and the employment of BPs with ultrahigh thermal conductivity are employed to mitigate the overheating problems. Due to the relatively fewer studies of heating management for AEM devices, readers are suggested to read some excellent reviews on thermal management for electrochemical devices, where several effective strategies that have been demonstrated for PEM counterparts might provide novel ideas for AEM devices.78,518,519 Therefore, the fast development of AEM technologies also urges the research community that thermal management studies in the field of AEMWEs and AEMFCs need to be focused on to help address practical technical problems.

6. Operation monitoring for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

With the fast development of key components and operational management for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, advanced techniques used to monitor the dynamic operation of cell devices and the key components are in high demand. Herein, a series of real-time observation technologies, including electron microscopy, spectroscopy, and X-ray spectroscopy techniques will be discussed. X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), particularly synchrotron XAS, has played a significant role in the electrocatalyst community owing to its sensitivity and strength in deciphering atomic configurations and elemental coordination environments. The fundamentals and recent progress on XAS in electrocatalysis have been extensively documented, and readers are suggested to refer to the works reported by Duan, Feng, and Li.254,310,520 Apart from the electrocatalyst, challenges arising from many other key components and related operational problems remain to receive much attention, in which a variety of in situ and/or ex situ techniques will play a decisive role. These include in situ fluorescence spectroscopy,521operando neutron radiography,522–524 nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR),139 infrared spectroscopy,525etc. In this section, the unique techniques with important roles in visualizing the behavior of key components in operation, deciphering membrane/ionomer/MEA degradation pathway, and revealing working principles and management mechanisms will be discussed.

6.1 X-Ray computed tomography

X-Ray computed tomography (CT) is a nondestructive technique used to visualize the microstructure and internal composition of a test sample and present its three-dimensional distribution viewed from many directions.526,527 An illustration of a laboratory X-ray CT is presented in Fig. 37a, where a series of X-ray transmission images of the sample are obtained by the detector. Owing to its prevailing advantages, X-ray CT has been widely applied in water electrolyzer and fuel cell technology, including membranes,528 catalysts,529 GDLs,524 water management, etc. In particular, X-ray CT has also become rather significant in detecting and analyzing surface and interface structures such as triple-phase boundaries, in view of its high sensitivity and spatial resolution. In this section, we will particularly select some interesting works which have used X-ray CT for the characterization of membranes, catalysts, and/or GDLs in AEM water electrolyzers and/or fuel cells in an attempt to promote the fast advancement of operation monitoring techniques in AEM technology. Xu and co-workers targeted the membrane-electrode interfacial compatibility and proposed to fabricate a tightly bounded interface between the AEM and the electrode.491 The VBBQPPO-based MEA was in situ converted into crosslink-immobilized by a gradual thermal treatment under N2 atmosphere. Consequently, a dense 3D-zipped interface layer (3D-ZIL) and a tight chemical contact were formed for the MEA (denoted as ZIL-MEA) compared with normal MEA without thermal treatment. Micro-X-ray tomography was employed to investigate the interface structure of the ZIL-MEA. In Fig. 37b–d, the crevices and cracks can be observed at the interface for normal MEAs, while no cracks can be detected for 3D-ZIL, demonstrating the tight interface connection. In addition, X-ray CT was also used to detect the catalyst layer and examine water diffusion and the catalyst failure mechanism.530 Based on the advantages of employing non-noble-metal-based materials as electrocatalysts for AEMFCs, Zenyuk's group fabricated Fe–N–C as an electrocatalyst and employed ex situ nano-CT and operando micro-CT to visualize water distribution within the catalyst layer. As shown in Fig. 37e–h, a micro-X-ray CT, together with two nano X-ray CTs of the cathode catalyst layer at 50% and 100% RH, respectively, is presented. Apart from the MEA and the catalyst layer, X-ray CT can be also used to justify the water transport conditions by detecting the water conditions in the gas-diffusion layer. To this end, Zenyuk et al. selected four types of commercially available gas diffusion electrodes, namely, the Freudenberg, Toray, SGL 10BA, and SGL 24BA, and employed X-ray CT to study their water transport behavior.531 One should note that previous reports normally interpreted the external contact angles (CAs) of GDLs as their internal wettability due to the lack of an efficient method to accurately test the internal properties. In this work, the authors adopted an algorithm method and computed the local CAs from the pore space images that were captured with the use of X-ray CT.531,532
Fig. 37 (a) Illustration of a laboratory X-ray CT instrument that is composed an of X-ray source, sample stage, and detector. Reproduced with permission from ref. 526. Copyright 2021 Springer Nature. X-ray CT images of (b) ZIL-MEA, (c) M-MEA-B, and (d) M-MEA. Reproduced with permission from ref. 491. Copyright 2021 John Wiley and Sons. (e) Cross-sectional micro-X-ray CT image of a catalyst layer (CL) and (f) the zoomed images at 50% RH and 100% RH. (g–h) Three-dimensional representations of the CL. Reproduced with permission from ref. 530. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (i and j) 3D maps of contact angles (CAs) and local roughness of four different types of GDLs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 531 Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society.

The 3D maps of the CAs and the local roughness of the four GDLs (from left to right SGL 10BA, SGL 24BA, Toray H-060, and Freudenberg H23C2) are shown in Fig. 37i and j. In addition, the probability distributions of the CAs for the four GDLs were simulated, where a mixed wettability can be observed for all GDLs. The mean internal contact angle values for the GDLs were calculated following the sequence: Freudenberg (96°) < Toray (98°) < SGL 10BA (102°) < SGL 24BA (112°). All the contact angle values were further used to calculate the wettability percentage of the GDLs, and it was found that SGL 24BA was the most hydrophobic, while Freudenberg was the most hydrophilic, in accordance with the mean internal CA values. It was concluded that the hydrophilicity is attributable to the surface metal oxide groups, while the hydrophobicity can be correlated with the PTFE content and roughness to trap air. This work also highlights the importance of using X-ray CT in deciphering water transport in GDLs, which will lay a solid foundation for the investigation of other components and operational issues by using X-ray CT.524,533

6.2. In situ spectroscopy and electron microscopy testing

Aside from X-ray CT, in situ spectroscopy and electron microscopy techniques, including Raman spectroscopy, Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM), have been widely reported in articles on membrane technologies and beyond.525 Vibrational spectroscopy techniques such as Raman spectroscopy plays an increasingly important role in characterizing O–H vibration bands and the interfacial water structure at catalyst/electrolyte interfaces, which has provided valuable insight for deciphering ion/species adsorption and the catalyst design rationale.406,534,535 TEM and scanning TEM (STEM) enable the observation of the dependence of sample morphology and atomic structure evolution on the applied potential. The fundamental principles of these techniques have been presented in several excellent review articles;536,537 hence, we focus on the employment of these techniques in the field of AEM technology to summarize how the techniques benefit the development of catalysts, membranes, ionomers, etc. The studies on the employment of various operando spectroscopic techniques in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells have been discussed previously.205,406
Fig. 38 Pathways toward practical deployment of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells from fundamentals to technologies.

7. Conclusions and perspective

Water electrolyzers and H2 fuel cells are regarded as ideal clean energy solutions, which could efficiently utilize renewable resources and secure a sustainable society. To fulfill this vision, developing low-cost high-performance key components, including anion-exchange membranes, ionomers, electrocatalysts, bipolar plates, gas-diffusion layers, etc., determining the optimal operation conditions, including the electrolyte, working temperature, water/heat management, etc., and employing advanced operando techniques (e.g., XAS, X-ray CT, Raman spectroscopy, etc.) to provide feedback for the device design are critical and unavoidable (Fig. 38). The fast advancement of catalysts and anion-exchange membranes, to some degree, have propelled the development of AEM technologies, although state-of-the-art advances in some key aspects, namely, membrane durability, working management, and operation monitoring are far from adequate. A fundamental understanding of the membrane failure mechanism, water transfer dynamics, and catalyst activity descriptors remain to be provided with new insights, not even mentioning more comprehensive and challenging aspects, such as MEA fabrication, water management, etc. Therefore, we present following the perspective on what needs to be further studied from a range of aspects to accelerate AEM technology development and translation into applications:

(1) From the viewpoint of key components, a low-cost high-performance membrane featuring long durability is the key target for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. This is because membranes essentially determine the viability and practicability of these two devices. Critically speaking, current studies on the AEM focus on ionic conductivity and mechanical stability; however, the durability, especially the long-term durability under practical working conditions (10000 h or longer), is rarely reported. It has to be noted that the obtained membrane durability tested under either ex situ conditions or in mere KOH solutions cannot accurately reflect the authentic working environment in AEM water electrolyzers and/or fuel cells. Consequently, membrane electrode assembly (MEA) based cells and/or cell stacks are suggested to be used for the assessment. Furthermore, standard testing protocols (Fig. 30d) are required to give reliable and repeatable evaluation and screening for membrane materials. In addition, the catalyst layers, comprising the electrode electrocatalysts and ionomers, are the second biggest challenge to overcome. This is because the triple-phase boundary is an exceptionally critical environment that requires efficient transport of gas, liquid, and electrons, while it is easily influenced by many conditions, including the water content, temperature, layer thickness, ionomer/catalyst ratio, gas stream flow, etc. Moreover, the slightly different requirements between anode and cathode imply that the catalyst layer design for the anode and the cathode should be different, suggesting a viewpoint from the whole device (water electrolyzer and/or fuel cell) to carefully evaluate and regulate their respective anode and cathode layer parameters is a high necessity. Given that the catalysts and ionomers are used together to fabricate the catalyst layer, a strong binding property of ionomer/catalyst is another important aspect that needs to be further developed to improve the overall durability. Apart from the aforementioned aspects, improvements in the gas diffusion layer and bipolar plate to increase the mass transfer capability, cell efficiency, and cost-effectiveness, are necessary. The use of MEA-based cells to evaluate and screen catalysts and materials is very important, as the requirements on a single component might be incompatible.

(2) From the viewpoint of operational management, optimal operation conditions to attain cell activity and durability need to be comprehensively studied and modeled. In terms of AEM water electrolyzers, the key operational conditions, including temperature, working electrolyte, and feeding flow rate, need to be balanced to achieve long-term operation. The cell performance is easily affected by the parameters of these operational conditions; hence, the operation conditions should also be optimized from a standpoint of a single cell or more cells, which would provide reliable and realistic data for practical operation. In the case of AEM fuel cells, CO2 concentration and water management are two critical parameters that affect the cell performance, in addition to the temperature, gas stream flow rate, etc. Developing membranes with high withstand capability to CO2 is one approach, which means that the use of ammonium-based membranes might not suitable because they tend to be attacked in the presence of CO2. A fundamental understanding of the membrane failure mechanism induced by CO2 is desirable, which would help in designing membranes that are less likely to be affected by CO2. Another plausible solution is gas stream management. A CO2 filter can be considered to mitigate the CO2 pollution problem from its source, even with the sacrifice of cost. Self-purging and developing CO2-resistant membranes and catalysts need to be further studied though they will also bring some other issues.538 Cell stack overheating and gas crossover are problems that commonly appear in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Gas crossover not only reduces the cell efficiency but also increases the safety problems because of the potential for explosion with the increasing concentrations of hydrogen and oxygen. Overheating also causes a decline in cell efficiency and intensifies cell stack degradation, leading to a significantly reduced lifespan. Consequently, cooling fans and water are normally employed to ease the overheating of the cell system. It is reasonable to conclude that the success of low-cost, high-performance cell stacks of AEMWEs and AEMFCs is impossible without careful operational management, mainly including the electrolyte, water, heating, gas, etc.

(3) Third, advanced and operando characterization techniques would provide valuable feedback for the materials design and architecture engineering in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Considering the multiple solvated ions or species involved in the HER/HOR, advanced characterizations, particularly the surface-sensitive spectroscopy methods, are suggested to probe the changes in the surface and interface structure during the electrochemical reactions. Surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy (SERS) has been adopted to validate the role of OHad species in the alkaline HOR/HER.406 Likewise, surface-enhanced infrared absorption spectroscopy (SEIRAS) has been employed to study the adsorption of cations or hydrogen on the Pt surface.223,539,540 Apart from the aforementioned advanced characterizations, the in situ techniques should also be coupled with them to monitor the real-time changes in catalyst surfaces and solid-electrolyte interfaces during the HER/HOR reactions. As for the catalysts themselves, in situ XAS is a representative tool that can provide precise information on the electronic structure and configurations for certain metals in catalysts. These techniques have already been employed to study the coordination evolution of Pt single atoms in the HER.541 Jia and co-workers employed in situ XAS to study the HER/HOR kinetics of Pt after the addition of transition metals.239,542 Following this concept, other in situ techniques such as in situ Raman spectroscopy and Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy might also be helpful to determine the dynamical changes.

(4) Last but not least, theoretical simulations or calculations are highly necessary. DFT-based calculations could provide more theory-led design concepts to guide material design strategies in the future.210 On the other hand, the theoretical calculations are also helpful for understanding the complex reaction mechanisms where multiple species are involved, which would play a unique role in deciphering the membrane failure mechanisms and catalyst dissolution dynamics. We hope that a clear understanding of every key component will lay a very solid foundation for the construction of AEMWEs and AEMFCs. In addition, by coupling the advances on operational management and operation monitoring techniques, low-cost high-performance AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells are expected to show fast progress and deployment in the near future.
